% ---------- 简介（无页码） ----------
\vspace*{4cm}

\begin{center}
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}\\[6pt]
    {\Large\scshape Introduction}\\[6pt]
    \rule[0.5ex]{5cm}{0.8pt}
\end{center}

\vspace{2cm}

{\centering
\color{gray!80!black}
\begin{minipage}{0.8\textwidth}
    \setstretch{1.6}
    \itshape
    \raggedright
    \setlength{\parindent}{1em}   
    Bittersweet yet fresh---science, love, and mystery.

    When you think about it, ``youth'' is a lot like a well-crafted detective novel.

    The mystery of the cherry blossoms that once symbolized a love that never came true. A faint feeling of something off during club recruitment. The story hidden in the age of a great old tree. The hamster that vanished from a locked room. The desperate choices made by high schoolers, dedicated to a biology club on the verge of collapse.

    Each seemingly unrelated cause and effect is actually connected somewhere---and only when we look back do we finally recognize it as ``youth.'' And only then do we realize it: the important thing we missed.

    ``Let's test it\dots scientifically!''

    This is a modest activity log of Takatsunagai High School's ``Science Department,'' as science-loving high schoolers take on the many mysteries they face in everyday life.

    ---and the story of how a first love is unraveled.
\end{minipage}
\par}

% ==============================
%        Prologue
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Prologue\\
    When the Summer of My Senior Year Ends
\end{center}
\label{sec:prologue}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Prologue}
\vspace{1cm}

Youth, I think, is like a well-crafted detective novel.

For those caught in its vortex, they can't even tell what it is they're experiencing.

Only after everything ends---only after it becomes impossible to undo---do you finally see the whole picture.

Looking back while saying things like, Man, we were idiots back then. But it was fun, people can, for the first time, clearly recognize it as youth.

As if a brilliant master detective were right beside you, laying out the deductions in perfect order.

The summer of our third year ended.

For us, it also carried the meaning of a small turning point.

Just as I had finally found some peace and was studying in the library, Iwama Rio gently tapped my shoulder.

``Hey, got a minute?''

The whisper, mindful of everyone around us, seemed to have a different waveform mixed into it than usual. That slightly excited voice I heard when she got data she never expected, or when she found a rare insect. As always, I silently nodded.

We moved to the vacant lot behind the biology lab. The grass rose in a little mound like a small old burial mound---perfect for sitting down and taking a break. The two of us sat side by side in our usual spot on the slope.

``It's nice weather, and\dots I just felt like I wanted some outside air,'' Iwama murmured, as if making an excuse.

As we chatted about nothing in particular, we heard the desperate cry of a tsukutsukuboushi cicada from the back hill. And when the conversation naturally broke off, Iwama pulled a white envelope from her tote.

``Um\dots Del-chan, this\dots''

The voice that was usually so crisp it felt almost springy was wavering today.

I took the envelope. Nothing was written on it. To confirm what it meant, I looked at Iwama.
For some reason, she quickly averted her eyes.

``Sorry for just handing you something like this out of nowhere. But, um\dots in a way, it's like I'm returning the favor\dots''

I flipped it over, thinking I'd check what was inside, but it was firmly sealed with glue. I'll need scissors to open this, I thought---and then Iwama added,

``I wanted to give it to you earlier, but\dots I figured it might not be right while you were working so hard in club. So\dots it might be kind of late, but\dots I wanted to give it to you now, at this moment\dots''

It wasn't very clear. What on earth was inside? It was starting to feel like a ``guess what's in it'' quiz.

Iwama's ears were faintly red. Noticing my gaze, she casually covered them with her hand.

``Um, well, I'm going back!''

Before I could ask whether it was okay to open it here, Iwama left, abandoning me on the spot.

When I returned to the library, Iwama's bag was already gone. Had she gone home?

After carefully cutting the seal with scissors, I took only the envelope and moved to the local history materials corner, where no one was around. Somehow, I felt like it would be better to take out the contents somewhere out of sight.

What came out was a postcard-sized piece of paper, laminated. Inside, a splendid pressed flower in a pale pink hue was sealed against fine white paper. In the quiet, I stared at that fragile color for a while.

That was all that was in the envelope. But from the feel of it, I could tell there was a sticky note on the back. When I turned it over, I found writing in fountain pen on a simple pink note.

\textbf{I tried using a film that reflects ultraviolet light, but I'd be happy if you could store it somewhere that gets as little sunlight as possible, so the pigments don't break down from the light.}

That was all. There wasn't even a signature.
I felt an odd familiarity in the order of priorities---writing not a message, but handling precautions.
That was how we always were.

In the end, I couldn't say for certain what the pressed flower meant.
And still\dots I never thought it would be this flower. A nostalgic memory came back to me.
Gazing at its modest pale pink, I began to recall days that felt as if they were from long ago.

The beginning of a story---days we would someday call youth---


% ==============================
%        Chapter 1
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 1\\
    In the Pale Pink-Cut Domain
\end{center}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 1}
\label{sec:ch1}
\vspace{1cm}

Spring really is a giddy season.

Just when you think fresh, dewy green has suddenly welled up out of the world that stayed dry and brown all winter, flowers start popping open everywhere. It is as if the earth's vitality, long pinned down by frost, senses the north wind easing and bursts out because it can't stand it anymore.

The one that jumps the gun is, of course, the plum.

Even while you're still walking with your neck hunched against the cold, there are times when a wind carrying a dense fragrance blows over you---so rich you'd think someone was hosting a perfume judging contest. The plum blossoms are out. Only after you notice the red and white at the tips of the branches do you finally realize, Ah, it's this season already, and that has become the yearly routine.

Along with the plum fragrance, my nose starts to itch too. Cedar.

After the war, demand for lumber skyrocketed for reconstruction, and under the banner of an expanded afforestation policy, the government went and planted a stupid amount of cedar. We in the modern day are the ones paying for what our predecessors did. Across Japan, cedar male flowers bloom and scatter their yellow pollen like smoke, as if out of spite, attacking people's eyes and noses. While you're sneezing with your nose running, the plum blossoms have already fallen.

Then the headliner arrives.

If we're talking about the ultimate spring feature, it has to be cherry blossoms.

Is there any other plant that makes the entire country of Japan get that excited? The one that starts blooming from the south and gets reported every day even on the weather forecast is a variety developed in the Edo period called Somei-yoshino. Somei-yoshino were propagated from a single specimen through grafting and cuttings---meaning they are all clones, so to speak, duplicates. Because every tree has the same genes, they bloom all at once in the same place.

When those faintly pale-pink flowers burst open, it is immediately, All right, time for cherry-blossom viewing, and the whole thing turns into a festival. People pour into the neighborhood parks, kids cram down sakuramochi, and adults guzzle beer. Japan's many---easily over a hundred---famous cherry-blossom spots overflow with crowds.

In Kanto, you go through all of that, and only then does April finally arrive.

And by then, someone with no stamina like me is completely worn out. I have no strength left to get excited by the warm spring weather. At best, I stare at the brown, soggy cherry petals gathered along the roadside while sniffling and snuffling against the cedar pollen that is still raging just as fiercely as ever.

Even if you are a brand-new first-year high school student, stepping right into the heart of so-called youth and envied for it, that does not change in the slightest.

``Morning, Delta! It's a perfect morning!''

Called out to in an absurdly bright voice, I sighed before turning around.

A familiar guy was grinning and waving a big hand at me.

I lifted my hand slightly in reply, then faced forward again. As I kept walking at a slightly slower pace, he came up alongside me, looking like he was truly enjoying this ``perfect morning.''

Mizasaki Ryuichi. Apparently, every day he sets a new personal record for how fun life is, so he always greets people like this. True to that cheerful inner self, he was a guy with a somewhat flashy, frivolous vibe.

``Hey, it's only the second day of high school, and you already look kind of gloomy.''

``Because I'm walking in the shade.''

The blazing morning sun was blocked by houses if you walked along the east side of the road. It was already warm; if I let sunlight hit my blazer, I'd go past a comfortable temperature and end up hot. Walking in the shade was an extremely rational choice.

``Fair point. But you know, this morning, I've got a problem if my best friend walks in the shade.''

Mizasaki stepped over the white line and shifted out toward the road, darting into the sunlight.

I was about to tell him to get back because it was dangerous---then I realized why he was doing it.

Something about him was different---he'd dyed his hair. A dark brown, or maybe a brownish-black---subtle enough that if he stayed in the shade, you might not even notice the change from his natural hair.

Under my gaze, Mizasaki tilted his head with an affected flourish.

``Well? Notice anything?''

``You're going a little bald.''

``Mm. I'm not going bald. I dyed it. You know---high school debut.''

Unlike the middle school we attended, Tsunanagai High School had no rules about hair color. Even so, dyeing your hair the day after the entrance ceremony took some nerve. Still, the fact that he picked such a restrained color felt very Mizasaki---like he couldn't quite commit to being truly flashy.

``High school debut is admirable, but isn't dyeing your hair a hassle? Black hair grows in at a pace of a centimeter a month.''

``You really don't get it. It's fine if it's a hassle. This is the hummingbird's single drop of water carried to a forest fire. I'm just doing what I can to be popular.''

``Don't use the South American hummingbird parable for something sleazy.''

``Sleazy is a nasty way to put it. A high school boy wanting to be popular is the same as a male peacock spreading beautiful tail feathers. Here---put in Delta terms, it's `the law of nature.' You've got good raw material too, Delta. You should care more about your appearance.''

Calling it the law of nature---he really was insufferably smug.

``You can aim to be a flashy peacock if you want. I'll aim to be a crow.''

``Huh? A crow? Why go out of your way to pick such a plain bird?''

``Because they're smart. Crows are.''

``Ah, I see. So you're going to focus on academics. As expected of the honor student---different as always!''

Mizasaki accepted my rationalization with a pleased look. We'd been friends since elementary school. Most of our ``debates'' were basically little skits, and most of each other's reactions were within expectations.

If Mizasaki dyeing his hair made him a peacock, then I was a pitch-black crow.

I don't really care how other people see me. Mizasaki wants to walk out in the sunny spotlight, but I prefer the quiet shade. Mizasaki uses hair wax, but if my hair is a little messy from sleep, I just wet it and call it good. Mizasaki apparently never skips strength training, but I like my slightly skinny build. Mizasaki keeps telling me I should switch to contacts, but I've stuck with glasses since middle school.

It feels more comfortable to be together when we differ this much.

Maybe even he felt hesitant about walking outside the white line, because Mizasaki came back into the shade.

``\dots By the way, Delta, I've got a really important thing to tell you.''

When this guy says something like that, something stupid usually follows.

``What is it?''

``There was this girl named Iwama, right?''

``Yeah. The one who sits behind me.''

``Hoh. So you noticed her too? So you do remember her.''

Mizasaki grinned at me.

Remembering her or not aside, our attendance numbers were one apart, our seats at the entrance ceremony were next to each other, and in the classroom our seats were directly in front of and behind one another. Whether I liked it or not, her face and name were going to stick.

Iwama Rio. A girl with a neat ponytail who looked exactly like the kind of top student she probably was.

The kind of presence where, just from her face alone---just from a first impression---you could tell she lived in a world different from mine. She was as radiant as a cherry blossom, living up to her name.

``So what about Iwama?''

``Man, she was crazy cute!''

``\dots That's the `really important' thing?''

``Come on, Delta, you thought so too, right? Hey, we were talking. Did you see that smile?''

It was true that, objectively speaking, Iwama's looks were attractive: distinct features, arched eyebrows that gave her an intelligent air, and a smile so bright it was almost blinding.

``She did smile a lot.''

``Right? And it wasn't just her looks---everything about her, the way she carried herself, you could tell she was well raised. And the way she talked---clear and crisp, not a hint of stumbling. She's like an honor student wearing clothes, walking around.''

``If an honor student were walking around without clothes, that would be a problem.''

``Guys who nitpick don't get popular. Anyway, you agree she has that honor-student vibe?''

``Yeah, I figured that was her type.''

She looked so thoroughly like a top student that it would feel like a lie if her grades were bad. She was bound to earn the trust of classmates and teachers alike. She would almost certainly get picked as class representative. She could probably do sports too, and the athletic clubs would be lining up to recruit her.

Because she was a cherry blossom. Iwama Rio---an honor student who carried ``sakura'' in her name.

A popular star everyone paid attention to every spring.

``And, and, Delta---listen. I made a genius discovery.''

Mizasaki gave me a pointed look, like he was about to say something significant.

``A genius discovery?''

``Yeah. I found this miraculous point of commonality between you and Iwama. You wanna know, right?''

I didn't particularly want to know, but Mizasaki clearly wanted to talk. I jerked my chin to urge him on.

``First, remember where your `Delta' nickname came from.''

``My surname is Izuta, for starters.''

Delta was a nickname that came from a certain incident back in middle school. There was no historical reason like my ancestors living in some Delta Triangle State. Mizasaki seemed eager to spread that misreading even in high school, and I really wished he wouldn't.

``Yeah, yeah, details don't matter. Now---Iwama.''

As if aiming for dramatic effect, Mizasaki paused.

``\dots Try reading the kanji in `Iwama' as a jubako reading.''

Iwama---岩, 間---gan, ma---Gamma.\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} In Japanese, 重箱読み (\textit{jukubako-yomi}) refers to a mixed reading where a compound uses different reading types in the same word---typically one kanji read in \textit{on'yomi} (Sino-Japanese) and another read in \textit{kun'yomi} (native Japanese), or vice versa. It's often discussed as a ``mismatched'' reading style, but it's common in real Japanese. Here, Mizasaki jokes that 岩間 can be force-read as ガンマ (Gamma) by splitting it into: \textbullet\ 岩 → ガン (gan, \textit{on'yomi}); \textbullet\ 間 → マ (ma, \textit{kun'yomi}). So ``Iwama'' becomes ``Gamma'' as a playful, contrived pun---fitting the story's Greek-letter motif (Delta $\leftrightarrow$ Gamma).}

``See? Right? Right?''

Mizasaki looked smug, like he'd just come up with a way to solve a math proof in three lines.

``You're Delta, and she's Gamma. α, β, γ, δ\dots\ Not only are your attendance numbers next to each other---your Greek letters are neighbors too. Right? That's amazing, isn't it?''

``That's very amazing.''

``Don't praise it like you're bored. Come on---there's definitely some kind of connection! And hey, if you want to use it as a conversation opener with her, I'm totally fine with that.''

``Why would I need a conversation opener?''

``What do you mean? Obviously it's to get closer to her.''

Looking over, I saw Mizasaki was back to walking out in the sun on the road side, looking thoroughly pleased with himself. His hair---colored like an aqueous solution of iron(III) hexacyanoferrate---suited Mizasaki well in the bright spring light. I didn't know about hummingbirds or peacocks, but he certainly seemed to be enjoying himself, and good for him.

``I'm not getting closer to her. You know what I'm like.''

Mizasaki turned toward me with an unusually cheery grin.

``You never know. Fate has a way of pulling pranks that are so bad they feel like a joke.''

``I'm telling you I have no intention of getting closer. No matter what fate does, that won't change.''

``Man, that's a sad thing to say. She's your classmate, you know? You should get along.''

``You're a different type. No matter how you look at it, she's someone who lives differently than I do.''

``Yeah, but think about it.''

Cutting me off halfway, Mizasaki raised his index finger sharply.

``The conditions are already in place---almost too perfect. Delta and Iwama's seats are in the row closest to the hallway. And Iwama is all the way in the back. Which means, when Iwama is seated, the only people she can talk to are the person to her left or the person in front of her. And who do you think sits in front of Iwama?''

``Who do you think? Me.''

Right after enrollment, seats were assigned by attendance number. Our class had a weird number of people whose names started with ``A,'' like Aizawa and Arasuna, so Iwama and I ended up lined up at the very back of the hallway-side row, with the highest attendance numbers.

``See? And in other classrooms too, because of attendance numbers, Delta and Iwama are probably close to each other.''

Mizasaki pressed in with an aggressive eagerness, like when he tried to force-sell me the mnemonics he made for memorizing the periodic table. It almost made me want to suspect he had some ulterior motive.

``\dots It's kind of sad. Having to be near me all the time.''

``What are you talking about? Being near Delta isn't some uncomfortable place!''

``I appreciate the sentiment, but you're probably the only one who thinks that way, Mizasaki.''

Mizasaki laughed cheerfully.

``Maybe!''

Of course, I wasn't the type to cause trouble for the people around me. But I wasn't outwardly active. I wasn't sociable. I wasn't someone who walked in the sun.

A so-called shadow-dweller---put plainly, an introvert.

If you only had someone to talk to in front of you or to your left, and the person in front of you was just a shadow-dweller who did nothing but pass worksheets back, Iwama would probably end up feeling lonely.

She was obviously the type who mingled with people and walked in the sun---she wasn't someone on this side.

Beyond the ginkgo-lined street that rose into a gentle slope, the school gate of Tsunanagai High School came into view. The old-fashioned gate, built from stones stacked high, projected the dignified presence you'd expect from the top prep school in the prefecture.

The ginkgo trees along the avenue had put out pale yellow-green new buds, and beneath the blue sky their whole canopies gleamed in the morning sun.

It was pleasant today, but the forecast said the weather would break from evening, and a storm would come at night.

At lunch, Mizasaki vanished somewhere, claiming he was ``scouting other classes.'' With no reason to go along, I opened my lunchbox at my desk. A carefully packed lunch I'd made myself---though half of it was mini tomatoes.

By the way, I thought mini tomatoes were one of the end points the history of agriculture had arrived at.

Inside that thin skin was a packed concentration of natural miracles and human ingenuity. The balance of refreshing tartness and sweetness that never got old. The skin repelled water well, so they were easy to wash, and yet they were also easy to eat. Bite-sized, so you didn't need a knife, and you could eat them without getting your hands dirty. If you bought them at a direct-sale stand, the price was reasonable too. And thanks to the development of hydroponics and greenhouse cultivation, you could buy them year-round.

As I bit into one with a tiny pop, I let my thoughts drift to the genetic information woven into its cells. Ancestors that grew wild in the Andes, bearing only tiny fruit---surely sour and astringent in taste. Humanity domesticated them, and over generations, little by little, improved them toward our preferences. The vast history of biological evolution and the long history of human effort were engraved into each and every one of these little spheres. That symphony they played! A plant that had absorbed sunlight used the energy gained through the ingenious system called photosynthesis, mobilizing countless genes to produce a diversity of substances through an almost mind-numbing process---achieving that exquisite balance of taste and aroma on the tongue. There was no way humanity could reproduce this mechanism from scratch. If it wasn't a miracle, what else could you call it?

``Do you like mini tomatoes?''

A voice spoke to me out of nowhere, and startled, I turned to look at the seat behind me.

Iwama Rio was staring at me with her big eyes wide and round. I immediately dropped my gaze.

``Y-Yeah\dots\ I mean.''

Realizing I'd given a stupid answer, I added,

``\dots Was I eating them that deliciously?''

The moment I said it, it occurred to me that from where Iwama sat, she should only have been able to see my back and the back of my head. I'd said something stupid.

``No. It's just---there are so many. I don't think I've ever seen someone with this many mini tomatoes in their lunch.''

With fingers lightly tanned by the sun, Iwama pointed at my desk.

One tier of my stacked lunchbox was entirely mini tomatoes. The other tier, meanwhile, was packed tight with white rice seasoned with ume-shiso furikake and the rest of the side dishes.

``They're good. Mini tomatoes are.''

At my answer---which might not even have been a real reason---Iwama smiled brightly, like a flower blooming.

To borrow Mizasaki's words: ``Did you see that smile?''

``Yeah! Mini tomatoes are so good.''

Just having her repeat the same thing made it feel as if she were affirming my entire existence. It was a terrifying level of people skills.

``But don't you get tired of them if you eat that many? I almost thought you had a fight with your parents.''

Apparently, Iwama had suspected my parents had packed my lunch with mini tomatoes out of spite. But that wasn't it. That wasn't it at all.

``No. I'm just eating this many because I like them. Even this isn't enough.''

I was confident I could talk for a full hour about the symphony of mini tomatoes, but I held back. Instead, I showed Iwama the lunchbox filled with mini tomatoes. I'd already eaten a few, but the rest were packed so tightly that they didn't shift---like a puzzle locked in place.

``Amazing! It's a close-packed structure!''

A phrase I hadn't anticipated flew out of Iwama's mouth, and I was thrown into confusion.

It seemed she picked up on the change in my expression right away, because Iwama hurriedly waved both hands.

``Ah! I said something weird! Sorry---I just meant they're packed efficiently.''

``No, it's not like it was weird or---''

``So that means, Izuta-kun, you make your own lunch?''

Feeling a slight sense of wrongness at the forceful topic change, I decided it was fine and nodded.

``I wouldn't go so far as to say I `make' it.''

I mostly just packed things. Mini tomatoes.

``That's impressive! I---I have m---my mom make mine for me.''

Even though nothing about the conversation had been strange, it was like she was flustered and trying to change the subject anyway.

Iwama's hands moved smoothly as she opened her own lunch and showed it to me. The side dishes were well-balanced, and the white rice was topped with a small umeboshi. The fact that she could so naturally show the contents of her lunchbox to a guy she'd almost never spoken to before made me suspect, unfairly, that it was less a lack of caution and more that she was simply kindhearted---someone who had been raised in a good home.

\fullpageimage{1.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}
\vspace{1cm}
So she was about to say ``Mama'' earlier, was she?

When I looked at the fingertips holding her lunchbox, her nails were neatly trimmed. Did she do martial arts, or maybe swimming?

\dots\ Not good. I ended up observing her. I kept the conversation going in a way that would sound natural.

``It's fine if someone can make it for you. Both my parents work in Tokyo, so they leave early. That's why I get it ready myself.''

``Huh! I see! My mom works from home, so I end up relying on her all the time. But I'm in high school now, so maybe I'll try making it myself next time.''

As she spoke, Iwama took out a pair of lacquered chopsticks.

Works from home---what did that mean? Programmer, designer, writer\dots\ If her house doubled as a shop, there were other possibilities too. But if the home and storefront were connected, you wouldn't usually call it working from home. A private cram school where you let people into your house seemed possible, but\dots

I saw Iwama tilt her head slightly. I looked away and popped a mini tomato into my mouth. I'd taken the stem into my mouth along with it, but I couldn't spit it out, so I swallowed it. The stem's herb-like scent lightly tickled my nasal cavity.

What was I doing? It wasn't like I was interested, and yet I was trying to pry into Iwama's family situation. It was disgusting.

Iwama picked up a small bite of rice with careful chopstick work and put it into her mouth. The way she chewed properly, working through the small mouthful, paired with her ponytail swaying a little, somehow made her resemble a rabbit or some kind of rodent.

The ponytail was tied---yesterday and today alike---with a plain black hair tie, no decoration.

It was probably just my imagination.

``Have you decided on a club yet, Izuta-kun?''

Iwama brought up a new topic herself. I shifted on my chair and turned sideways to face her.

``Clubs\dots\ No, not yet. I'm planning to decide after trying a few out.''

``Yeah, that makes sense. I'm still unsure too.''

Just as Mizasaki predicted---no, just as he hoped---I'd ended up talking with Iwama in a natural flow.

By the way, to Iwama's left was a rowdy-looking boy. He was off somewhere with his group.

``Iwama-san, do you have any clubs you're thinking about?''

As basic courtesy, I asked the same question back, and Iwama let out a small ``Mmm'' as she thought.

``Let's see\dots\ My mom told me I should join a sports club, but I'm not really that good at athletics, so\dots''

Modest of her. Iwama was on the taller side, but she wasn't delicate. Even her build made it seem like she could handle any sport.

``In middle school---what club were you in?''

When I asked, Iwama hesitated a little, even though it wasn't like she had anything in her mouth.

``Um\dots\ it's kind of a weird club. Izuta-kun, you might laugh if I tell you.''

``I'm not going to laugh.''

If it were something like a Hide-and-Seek Research Society, or an Ojou-sama Club\dots\ I might have laughed, though.

``Really?''

Even as I braced myself for just how weird it could be, I nodded.

Looking a bit guilty, Iwama parted her lips slightly.

``I\dots\ I was actually in the Kagaku Club. You know---growing alum crystals, looking at stars, doing vegetation surveys\dots\ I wasn't seriously active or anything, I was more of a casual `enjoy it' member.''

Leaving aside whether being an ``enjoy it'' member was good or bad, there was nothing there that seemed laugh-worthy.

``Huh. Actually, I was---''

I was about to say I'd been in the chemistry club too, when I noticed something off. Alum was fine, but a chemistry club wouldn't be looking at stars. And vegetation surveys were pretty questionable too.

``Kagaku Club, like science?''

``Yeah! The kagaku with the `grain' radical---science!''

Science and chemistry. They were close in meaning and pronounced the same, so misunderstandings like this happened sometimes.

``I don't think that's a weird club at all. Actually, I was in the Kagaku Club too. Though mine was the chemistry one. The ba-kagaku club.''

``Whaaat---really?''

She reacted in a loud voice, then clapped a hand over her mouth.

But I didn't miss the way her dark eyes truly flashed---brightly---rather than as mere rhetoric. Maybe it was just the fluorescent lights reflecting at that angle, since she leaned in slightly toward me.

In a voice she lowered just a bit, Iwama said,

``I can totally see it, Izuta-kun. You'd look good in a lab coat.''

No one had ever told me that before. It sounded like a compliment, but it probably wasn't. More likely it was a roundabout way of saying, ``You seem really indoorsy.'' I figured it was one of this honor student's skills---making anything sound like praise.

``I actually wore one pretty often. We met three times a week, and about half of it was experiments.''

``Like what? What kind of experiments?''

``If I'm picking one I liked\dots\ the luminol reaction. Do you know it, Iwama-san?''

``Yeah! The reaction they use in forensic science---the one that glows if there's blood.''

As expected of a former science club member. She was quick, which helped.

``Right. I liked how it can detect even tiny traces of blood. We split into a criminal trying to destroy the evidence and a forensics team trying to find it, and played it like an investigation game. In the end, just as you'd expect, the forensics side won easily. That's how we learned how amazing catalytic reactions are.''

I'd been on the forensics side. No matter how many methods Mizasaki could think of to wipe away the bloodstains as the ``criminal,'' he was powerless before chemistry. I still remembered the frustrated look on Mizasaki's face from back then.

Before I knew it, Iwama was staring at me with her mouth slightly open, blankly.

---Not good. I'd talked too much.

When I did this in front of normal people, it almost always triggered this kind of reaction. When I'd talked about the symphony of mini tomatoes, even Mizasaki had given me a strained smile once.

``Sorry. You can just tune that out.''

When I said it, Iwama snapped back and shook her head.

``Ah---no, that's not it. It's just---''

I never found out what that ``just'' was.

``Well, well, Delta---can't let my guard down around you. A capable man moves fast!''

Mizasaki came over with a beaming grin and sat down beside Iwama. I considered killing him.

``Ah, um\dots\ Mizasaki-kun!''

Iwama replied with a raised brow and a friendly smile.

Even if she'd remember the name of the person in the seat in front of her, the fact that she remembered the name of Mizasaki---who sat almost on the opposite side of the classroom---was impressive. Forty students in Class 1-C, and we'd only met faces yesterday.

Maybe being called by name caught Mizasaki off guard too, because he froze in front of Iwama. It wasn't often you got to see this guy freeze without it being an act.

``\dots Yeah. Wow, Iwama-san---you already remember your classmates' names?''

``Kind of. But you remembered mine too, Mizasaki-kun.''

``Well, I mean---when you're Iwama-san, right?''

Saying that, Mizasaki regained his usual grin and looked between me and Iwama. The hair color he'd dyed so subtly looked almost black under the classroom lighting.

``Huh? Am I that famous?''

``Of course you are. When there's a single beautiful cherry tree blooming proudly in a mountain full of nameless underbrush, anyone's going to notice. Right, Delta?''

Don't ask me.

To signal that I wasn't going to play along, I stuffed a mini tomato into my mouth.

Seeing that I didn't answer, Iwama smiled and asked Mizasaki instead.

``Um, Mizasaki-kun\dots\ what's `Delta'?''

She was good at steering the topic away. She'd probably brushed off flashy guys like this before.

But of all topics, it was one that would make this guy happy.

``Oh! That's a great question. `Delta' is actually the real name of Izuta Sho right here.''

``Huh? Delta is your real name\dots?''

Even though she obviously knew it was a lie, Iwama asked with pure curiosity, without a trace of sarcasm.

``Yup. `Izuta' is basically like a posthumous Buddhist name. Back in middle school, everyone called him Delta. Iwama-san, you should call him Delta too.''

``Don't kill me off without permission.''

Seriously---just rattling off nonsense without a care\dots

While we were at it, the school bell rang.

By the time I hurriedly finished the rest of my lunch, lunch break was over.

``Sounds like a major incident happened.''

When Mizasaki said it like that, it was usually something ridiculously trivial.

``That's terrible. Let's go home quickly.''

I changed shoes and stepped out of the entryway. Classes were over; all that was left was to go home.

``Hey, hey, you can't go home. I'm sorry, okay? Are you still mad about lunch?''

``I'm not the kind of person who gets mad over something like that. But it was pretty rude to Iwama-san.''

``Yeah, you're right. I messed up. The second she said `Mizasaki-kun,' my brain just went all cherry-blossom pink.''

``Isn't your brain cherry-blossom pink by default?''

``Well, sure. With white brain cells and red hemoglobin, the brain's probably close to pale pink.''

He really was a guy who could talk his way out of anything, in the stupidest places.

``Reflect on what you did.''

``Don't worry. I properly apologized---casually, like it was no big deal---for getting clingy and annoying.''

I was relieved to hear he'd actually reflected on it. Mizasaki was a guy who, as the price of his communication skills, sometimes did insensitive, pushy things---but at heart, he was surprisingly decent.

``\dots So what's the `major incident'?''

When I asked, Mizasaki suddenly stopped. A broad grin turned toward me.

``There are two cherry trees growing on the back hill.''

``The back hill?''

I looked back. Beyond the school building, I could see a patch of forest on slightly higher ground.

Tsunanagai was a town with the south facing the Pacific Ocean, and mountains on the other three sides. The farther north you went from the sea, the higher the elevation became, little by little. Our Tsunanagai High School was located in the northern part. The school gate faced the southern sea, and behind the school buildings---past the grounds---there were mountains.

``Yeah, the back hill. You can't see them from here, but anyway, there are two cherry trees. They grow right next to each other, and apparently they're called the Meoto-zakura. It's a super popular spot.''

``Good for you.''

``Right? And get this---those cherry trees are at their peak right now.''

So he wanted to go look.

``It must be a late-blooming variety.''

``Yeah. And those cherry trees have a legend---a really interesting legend.''

I nodded to prompt him on.

``They bloom so that at the peak, the two trees make this unbelievably beautiful heart shape. And if a man and a woman see it---then they absolutely end up together.''

``Men and women only? In the Reiwa era?''

When I jabbed at him, Mizasaki tilted his head, looking a bit serious.

``Hmm. That's probably just a figure of speech. I don't think it has to be a man and a woman. The point is romantic success.''

``So Mizasaki wants to go see that tree with me.''

``Yeah. That's basically it.''

At his cheerful answer, I deliberately left a short pause.

``\dots Should I take that as a confession?''

``Wh---! I mean, Delta is an important best friend, but---b-but it's not like I think of Delta that way, or anything\dots!''

And so we ended up going to the back hill.

Once we passed through the school gate, we could see the town center beyond the ginkgo-lined slope. And farther beyond that, the sea. In the bright sunlight, the waves glittered and flashed. The forecast said the weather would break later, but it looked like it would hold for a while longer.

Instead of heading down the tree-lined road, we took a side path toward the back of the school. It was narrow, but paved. Since the campus was surrounded by mountains, they probably used this road whenever they needed to bring something in toward the grounds.

``Still, it's suspicious. `If you see it, you'll definitely end up together'---if you look even a little, it feels like you'd find an easy counterexample.''

In response to my point, Mizasaki wagged his index finger like tsk, tsk.

``Sure, yeah. `Definitely' might be overselling it. But apparently for the last nineteen years, at least one pair has succeeded every year. That's amazing, isn't it?''

``Who did you hear something that shady from?''

``A trustworthy senpai. Anyway, nineteen years of miracles is a real fact.''

``\dots That's just probability. If it's a famous cherry-blossom spot and a bunch of pairs go every year, then sure, at least one pair might end up together. And if two people are close enough to go see `romance legend' cherry trees together, their love is probably going to succeed on its own even without borrowing the tree's power.''

``Seriously. A guy who's too logical doesn't get popular.''

After a small shrug, Mizasaki gave me a grin and looked my way.

``And there's more to this `major incident.'''

``There is?''

``There is.''

Maybe he was trying to maximize the impact, because Mizasaki slowly exhaled and left a pause.

``According to that senpai, apparently this year, nobody's seen the heart mark. Not a single person.''

``\dots Why not?''

``That's what I've been wondering. Supposedly the shape got messed up, and it doesn't even look like a heart anymore, even if you're being generous.''

``That's a shame.''

I imagined a couple going out of their way to head up the back hill with hopes of romantic success, only to fail to see the heart. Just imagining it made me feel sorry for them. If anything, they'd end up feeling like their love life had been sabotaged.

It hurt far more to have your expectations betrayed than to never expect anything in the first place.

``Well, seeing is believing. Anyway, let's go check it out. Sounds interesting, right?''

``Yeah.''

I quietly followed Mizasaki as he pushed ahead, leading the way.

Walking along the fence brought us out behind what looked like a gym storage shed. The path widened there, and there was even a plastic bench. Maybe this was where the sports clubs came to take breaks. It didn't look like it was used much; a rusty steel can and a mud-caked hardball lay off to one side.

``We should be heading onto the hill around here\dots''

Mizasaki said, scanning the area.

Then, all of a sudden, he turned to face me.

``Ah. Whoops.''

It sounded oddly deliberate.

``Sorry---I just remembered I've got something I absolutely can't miss today. See you tomorrow.''

``Huh?''

``I'll make it up to you, I swear! I'll buy you a whole case of mini tomatoes, so forgive me. Later!''

Mizasaki winked, pressed his hands together in front of his face, and took off at a jog.

And he didn't even run back the way we'd come. He ran in the opposite direction.

If he were really in a hurry to go home, the shortest route would be to retrace our steps---so why?

As I turned to look back down the path---

I understood why Mizasaki had vanished so suddenly.

And that reason more than explained why he'd run off in the opposite direction.

From the far end of the path, the reason came walking toward me.

---It was Iwama Rio.

``Huh? Delta-kun, why are you in a place like this?''

Iwama had her school bag with her. She was probably on her way home. She stopped in front of me.

``I got forcibly dragged here\dots\ And for the record, you don't have to force yourself to call me Delta.''

``Really? Then\dots\ Izuta-kun.''

That was far better. Being called ``Delta-kun'' didn't exactly make me happy.

``Why are you here, Iwama-san?''

``Me? I actually heard something that sounded interesting, so I came to check it out.''

I had a bad feeling. A very bad feeling.

``\dots Something interesting?''

``There's this famous cherry tree on the back hill, and apparently if you see it, you'll be happy. And only around the time you start school, this warding pattern called an Inome shows up! I couldn't stop wondering about it, so I came to see it.''

I see. Little by little, I was starting to understand the situation.

You didn't need to be a famous detective to realize this wasn't something chance had put together.

``\dots Was that, by any chance, something you heard from Mizasaki?''

``Yeah! I didn't totally get it, but he told me about the Inome thing as an apology for something.''

That bastard\dots

``Did you come here too, Izuta-kun---maybe because Mizasaki-kun told you about it?''

``\dots Well, I wouldn't say he `told' me so much as he `dragged' me.''

For the record, Inome basically meant a heart shape. I didn't know whether the real legend was romantic success or warding off evil, but Mizasaki had probably switched the story depending on who he was talking to, and arranged to bring me and Iwama together here. What a fraud.

``Huh? But where's Mizasaki-kun?''

``He said he had something to do, and he went home.''

Leaving me behind.

``Oh\dots I see. Even though Mizasaki-kun sounded like he was interested in the Inome too. Poor thing.''

If Mizasaki had been interested in anything today, it was probably just one thing: what would happen if he threw Iwama at me and watched the results---

As I thought that, something about Iwama's words struck me as odd.

``\dots Poor thing? Mizasaki?''

``Yeah. Because tonight, a really strong low-pressure system is supposed to come in, and the forecast says it'll get pretty rough, right? So the blossoms might all fall. That means this might be the last chance.''

``I see. So if you miss today, there's no chance.''

After I'd said that much, I had a vague---very vague---sense of what was about to happen. To make matters worse, it seemed Mizasaki hadn't told Iwama about the romantic-success part. Iwama thought the cherry trees on this back hill were simply a famous spot that brought good luck.

And worse still, Iwama now knew I'd come to see the cherry trees too.

If this was how it was going to go, then Iwama would---

``Then since we're here, Izuta-kun, want to go see it together?''

Iwama flashed that ``did you see that smile?'' smile, looking delighted.

``Uh\dots''

``Huh? Were you already planning to head home?''

``No, not exactly.''

``Then let's go! You want to start high school off with good luck, right?''

Starting high school off with good luck\dots\ huh.

It was hard to refuse. More accurately, I didn't have a reason I could use to refuse. There was no way I could bring up the romantic-success legend here and say, ``It'll be awkward if it's just the two of us.'' I couldn't say that.

This was all exactly what Mizasaki wanted. He was probably somewhere right now, imagining what would happen if he shone this much blinding sunlight on a shut-in like me, and getting excited about it.

I thought it over.

And then---wasn't this an opportunity to prove that nothing would happen?

I could show him that my way of living wouldn't be shaken by something like this.

``\dots Yeah. Since we're here, let's go take a look.''

``Mm-hm! Let's go find it---the cherry tree of happiness!''

The moment she said it, Iwama hurried into the mountain path as if she couldn't wait. There were no signs, but I didn't see any other trail that went up the hill. This had to be the way.

It was awkward, so I followed a little behind Iwama. The forest was bright. Last year's acorns lay scattered underfoot. The woods on the back hill were a deciduous broadleaf forest---centered on the producers of those acorns, in other words, mainly konara and kunugi oaks.

They were called deciduous because they dropped their leaves in autumn. Broadleaf because the leaves were wide and flat. A forest with many trees like that.

Originally, this warm region should have been in a climate band where evergreen broadleaf forests stayed leafy even in winter, but people had gone into the mountains and cut trees for so long that fast-growing deciduous broadleaf trees had become more common.

And deciduous forests were bright.

In winter, when the leaves had completely fallen, sunlight could reach all the way to the ground. In this season of new buds, pale green leaves decorated the branches sparsely, and when you looked up at the sky, it almost felt like looking at stained glass.

Cherry trees were deciduous too. They thrived in bright forests---sunlit, popular.

``Hey, Izuta-kun!''

Iwama stopped and turned back toward me.

``There are so many katakuri blooming. That's rare, isn't it? Does this mountain have a lot of them?''

The ground was still mostly brown. Here and there, clusters of purplish pale-pink flowers bloomed. Their long, narrow petals curled back as they opened, beautiful like tiny pinwheels.

``Yeah. Katakuri are common in deciduous forests like this.''

She'd brought up a plant name, so my mouth moved on its own.

Not a good sign. I hurriedly shut my mouth. I was fully aware of it, but once I started talking, I went on for a long time. In front of people I didn't know well, I was especially careful to hold back.

``Huh? Why?''

Iwama looked at me with genuine interest.

``Sorry\dots If I explain, it'll take a while. Don't worry about it.''

``Huh? I want to hear. Tell me while we walk.''

She asked for it instead.

Well, it might be better than silence. I decided to talk a little.

``You know katakuriko, right? The starch you use to thicken food.''

``Yeah. I haven't really used it for cooking, but I used like three kilograms of it for a dilatancy experiment once!''

\dots?

That sounded like a strange statement. Was there an experiment that required three kilograms of katakuriko? At the very least, it didn't sound like chemistry.

``Dilatancy?''

``Ah---sorry, I said something weird! Don't mind it.''

Iwama, for some reason looking apologetic, urged me on. Since I didn't really know what dilatancy was either, I continued.

``\dots These days katakuriko is mostly made from potatoes, but originally it was made from this katakuri plant. You probably know that, Iwama-san.''

``Right---the starch in the katakuri's root was the source. That's why it's called katakuriko.''

``Yeah. Katakuri is a plant that stores starch---its nutrients---in the underground stem. It takes years to gradually build those reserves, and it's only after about eight years from germination that it finally blooms for the first time.''

``Eight years\dots It lives that long, even though it's such a small flower.''

``That's katakuri's survival strategy.''

On a south-facing slope, under the dazzling new buds, katakuri basked in the light and flaunted its fleeting glory.

My mouth had already become impossible to stop.

``Sunlight still reaches the ground directly at this time of year, before the trees fill out with leaves. Katakuri spreads its leaves for photosynthesis for only two or three months---until it turns warmer, the greenery deepens, and the ground becomes shaded.''

``Only two or three months? I guess you don't see them in summer\dots''

``Right. It stores the nutrients it makes during that time in the underground stem. Once the leaves above thicken and sunlight stops reaching the ground, katakuri withers, leaving only the underground stem. It's a way of life where it builds growth little by little over a long time, so it takes years before it can bloom. It's basically like it goes into a long hibernation from summer through winter.''

``I see---so that's why it's a deciduous forest.''

Iwama clapped her hands once, looking convinced.

``Because you can only do photosynthesis when the ground is sunlit---when the leaves have fallen. In a forest that stays leafy all winter, it turns shady and you can't store nutrients.''

I was glad she caught on so quickly.

Katakuri's survival strategy only worked in forests where leaves fell in winter.

``Exactly. Since it only appears above ground for a brief moment in spring, plants that live like katakuri sometimes get called this---''

``\textit{Spring ephemeral}!''

Iwama answered before I could, and it startled me.

``So you already knew.''

``Ah, um\dots I didn't know it well or anything, but\dots you know, when I was flipping through my biology textbook, I feel like I saw that word. And I thought it sounded kind of cute.''

``That's an impressive memory.''

The textbook sale had only taken place recently. I hadn't even opened any of them yet.

Spring ephemeral---a fleeting thing of spring. Iwama didn't need the Japanese explanation.

The mountain path continued as a staircase made by laying thin logs across it. I noticed my breathing had sped up, and I realized I'd talked too much. I'd let the words pour out and, in doing so, I'd probably even neglected the oxygen intake I needed for walking. And I'd neglected my consideration for her, too.

I'd tried to sound smart. I'd shown off my knowledge.

And I'd done it to a girl I'd barely met.

She'd been in the science club, and she even knew terms like ``close-packed structure'' and ``spring ephemeral.'' She called herself an enjoyer, but she was probably fairly knowledgeable about science too.

And in front of someone like that, I---

Iwama stopped.

``\dots What is it?''

``Sorry. I was kind of pretending I knew what I was talking about\dots even though I'm not really knowledgeable in this field.''

She looked genuinely apologetic. She worried about strange things.

``Pretending you know something is acting like you know something you don't know. Saying you know a term you actually know isn't wrong.''

``Maybe\dots But I only skimmed over `spring ephemeral' in a textbook, and I've never actually seen katakuri roots in real life, either\dots''

There was a broken branch nearby, so I picked it up.

``If that's the issue, then the first half is already solved, and the second half can be solved like this.''

I examined the nearby ground, then stabbed the branch into the soil beside a katakuri that was growing in soft-looking dirt. I repeated it a few times and dug the katakuri up with the soil. As I carefully brushed the dirt away, a small white bulb appeared---like the stem had simply thickened slightly.

Iwama watched as I extracted the katakuri, her eyes wide. Digging up a flower might have been a bit tactless, but since this was probably school grounds, I wanted it to be overlooked as a learning activity.

``This is the katakuri's bulb---its underground stem. It isn't actually a root; nutrients are stored in the stem portion, the same way they are in a potato.''

When I handed it to her, Iwama said, ``Thank you,'' and accepted it carefully, as if she were touching delicate glasswork. She stared at the bulb, absorbed. She touched it with a finger, then pinched it lightly.

\fullpageimage{2.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}
``It's tiny\dots''

``Back when people actually made katakuriko, it must've been a real ordeal.''

I took a plastic convenience store bag from my backpack and opened it up. I put the katakuri that Iwama handed back into it, puffed a little air into the bag, and tied it shut. Iwama watched the whole sequence with interest.

``What are you going to do with that?''

``I'm thinking I'll take it home and make a pressed plant specimen or something.''

A pressed plant specimen is, simply put, created by flattening a plant like a pressed flower and drying it. I already had a katakuri specimen at home, so I could've just discarded it here, but I didn't feel comfortable doing that in front of Iwama. I stowed the plastic bag in my backpack.

Today's objective was the cherry trees. We started walking along the mountain path again.

``So you were in the chemistry club, Izuta-kun. The bakegaku one.''

``\dots Yeah.''

``But you studied plants too? Your katakuri explanation felt more like biology than chemistry\dots\ You were really knowledgeable. And pressed plant specimens don't sound like something a chemistry club would do.''

``Yeah\dots\ Well. A lot happened.''

When I brushed it off, Iwama smiled at me.

``You like plants, don't you?''

``Not exactly\dots\ I admire them.''

``Plants?''

``Yeah. Because they're straightforward.''

Iwama tilted her head slightly, and I realized I hadn't said enough.

``Plants always live in a way that's true to themselves, straight through. I admire that.''

Iwama looked at me, surprised. Then she quickly returned to a polite smile.

``\dots Living without hesitation does sound kind of enviable.''

``Right.''

Had we walked quite a bit by now? When I looked back, the school building's rooftop was far below us.

Iwama stopped, and when I raised my eyes, a boy and a girl were coming down toward us, chatting amicably. Iwama moved to the side to let them pass. I did the same.

``After all that climbing, it was seriously a letdown.''

``Well, it can't be helped. The wind was strong this winter.''

They were upperclassmen, judging by their tone. Flashy-looking, and the way they stood close made them seem like a couple. The two of them glanced at us in unison, then waved with cheerful smiles.

Iwama bowed her head, so I gave a small bow too. It was a convenient excuse to avert my eyes.

After passing the couple, I worried Iwama might start thinking the cherry trees we were heading to were that kind of place, but there didn't seem to be any need. She kept walking ahead as usual.

The mountain was quiet. According to Mizasaki, it was supposed to be a ``super popular spot,'' but there was no one else at all. We'd climbed quite a ways, but the only people we'd passed were that upperclassman couple.

``Ah---maybe that's it!''

Iwama pointed around the bend.

When I caught up, I could see the pale pink of cherry blossoms ahead. All around was new greenery that looked like watercolor, but that spot alone was vivid---like someone had dripped oil paint across it---cherry blossoms blooming in profusion. They were in full bloom.

As we drew closer, I could hear cheerful chirping---chun-chun.

Looking more closely, I saw a lot of sparrows perched in the cherry tree. They innocently bit the blossoms off at the base, sucked out only the nectar, and tossed the rest to the ground. Because this feeding method doesn't carry pollen, it's sometimes called nectar-robbing. From the cherry tree's perspective, they were visitors with nothing but downsides.

``It's a mess.''

``But sparrows are cute, so you can forgive them, right?''

``\dots Can you?''

Iwama and I stopped, almost unconsciously, in front of the cherry trees. Sparrows flocked in the surrounding trees as well. Iwama watched them with an affectionate look.

``I think sparrows are creatures God designed.''

She said something outlandish, and it didn't even sound like a joke.

``They're small and round, with perfectly round eyes, and most of all---the black spots on their cheeks! I bet God decided to create something unbelievably cute and came up with them.''

When I stared at her, dumbfounded, Iwama flustered and waved both hands in front of her chest.

``Of course I'm joking, but\dots\ we live in the age of Neo-Darwinism, right?''

As she said that, Iwama turned her body toward the cherry trees.

The suspicion swirling inside me hardened into certainty. Neo-Darwinism is the modern form of evolutionary theory---Darwin's concept of natural selection strengthened by newer findings.

A top student studying ahead wouldn't be strange on its own, but slipping that term smoothly into casual conversation was another matter entirely. She wasn't just a good student.

Iwama wasn't an ``enjoyer''---no matter how you looked at it, she was a serious one.

The two large yamazakura trees stood on the valley side of the slope, leaning close as if nestled together. Once the trail got close enough, it curved to keep its distance as it detoured around them. I still couldn't see anything that looked like an inome (or a heart). We continued forward.

But right past the bend, the path ended in a dead stop.

``Whoa\dots\ this is bad.''

A huge fallen tree. Near the cherries, a large konara had snapped at the base and lay across the path, blocking it completely. Judging from the soil around the roots, it hadn't been down for very long. The wind had been strong this winter; it must've fallen sometime during that season. Black-and-yellow striped tape was wound around the trunk over and over.

We stopped. It looked like we couldn't go farther unless we climbed over. It seemed possible to scramble up, but the tape suggested the area was off-limits.

``So it's a dead end.''

``But look! I think you're supposed to view the cherry trees from there.''

Iwama pointed off to the side of the trail. There was a wooden platform set up facing the cherry trees---just wide enough for two people to sit side by side. And unbelievably, the platform itself was shaped like a heart.

There was a sign attached to it, but time had done its work. Gray lichen covered the surface, and it was completely unreadable. I used a fallen branch to scrape it off, and at last the letters appeared.

It said: Cherry-viewing platform. Beneath that was an explanation.
\begin{center}
{\bfseries
Please cherish the plants! Do not leave the trail, 

and view the cherry trees from this platform $\heartsuit$

\medskip
Graduating Class of Heisei 16
}
\end{center}

It seemed to be a graduation project. When I looked at it that way, the construction really was rough. High school seniors from twenty years ago had probably cut the lumber and assembled it. Judging by the grain, the material seemed to be a hardwood, and even after all these years, it remained without rotting. It was probably chestnut. Wood that was hard and contained a lot of tannins didn't rot easily even in damp places, and had been used for things like building foundations and railroad ties.

Iwama, perhaps after seeing the heart at the end of the explanation for the cherry-viewing platform, climbed onto it and looked at the cherry blossoms.

``What a view! From here, it really might be that the cherry blossoms look the best.''

Behind Iwama as she looked impressed, I stayed where I was on the mountain trail and didn't move.

``Huh? Izuta-kun, come on up too! It's dirty, but it's sturdier than you'd think!''

Iwama invited me innocently, but that wasn't the issue.

This heart-shaped platform was obviously meant for couples.

``What's wrong?''

Iwama tilted her head. There was no way I could refuse by saying something like, ``Well, getting on a heart with a girl one-on-one is kind of embarrassing,'' so I put on a face like I didn't care and lined up next to Iwama.

The designer had to have been a schemer. The cherry-viewing platform was sized so perfectly that it looked like two people could stand on it side by side, but as I stood there, it felt cramped; if I moved even a little, our shoulders might have brushed.

I looked at the cherry blossoms, trying not to be aware of it as much as possible.

It was a platform placed so you could get a little closer to the cherry blossoms than from the mountain trail. Not too close, not too far---two yamazakura trees in full bloom looked like they were covering my field of view perfectly.

My breath stopped without me realizing it.

It was as if the entire world had turned into spring.

``\dots Yeah. It's beautiful.''

The petals, a deeper pale pink than Somei-yoshino, rose with even a slight breeze and rained down over us. Sparrows busily stealing nectar dropped blossoms one after another.

The ground around us was blanketed not only with cherry petals, but also with katakuri flowers. This cherry-viewing platform off the trail probably also served to keep visitors from stepping on these katakuri.

On the back mountain, where the green still wasn't deep, this space might have been the most vividly colored place of all.

``Hmmm\dots''

But Iwama looked like she wasn't quite convinced.

``\dots Hey, which one do you think is the Inome? We're supposed to be able to see a pattern that makes you happy, right?''

``Oh yeah, there was that story too.''

I said it as if I wasn't that interested.

``What kind of pattern is the Inome, Izuta-kun? Do you know?''

``Uh, no. I mean, what was it again\dots''

I'd hoped it would end with that vagueness, but it didn't. Iwama took out her phone and started looking it up.

``\dots Figure it out?''

When I asked, Iwama stared at the screen for a bit, then nodded.

``Yeah. Apparently Inome is written as `boar's eye,' and it refers to a heart-like pattern.''

``Ah, I see. So that's why the cherry-viewing platform is shaped like a heart too.''

``Oh, right. That might be it!''

I wasn't good at acting, but it seemed the innocent Iwama hadn't suspected I was pretending not to know.

Iwama lifted her gaze from her phone screen back to the cherry blossoms.

``But it's weird. I can't really see a shape that looks like an Inome\dots\ Maybe that big gap between the two trees is it?''

The Meoto-zakura each spread their branches in full bloom, filling my view with pale pink. Between them was a hollow opening where the blue sky looked cut out. But it was a distorted inverted triangle. No matter how you interpreted it, it wasn't a heart---no, it wasn't a shape you could call an Inome.

``Calling this an Inome is kind of a stretch, isn't it?''

``Yeah\dots''

Her voice sounded more down than I'd expected, and I turned to look at Iwama.

Maybe it was a bad prank by the cherry trees. But a faint shadow seemed to have fallen across Iwama's face.

I see.

Unlike me, who hadn't been expecting anything at all, Iwama had been looking forward to finding a pattern that would make her happy.

---I mean, I want my high school life to go well!

I remembered what Iwama had said before we entered the mountain. The straightforward honor student heard an interesting rumor, and even if it was just at the level of a charm, she'd climbed the back mountain in search of happiness.

But that happiness didn't exist.

Mizasaki had said it. Inome or heart, nobody had been able to see it this year. It had probably already been lost. That guy had committed an unbelievable sin. Just because he wanted to mess with me, he'd dragged Iwama into it and made her chase happiness she couldn't grasp.

More than never having expectations in the first place, having your expectations betrayed hurt far more.

I searched for words to encourage her somehow.

``The winds were strong this winter. Maybe a branch broke and the ha---Inome collapsed. Look, there's even a tree down.''

I pointed at the fallen tree blocking the path just ahead.

``But\dots\ the fallen tree didn't hit the cherry trees, right?''

Just as Iwama said, the fallen tree and the two cherry trees were positioned so they didn't look like they could come into contact.

``It's also possible a cherry branch snapped in the wind. It can't be helped.''

Iwama was still resting a hand on her chin, staring intently at the cherry blossoms.

``Is that really true? We still don't know. It's not like we've found any evidence of a broken branch.''

``Y-Yeah\dots''

A bewildered sound slipped out before I could stop it.

Something was off. Iwama's gaze was more serious than I'd ever seen it.

Not the profile of a cheerful girl who'd come to see the `happy' cherry blossoms on the back mountain, but the profile of a researcher I knew well.

``\dots Ah! Sorry!''

All of a sudden, Iwama snapped back to her usual expression and looked at me.

``Forget it! I've got this bad habit of getting serious the second there's something I don't understand\dots\ and we don't want the weather to turn, so should we head back?''

Her smile was bright, but I'd already noticed it was different in kind from that ``Did you see that smile?'' smile.

At least for now, she wasn't smiling from the heart.

The weather would probably hold a while longer. Even if I couldn't help her grasp happiness, it wouldn't hurt to stick with her until she felt satisfied.

``No. We can verify what we can verify here before we go back, right? It's not like I have something to do after this. According to Mizasaki, for nineteen years up through last year, people could see the cherry Inome. If it suddenly can't be seen this year, there has to be a reason.''

There was a brief pause, as if she were probing my true intentions.

``\dots You'll investigate with me?''

``Yeah. If it's a botanical consideration, I might be able to help a little.''

``Really\dots?''

She kept double-checking. Well, if we were just confirming whether branches had broken, it wouldn't take that long. I nodded.

The ``Did you see that smile?'' returned in full bloom across Iwama's face.

``Thank you! Then let's test it\dots\ scientifically!''

The sudden word choice left me a little thrown off.

``Scientifically?''

``Yeah. I want to properly figure out---with a scientific attitude---why the Inome we should've been able to see until now suddenly can't be seen this year.''

Calling it ``scientific'' just to solve a small mystery around us was an awfully grand way to put it.

Iwama had already changed so much that you could fairly call it a full-on form change.

It was like she was leaning forward---no, like she could only see what was in front of her. Either way, I felt a kind of heat bursting out of her that couldn't fit inside the frame of ``honor student.''

I thought she was interesting.

From the way it seeped out in bits and pieces throughout our conversation, Iwama was probably a serious science lover. As someone in the sciences myself, it didn't sound so bad to go along with that pursuit.

``Alright. Then first, let's check whether any branches have snapped. We can't see well from here. Should we get closer to the cherry trees?''

``Yeah! \dots But we have to make sure we don't step on the flowers.''

At her words, I looked down. Katakuri flowers bloomed evenly along the mountain trail. Maybe because the sunlight was good, they were so dense that if you stepped off the path, you'd end up stepping on katakuri no matter what.

``Is there any way we can get closer to the cherry trees without stepping on the flowers?''

As she said that, Iwama immediately started surveying the area. The mountain trail curved around to keep a certain distance from the two cherry trees. As far as I could see, katakuri were blooming across almost the entire area.

``Ah, Izuta-kun! If we go past that fallen tree, we might be able to reach them.''

Called by Iwama, I peered past the fallen tree. Sure enough, there was a patch where no katakuri were blooming at all, and if we went through there, we could probably get closer to the cherry trees to a certain extent.

``But there's restricted-area tape on that fallen tree---''

``It doesn't say `restricted area.' It's just yellow and black stripes!''

A dangerous statement popped out, and it took me by surprise. Iwama's curiosity didn't seem ordinary. But if it were anyone other than Iwama, I might have used the same logic myself.

``\dots Isn't it dangerous?''

``It's fine, it's fine! Izuta-kun, can you hold this for a second?''

With that, Iwama handed me her school bag and, as if it were only natural, put her hands on the fallen tree.

She maneuvered her body skillfully, avoiding branches as she placed a foot on the trunk.

``Wait. It's dangerous.''

Iwama stopped and looked back at me, puzzled.

``Huh? But it looks like the branches are pretty sturdy\dots''

``That's not what I mean\dots\ it's, uh\dots''

It was hard to say, so I broke off---but I'd already started, so I couldn't just gloss over it.

``\dots Your skirt.''

I took a step back, averted my eyes, and pointed it out. If Iwama took one more step, it was almost guaranteed to enter my field of view.

``Ah\dots''

Iwama hurried back down from the fallen tree.

``Sorry! I was so focused that I didn't notice. Ugh, I was torn about it, but maybe I really should've gone with slacks.''

Well, it would've been enough for me to just turn my back, but maybe Iwama had her pride as a girl---so crossing over the fallen tree and going to the other side was called off. After taking her school bag back from me, Iwama---still red-eared---unzipped it.

``Come to think of it, I brought binoculars. They might help.''

What she took out was a compact, foldable pair of binoculars.

She was almost too well-prepared.

``Why are you carrying binoculars around?''

``Come on, like, on the way to school\dots\ there are times you suddenly want to look at birds, right?''

``\dots Are there?''

Maybe because of the skirt thing, Iwama was a little flustered.

``You know, my dad bought them for me as an entrance gift.''

She showed me the binoculars, with their beautiful dark green body. Swarovski.

I didn't know whether Iwama herself realized it, but those had to be pretty expensive.

What kind of father buys that? Is it even okay to bring something like this to school? Does she really go birdwatching on the way to school? A whole string of questions raced through my mind. But this wasn't the time to sweat the small stuff---we needed to focus on solving the problem.

``Ten times magnification, huh. If we look at the cherry blossoms with that, we should be able to check the condition of the branches from here.''

``Yeah. Let's try.''

Iwama peered through the binoculars and adjusted the focus with practiced hands.

``Uhh, let's see\dots\ huh? Mmm\dots''

After searching for a while, Iwama lowered the binoculars and held them out to me.

``I couldn't tell if anything was broken. Izuta-kun, you take a look too.''

``\dots No, I'm fine.''

Looking through the binoculars that a girl from my class had been pressing her face against just a second ago\dots\ I don't know. I felt a bit resistant.

``It reduces the chance we miss something, so come on!''

She really was trying to investigate this carefully. There was no strong reason to refuse, so I took the binoculars. I pushed my glasses up onto my forehead, then looked through them.

``\dots This is impressive. For something this small, it's so clear.''

Not that I was trying to cover my embarrassment, but the first thing that came out was my impression of the binoculars.

``Right? Even though they're compact, they're bright, so you can see well even in the woods.''

We were getting off track. I carefully observed the cherry branches where the sparrows were playing. Sure enough, I didn't see any sign of major damage, like a branch snapping.

``You're right, Iwama. It doesn't look like anything broke.''

``Then why did it stop being an Inome? Maybe last year the branches grew too much?''

I thought about it for a moment. That seemed unlikely.

``No. Cherry trees bloom on the branches that grew the previous year. None of the parts with flowers are that long. Once a tree gets this big, it's hard for the branch tips to suddenly shoot out and change the overall shape. Even if they did grow, I don't think it would change the shape of the ha---Inome as a whole.''

``Wow\dots\ you even know stuff like that!''

That might've been a bit niche.

I thanked her briefly and handed the binoculars back. Iwama immediately used them to look at the cherry blossoms again. As I slid my glasses back into place, I felt a strange itchiness around my eyes---nothing like cedar pollen, though.

``Hmmm. So it didn't break, and it didn't overgrow\dots''

Iwama lowered the binoculars and murmured.

``Then why can't we see the Inome anymore?''

Why had the shape of that pale-pink cutout changed?

Bathed in a blizzard of petals, listening to the sparrows chirp, I thought.

And then---

``I see\dots\ I think I figured it out.''

Iwama whipped around toward me with incredible speed.

``You figured out why we can't see the Inome?''

``Yeah. And I think I can explain how we'd be able to see the heart properly, too. If I borrow your phrasing, I think I can explain it scientifically, to an extent.''

``The heart?''

``No, sorry. The Inome. Not a heart.''

That mattered.

``So why?''

When Iwama asked, I pointed not at the sky full of drifting petals, but at the ground.

``The hint wasn't in the cherry blossoms. It was in the katakuri.''

My roundabout phrasing made Iwama's eyes widen in surprise.

``Huhhh? What do you mean?''

``The cherry branches weren't broken, and they hadn't grown drastically. If the shape of the cherry trees hasn't changed, then what changed?''

Iwama still looked as though it hadn't clicked for her yet.

``Us---the side that's looking at them.''

A brief silence.

``\dots Wait, so you're saying our viewing position was different?''

``Yeah. That's the only explanation. If where you stand changes, the shape you see will naturally change too.''

``But if that's the case, then this cherry-viewing platform\dots''

``Starting this year, the position of this cherry-viewing platform changed. And what tells us the correct position is---''

I pointed again to the katakuri blooming around the cherry-viewing platform.

``The katakuri.''

Iwama still didn't seem to get it. I continued.

``Katakuri are spring ephemerals. In order to bloom, they need about eight years of photosynthesis only in early spring, storing up nutrients. But what happens if there's a cherry-viewing platform there?''

Iwama looked like she'd realized it too, her cheeks flushing faintly.

``If there's a platform there, they can't get sunlight, so they can't photosynthesize!''

``Yeah. The Inome cut out by the cherry blossoms was visible every year for nineteen years up through last year. That means the cherry-viewing platform was in the correct place for a long time. In other words, at the spot where the platform used to be, katakuri shouldn't be able to bloom this year.''

``That's amazing. It's scientific!''

Apparently, Iwama really loved the word scientific.

``In this area where katakuri are blooming everywhere, there was one spot---just one---where, for some reason, no katakuri were blooming, right? If we stand there, we can test this hypothesis.''

``On the other side of the fallen tree\dots!''

Iwama started moving as if she couldn't wait. I followed. When we peered past the fallen tree, there was a section beside the mountain trail where you couldn't see even a hint of the katakuri's pale pink.

The reason Iwama had tried so hard to climb over the fallen tree was that she'd been trying to get closer to the cherry trees from this flowerless patch.

This was probably the cherry-viewing platform's original location. In other words, if I hadn't stopped her, Iwama would've found the Inome.

Meaning to take responsibility, I climbed up onto the fallen tree first.

Then I held out my hand to Iwama.

``If it's like this, you don't have to worry about your skirt, right?''

``Thanks!''

Iwama clasped my hand without hesitation. At the feel of it, my arm stiffened for a moment.

We climbed over the fallen tree together. We let go right away.

Iwama immediately stepped into the area cut out by pale pink and looked up at the cherry blossoms.

``Hey, Izuta-kun, this is incredible! Such a beautiful Inome!''

I stood beside Iwama and looked where she pointed.

My heart was stolen at once.

The two cherry trees leaned close like a married couple, their branches decorated with pale pink blossoms overlapping.

Between them was a cloudless blue sky, cleanly cut out into the shape of a perfect heart by the cherry blossoms.

``\dots So this really was the right position.''

Iwama murmured. I nodded.

``Someone who didn't think things through probably moved the cherry-viewing platform because they thought the fallen tree was dangerous.''

It was probably done with the students' safety in mind. So no one would have to climb over the fallen tree, they moved the cherry-viewing platform to this side of it over the winter. But when spring actually came, because the position had shifted just a little, the cherry heart could no longer be seen.

Iwama clasped her hands in front of her chest, her eyes even growing moist.

``This is amazing! Spotting the katakuri---Izuta-kun, that's your achievement!''

Iwama made her right hand into a fist and held it out toward me. I modestly made a fist as well and lightly bumped hers.

``This is your achievement too, Iwama. If it were just me, I probably never would've thought to climb over the fallen tree. And I wouldn't have checked whether any branches were broken, either.''

``You think so? Then it's both our achievement.''

Smiling, Iwama turned back to the cherry blossoms and clasped her hands again. She closed her eyes, almost like she was offering a prayer. She seemed to be making a wish.

Looking at the cherry blossoms myself, I wished that this straightforward girl's high school life would be a fortunate one.

``Thank you for staying with me.''

When Iwama thanked me again, I wasn't sure how to respond.

``No, it's not like\dots\ I had fun too. If you really dig into it, it's kind of amazing that the mystery can get solved like this.''

``A victory for science!''

It was another overblown way of putting it, but it wasn't exactly wrong.
