If you have an assignment or a question, you start by observing the subject, then you mobilize everything you've learned to objectively narrow down the possibilities. From there, you form hypotheses and test them. Even if it differed from actual research work in scale and timespan, this search for the Inome could still be called a scientific endeavor to some extent.

``That's a very `former science club member' way of putting it.''

A breeze swept through, and cherry petals wrapped around us. Iwama said happily,

``I love science. Using the collected wisdom of people all over the world, you can face the mysteries right in front of you. You can stand on the shoulders of giants and look out over the town you live in. There's nothing more fun than that.''

I mulled over Iwama's words. From the shoulders of giants, over your own town\dots\ Interesting.

As expected of Tsunanagai High School. I never would've thought there'd be a classmate with such an unusual way of thinking.

I could feel my chest naturally pounding at the luck of meeting someone like that right after enrolling.

``\dots I hadn't thought of it that way.''

``Huh? You seemed like you liked science too, Izuta-kun.''

``Research is definitely interesting. But that's because it makes you feel like you might be able to contribute to humanity with your own strength. I think---thought---science is, above all, an endeavor to broaden humanity's possibilities.''

After slowly breathing in the spring air, I added,

``But sure, it's not so bad to try looking around you from the shoulders of giants. We got to see a beautiful view like this, too.''

Iwama, nodding along, looked happy.

``Just changing how you look at things a little can make the world this wonderful.''

That was a wrap-up that really suited an honor student. No matter how much you threatened me, I doubt words like that would ever come out of my mouth.

What I did after that felt impulsive---or rather, I wondered if it was, in a sense, like some kind of accident. Still caught up in the spring mood, I ended up feeling like doing it.

``If you'd like, why don't you take this home, Iwama-san?''

I took the katakuri out of my bag, still in a plastic shopping bag. If I were going to do something like this, I should've put it in a nicer bag, I thought, regretting it even as I did so.

``Huh, really? Are you sure?''

``I actually already have a katakuri specimen. If you're interested, I thought you could try making it into a pressed specimen. You can make one with something simple, like newspaper or cardboard.''

I'm not normally the kind of person who would suggest something like that to a girl I've only just met. But it was rare to meet someone like Iwama---someone I could talk with so well, someone you could call a kindred spirit. Maybe I'd gotten a little carried away.

The katakuri with dirt still on it, and the wrinkled plastic shopping bag. It was far too shabby to be a gift for a girl, but Iwama accepted it with a happy look.

``I'll try making one. Thank you!''

We'd gotten to see a heart---no, an inome---and it was about time to head down the mountain.

Before we left the spot where the cherry-viewing platform originally stood, Iwama took out her phone as if she'd just remembered. She opened the camera and pointed it at the cherry blossoms.

The shutter clicked several times. Even so, still holding her phone up, Iwama seemed to be thinking about something.

``\dots What's wrong?''

When I asked, Iwama groaned with a troubled ``Mmm,'' looking unsure.

``It's beautiful, but\dots\ with this, you can't tell just from the photo that it was taken this year.''

True---there should already be plenty of photos from past years out there. I could understand wanting to preserve the value of it being something found this year.

``Want to shoot today's morning paper in it?''

When I said it as a joke, Iwama looked at me fairly seriously.

``Huh? You brought a newspaper?''

``No, I didn't. That was a joke. Sorry.''

``You don't have to apologize that much\dots\ We could also pull up today's date on your phone and take the picture that way, Izuta-kun, but that kind of feels like a kidnapper or something, and it might ruin the atmosphere.''

That was true.

``Then why not take a selfie? Or I can take one with you in it, Iwama-san.''

I thought it was a good suggestion, but Iwama frowned slightly and shook her head.

``My face is\dots\ it's not something worth going out of your way to photograph.''

I swallowed \textit{No, no, you're being modest} at the back of my throat.

Like Mizasaki said, Iwama's looks were far from ordinary, but putting that aside, I could completely understand the feeling of wanting to take photos while not wanting to be in them yourself.

After thinking for a moment, Iwama held out her free hand in front of her phone's camera.

``What are you doing\dots?''

``I thought maybe it'd be okay if I took a photo with my hand in it.''

She seemed particular about wanting to include something in the shot. But sure enough, if she took a picture with her hand in it, it could become something unique rather than just a landscape photo.

I could understand the logic, at least.

After trying a peace sign and a thumbs-up, Iwama's expression turned like she still wasn't satisfied.

``What's wrong?''

``Mmm\dots\ \dots none of them feel quite right\dots\ It's like they don't match the shape of the Inome.''

I lack the kind of artistic eye for what people call ``photogenic,'' so I wasn't confident I could help Iwama. Still, I offered an offhand suggestion.

``If the background is an inome, then why not make an inome with your hand, too? Like making a heart.''

``I see!''

As she said that, Iwama made half of a heart with one hand. Then she looked at me with expectant eyes.

``\dots?''

I was confused.

``One of my hands is full, so, Izuta-kun, help me!''

I didn't understand the logic.

``Isn't there a way to make a heart with just your thumb and index finger? Girls do it a lot.''

``That's a finger heart! This is an Inome, so it's a little different.''

``\dots I see?''

Sure, that ``finger heart'' was called a heart out in the world because it looked like a heart. Even if it had the same shape, it couldn't become an inome, where the concept of a heart didn't apply. On the other hand, if you tried to make an inome the way Iwama was doing it, you'd need both hands. But Iwama was holding her phone. She was one hand short. Up to that point, I could follow.

But would she really ask me for the other hand she needed?

Still, if Iwama didn't mind, maybe I was the one overthinking it. This wasn't a heart---it was an Inome, a shape that called in good fortune.

Standing beside her, I tried to bring my hand together with the one she held up.

But my heart leapt in my chest, and it wasn't good. I could feel my ears burning. Sweat dripped down.

In the end, I quickly pulled my hand back.

``\dots?''

``No, I mean\dots\ maybe it'd be fine if it were two girls, but\dots''

I said it vaguely because I didn't want her to think I was being weirdly self-conscious.

Iwama seemed to realize at last, her cheeks flushing faintly as her eyes went wide.

``Ah\dots\ sorry! That was really---really presumptuous of me, wasn't it!''

``No, it's not something you need to apologize for. I'm just suggesting we do a different shape.''

``Then, how about we both do peace signs?''

I didn't understand this logic at all.

``\dots If it's a peace sign, wouldn't just one be enough?''

Iwama gave a small shake of her head at my suggestion.

``I realized something. The reason I want my hand in this photo is that I want to capture the uniqueness of this exact moment.''

``Uniqueness\dots?''

It was a term I'd only ever heard in math.

``Yeah. I want to record the fact that you and I found this beautiful view together, Izuta-kun. So if it's only the scenery, and neither you nor I is in it, I thought that'd be a waste\dots''

It was, admittedly, a logically airtight explanation.

Well, a peace sign was probably fine. I had no reason to refuse, so I went to stand beside Iwama. I made a halfhearted peace sign and held it out in front of her phone's camera.

``Closer!''

Iwama stepped nearer. Our shoulders were close enough to brush. My heart jumped again.

Thinking of the men this girl had probably sent tumbling without even realizing it, I clasped my hands together in prayer in my head.

Click---Iwama hit the shutter.

In front of the heart-shaped blue sky framed by pale pink cherry blossoms, two peace signs lined up side by side, slightly dark from the backlight. It ended up looking exactly like the letter W.

``Yeah, this looks good. Thanks!''

Iwama seemed satisfied with that.

After that, the sun began to sink, so we hurried down the mountain. By the time we got back to the school gate, it was already close to evening.

``Oh, right. Izuta-kun, tell me your contact info.''

As we went down the tree-lined road toward town, Iwama made the suggestion.

``I'll send you that photo.''

``Yeah\dots\ thanks.''

And just like that, Iwama and I exchanged LINE contacts.

Once the row of trees ended, the road split. My house was in the city, so I'd turn left off the tree-lined street, but since Iwama commuted by train, it seemed she'd keep going down the road ahead toward the station.

We each set off on our way home.

Over the sea, a mass of developed rain clouds was stretching dark arms toward the setting sun.

``Whoa---this rain is insane!''

It was night, when my little sister said that and I looked outside, the scene was practically a storm.

Listening to the violent rain battering the window and the roaring sound of the wind rushing through, I looked at the photo Iwama had sent again before going to bed. Advances in technology were astonishing. Even with a tiny smartphone camera, the contrast between the cherry blossoms and the sky was crisp and vivid in high definition.

And in the foreground, Iwama's hand and mine were lined up together. Maybe she'd done some grading afterward---there wasn't much sense of darkness from the backlight.

A peace sign held out with total confidence, chest out\dots\

and a slumped, listless peace sign.

I was almost impressed that you could see such a difference in personality just from someone's hands.

The storm didn't let up easily. The cherry blossoms that had been in perfect full bloom would probably be scattered mercilessly.

A thought occurred to me.

What if---

Iwama and I ended up being the only boy and girl who saw that heart this year?

If that was the case, then assuming that ridiculous superstition was true, this could be bad.

The cherry blossoms behind the hill that had supposedly granted romantic success every year for nineteen years.

Whether the record reached twenty years in a row would end up resting on the two of us---Iwama and me.

I decided I would absolutely keep it a secret from Mizasaki that we'd gotten such a clear view of the heart.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 2
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 2\\
    An Equation Too Beautiful
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch2}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 2}
\vspace{1cm}

``Mizasaki-kun, thanks for yesterday! Because of you, I could see it properly---the Inome in the cherry blossoms!''

The morning after we'd gone to see the cherry blossoms, Iwama reported it first thing, so my attempt to keep it a secret was immediately thwarted.

``That's great! Then Iwama-san's year is secure.''

Saying that while striking a slightly cool pose, Mizasaki shot me a smirking look.

I pretended not to know. It wasn't like Iwama had said she saw it together with me.

``Look, this is the photo, and\dots''

Noticing Iwama trying to show Mizasaki her phone, I couldn't help pressing my fist to my forehead.

``Huh? There are two hands in this---did you have three arms, Iwama-san?''

``No. This one is Izuta-kun's hand.''

There was still time before first period began. I stood up from my seat to go to the restroom.

After washing my hands thoroughly and staring blankly for a moment at the dull, overcast scene outside the window, I went back to the classroom. Mizasaki was already back in his seat. The moment he spotted me at the doorway, he gave me a big grin and a thumbs-up.

What a shameless guy. After pulling a stunt that practically arranged for Iwama and me to meet, he had the nerve to talk to Iwama with a face like he had nothing to do with it. He had guts, I'd give him that.

Avoiding looking at Iwama's face, I sat down. Unfortunately, class still hadn't started.

``Izuta-kun\dots''

At the small voice calling out to me, I turned around to the seat behind me. Like it or not, that meant I was going to look at Iwama's face.

Iwama's cheeks were, somehow, faintly flushed. It didn't look like embarrassment so much as an awkward expression born of guilt. Seriously.

``Um, sorry. I really didn't know at all, but\dots''

It would've been better if she'd stayed not knowing, I thought, silently cursing Mizasaki. Judging by how Iwama looked, Mizasaki must have told her about the romance-success version of the legend.

``Apparently those cherry blossoms also had a tradition tied to romance, and\dots\ I didn't know anything about that, and I invited you, Izuta-kun, and\dots\ when I really thought about it, the platform was totally heart-shaped, too\dots''

Even without thinking hard, it was heart-shaped---but yesterday, it had been an Inome.

``I-it's fine. Don't worry about it.''

I said it while keeping my tone as calm as possible. No---actually, I really was mostly calm. I'd already gotten all the flustered feelings out of my system last night, the way Iwama was doing now.

I couldn't exactly laugh at ``cherry-blossom-colored brains.''

Last night, I'd been half sleep-deprived from an internal reflection meeting just as stormy as the weather outside---thinking I didn't need to let my plant-nerd self show that much, or that forcing near-trash katakuri on her had been a mistake.

But I replied in an even, flat voice.

``It was an interesting experience, and we got to see a beautiful view. I'm grateful to you, Iwama-san.''

``Really? Then\dots\ I'm glad, but\dots''

As Iwama made a face that looked like embarrassment and guilt mixed together, I said,

``No matter who says what, you don't need to worry about it. Legends are just other people's words, in the end.''

``\dots Yeah. Maybe so.''

I noticed Iwama, for some reason, faltered over her words. It sounded like the kind of phrasing you used when you wanted to agree, but avoided it for religious reasons.

At the very least, it was clear that, for Iwama, yesterday's cherry blossoms had become a touchy subject.

``What was first period again?''

I asked, anyway, just to change the subject.

``H-Homeroom. Picking class committee members.''

Maybe she felt relieved that we didn't have to talk about the cherry blossoms. Iwama recovered her usual smile. For us, small talk like this---with nothing sharp or risky about it---was probably far more appropriate than conversations like, ``Yesterday's cherry blossoms were pretty,'' or ``Let's go again.''

``Committee selection, huh. I hadn't thought at all about what committee I'd pick.''

``Huh, you hadn't?''

``Well, I guess I can take whatever's left. What about you, Iwama-san?''

``Me? Hmm, I've been wondering what I should do too---''

I was surprised, but before I could put it into words, our homeroom teacher walked in.

Once HR actually started, Iwama ran for class representative with almost no hesitation. In a classroom where not a single person volunteered for the first few seconds, when I heard a ``Yes!'' from the back, it gave me this sense of, well---yeah, of course that's how it would go.

Iwama was chosen as class representative with unanimous applause. Borrowing Mizasaki's expression, she was the kind of person who looked like the personification of a class representative. It was the natural outcome.

For the other class representative, a good-looking guy with a face that seemed like he played soccer volunteered next, and since no one else raised their hand, that was also decided unanimously. After that, replacing the teacher, the two of them stood at the front of the room and ran the rest of the committee selection.

It really did feel like the front of the classroom suited Iwama more than the seat closest to the exit.

I'd been staring blankly at the blackboard when it was decided I wouldn't have any role. I'd thought I could take on any committee that no one raised a hand for, but this was an academic prep school---every slot was filled by volunteers.

The committee selection wrapped up without a hitch, and the whole class applauded. As if in response, sunlight streamed in and lit up the names lined across the blackboard. On the far side of the teacher's desk, Iwama narrowed her eyes, looking dazzled.

``Yo, Delta. You look kind of lonely.''

The next day at lunch, Mizasaki plopped himself down into Iwama's seat. He started spreading out his bento there.

``Finished scouting the other classes?''

``Yeah, all six classes, more or less. Need introductions to any cute girls?''

``With Mizasaki around, I've got plenty.''

``Oh, come on.''

Wondering why I had to be having this kind of conversation at all, I was picking up a mini tomato when I noticed Mizasaki looking toward the front of the classroom.

The owner of the seat Mizasaki was sitting in was currently eating lunch with a girl with the unusual surname Kannabi. Since her attendance number came right after Iwama's, her seat was in the very front row. Recently, Iwama and Kannabi had been together more often. Kannabi was a small girl wrapped in a dark aura that kept people away. Ever since enrollment, she'd looked like she was always alone. Maybe Iwama was looking out for her, thinking it wouldn't be good to leave things like that.

``Yeah, I guess girls stick with girls. Can't be helped.''

Mizasaki sighed, apparently arriving at that conclusion on his own.

``I don't really care about getting friendly with girls.''

``Hey, don't play tough. You were just starting to get along, weren't you? You even casually got a two-shot with Iwama-san.''

``Just the hands.''

``Well, whatever. Sometimes you've just got to accept it. Guys like us---science nerds---and a popular person like Iwama-san\dots\ we live in different worlds, that's how it is.''

Listen to what I'm saying.

There were a lot of things I wanted to explain, but it started to feel like too much trouble. If I said Iwama was actually the same type---a fellow science enthusiast---Mizasaki would start using his Gamma-Delta logic to preach destiny at me.

``So in the end, it's the same as middle school. Let's just be damp and clingy together, guy to guy.''

I'd expected Mizasaki to nod happily, but instead he stopped his chopsticks, looking like he was thinking.

``What's wrong?''

``No\dots\ I think we should seriously start thinking about it.''

Mizasaki said it in a grave tone, like he was about to bring up a breakup. Just to be clear, we were absolutely not in that kind of relationship. And when Mizasaki used a grave tone, it meant he was joking.

``Thinking about what---an allosteric effect?''

``No, we're not talking enzyme reactions right now. Something more important. What we're going to do from here on out.''

Still not dropping the overly serious expression, Mizasaki continued.

``I'm not opposed to being damp together. But how we're damp together matters, right?''

After a moment, I realized what he was getting at.

``Picking a club.''

``Of course. We came to Tsunanagai High School---the prefecture's top academic prep school, and a mecca for the sciences. I hear there isn't just one science club---there are several. Isn't that the perfect stage for us to have a damp youth? We can't afford to mess up our club choice.''

Tsunanagai High School had traditionally put effort into science-track education. The reason Mizasaki and I chose this school wasn't just that it was close to home and had high academic standing---though those points mattered a lot.

At this school, tough customers aiming for the sciences gathered from all over the prefecture.

Iwama was probably the same. One of the tough customers who'd assembled in search of other tough customers.

What club was Iwama planning to join?---I shook the thought out of my head.

``What clubs are there?''

``It should be on the school's site\dots\ Don't worry, I'll use a proper primary source.''

Mizasaki pulled out his phone and started looking.

``For science-y ones, there's the Physics Club, Chemistry Club, Biology Club\dots\ and then the PC Club, I guess? No, the PC Club's a bit different. It's less `information' and more games, illustrations, and videos.''

``Then we should at least check out the Chemistry Club and Biology Club.''

``Right. Then that's next week.''

And the following Monday, after we'd finished visiting the clubs, the two of us ended up tilting our heads in the caf\'e we stopped by after school.

``Wasn't today's welcome event kind of\dots\ weird?''

I nodded at Mizasaki's point. There really were a few things that felt off.

To decide what club to join, it was absolutely necessary to clear up the discomfort that had come up there.

A club could easily take up most of a high schooler's life outside of studying. We had to use our own clear eyes to judge which club suited us.

To fully explain this matter, I needed to look back over what happened that day from start to finish.

First, I'll write down the conversation we had that morning---

On Monday, when I arrived at school, I noticed the classroom was filled with an atmosphere that was slightly different than usual. A restless, unsettled buzz hung in the air.

``Today's the lifting day for welcome events. Everyone's talking about clubs.''

Before first period began, Iwama came back from over by Kannabi and explained why.

``Welcome events'' was short for new-student welcome activities. Some also argued it was written as ``new recruitment,'' emphasizing the act of scouting, but in short, it was the time when every club competed to gather new members.

``I see. No wonder the atmosphere's so giddy.''

I remembered that today was a special day of morning-only classes. I'd thought that meant no bento was needed, but last night Mizasaki had messaged me: ``We're picking clubs in the afternoon! Bring a bento.''

No---wait. I felt like I'd just heard a slightly odd word.

``\dots Lifting day? There's a rule?''

``It kind of sounds like Beaujolais Nouveau, right? I only heard this earlier too, but apparently it's a school rule that you can't do welcome events until one p.m. today.''

For a moment, it sounded like Iwama had used another academic term I didn't know, but it was nothing of the sort---it was just about wine, where a release day is set by French law.

``Why would they have a rule like that?''

``Who knows\dots\ Izuta-kun, have you decided which clubs you're going to check out?''

``For now, I figured I'd look at the science-track clubs. Like the chemistry club, or the biology club. What about you, Iwama-san?''

``I'm thinking I'll go try out the basketball club! A friend from middle school invited me.''

She said it with such an excited expression that, for a moment, I wondered if I was the one misremembering. Iwama should've been in the science club in middle school, and hadn't she liked science enough that we'd accidentally hit it off?

After agonizing over how to phrase my doubt, I opened my mouth with a hesitant mumble.

``You'll look at the science-track clubs\dots\ on a different day?''

There was a brief pause.

``\dots I'm not sure yet. I still haven't decided.''

``So you're not really thinking about cultural clubs?''

``No, I'm definitely interested in science clubs too, but\dots\ I can't bring myself to give up sports either. A youth where you throw yourself into sports sounds fun too, right!''

It was a vague way of putting it. But Iwama probably had her reasons. If she wanted to focus on sports, it was bound to be hard to also join a science club at this school.

After thinking it through, I realized how arrogant I was being. Whatever club Iwama joined had nothing to do with me.

``Sports, huh\dots\ Yeah, that might suit you, Iwama-san.''

Words I hadn't even meant to say slipped out. Maybe it was a foolish ritual of sorts---something to make myself accept that this person and I had different interests and aptitudes, and lived in different worlds.

Still, ``suits you'' was a fair description. A basketball club sounded like it would fit a bright, energetic honor student perfectly.

Iwama didn't reply. Using the act of pulling out my textbook as an excuse, I turned back to face forward.

The reason there was a specific lifting time for welcome events became clear once morning classes ended, short HR was finished too, and the classroom clock pointed to exactly one o'clock.

From the speaker above the clock came a brief pop of noise---like someone had flipped a switch.

【This is to announce that it is now 1:00 p.m., Monday, April 15. Welcome events are now permitted. Everyone, please be sure to act with restraint, and conduct clean, proper recruitment activities---】

Along with that strange announcement, messy, pounding footsteps came thundering down from the floor above. The first-years' classrooms were on the second floor, the second-years' on the third, and the third-years' on the fourth. The upperclassmen had started moving all at once.

From the footsteps punching through the ceiling, I couldn't sense even a speck of restraint or clean, proper atmosphere.

``Hey, Delta---don't you feel like something's off here?''

Mizasaki, who had been about to sit in Iwama's seat and spread out his bento, said that and hurriedly retied the furoshiki he'd been in the middle of untying. Iwama had probably gone to meet up with friends from other classes. She was already gone from the room.

``It sounds like a zoo with the cages opened.''

I shoved my bento back into my bag too. I judged it would be better to be in a state where we could flee at any moment.

And our judgment was accurate.

The sound of a great animal march came closer like a tsunami, and in no time at all, it reached Class 1-C's classroom.

``Pardon!''

Maybe I was mishearing, but it was probably ``Pardon''---a thick, booming male voice rang out from the hallway.

Right behind Mizasaki, the sliding door was flung open with such force it nearly broke. Without a pause, the door at the front of the classroom was thrown open too. Even though I sprang to my feet, feeling my life was in danger, it was already too late---there was nowhere for us to run.

``Anyone wanna work out? Anyone wanna sweat fresh, manly sweat with rugby?''

``We're doing a band performance right after this! If you're interested, come to the audiovisual room!''

``The snack club's tasting event is happening now! Any kids who want delicious cookies, come with us!''

The upperclassmen poured into the classroom, each shouting whatever they pleased. Then, like a surfactant, they surrounded the helpless first-years and launched into relentless recruitment.

``Hey, hey, hey---this is totally like namahage.''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note (Namahage):} Namahage are ogre-like masked visitors from Akita folklore who barge into homes around New Year, loudly calling out ``any lazy ones?'' In this scene, it's a joke comparing the upperclassmen swarming first-years for club sign-ups to that kind of loud, inescapable ``inspection.''}

Mizasaki's description was pretty much spot-on. I felt like there was even a candy-house witch mixed in. Once you ate the cookies, that was it---they wouldn't let you go until you filled out a club application.

``Mizasaki, we're running.''

``Got it!''

We exchanged glances and slung our bags over our shoulders.

It helped that we'd been sitting near the exit. The area along the wall was a blind spot for the upperclassmen rushing into the classroom. In the brief moment when the flow cut off, we slipped out smoothly.

But---

``Wait\dots\ you've got to be kidding\dots''

Mizasaki stopped. The sight over his shoulder left me stunned too.

The hallway was packed solid with upperclassmen. Not just in uniforms---some were in gi, others in sports uniforms. Predators in every color.

``Oh! You guys look like you've got stamina! Wanna join the swim team?''

Men with racing goggles perched on their foreheads for no particular reason pounced on us right away. Even though it was spring, they were all nicely tanned, their skin gleaming.

``Ah---uh, I actually can't swim\dots''

When Mizasaki tried to dodge with a convenient lie, a woman popped out from behind the men.

``It's fine, it's fine! I started out not being able to swim either!''

``We love beginners too. Honestly, wouldn't it be better to learn how to swim while you're in high school?''

Was there a manual or something? The arguments came instantly, neatly organized, steadily sealing off every escape route. We were surrounded and driven right up against the wall.

In times like this, I didn't know how to fight. The talkative Mizasaki resisted as best he could.

``He and I don't really seem like sports-club types, and we want to live calmly without competing, you know?''

``It's not just competitive swimming! We actually do synchronized swimming for the school festival, and lots of people spend their youth doing that without competing!''

``S-synchronized\dots\ swimming?''

Even Mizasaki was getting overwhelmed.

All the sunbaked swim team members wore bright smiles that were hard to brush off. And since we were physically boxed in, we couldn't even do the usual thing of bowing our heads and backing away. We were done for.

For a moment, the thought crossed my mind: It's a pain---maybe we should just look around a little, then escape. But that was probably exactly what they wanted. If they could maneuver this well in the hallway, then wherever they ``escorted'' us would surely have traps stacked two or three layers deep.

``Ah---uh---we actually have a condition where we swell up dozens of times over if we get in the water\dots''

Mizasaki had finally started saying nonsense. I kept my \textit{What are you, super-absorbent polymer?} retort to myself.

``Oh, nice! That sounds useful! Come on!''

The swim team's reply made no sense either, but one thing was clear: no matter what we said, they had no intention of letting us go.

All around us, I could see plenty of similar victims. Some of them gave up and were led away. I had no reason to believe Mizasaki or I had any more fighting power than they did.

And yet.

Just as I was about to give up halfway, she arrived.

It was like magic. Without stopping even for an instant, she cut through the crowded hallway of upperclassmen. She nudged aside the elbow of a broad-shouldered swim team member as if she were brushing it away, then stepped right in front of us.

``Delta-kun. Mizasaki-kun. Do you have a moment?''

At the low voice that rang out as if the commotion didn't exist, Mizasaki, I, and the swim team members all froze.

It was our classmate, Mikage Aya.

Mizasaki's assessment was: ``Iwama-san is the sun, and Mikage-san is the moon.''

I couldn't say I didn't get what he meant. She was tall, and carried herself like someone grown. Even with a quiet air about her, her narrow eyes held a dignified beauty. In Mizasaki's words: a cool beauty.

Her long, silky black hair was tied back at the nape of her neck, giving her the air of a female samurai. It was easy to imagine her drawing a Japanese sword and cutting down villains left and right.

Since her attendance number put her seat right in front of Mizasaki's, I'd had a few chances to talk to her before. That was why she called me ``Delta-kun.''

There were even times when she'd joined our conversation from the other side while we were debating the Michaelis--Menten equation. Apparently, she liked math.

By the way, neither Mizasaki nor I was good at math.

After rescuing us from the swim team members' clutches, Mikage slipped through the hallway where the upperclassmen ran rampant and led us to the science building, where there were fewer people.

\fullpageimage{3.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}

It was hard to believe, but once I watched closely, I understood the trick. She was instantly calculating the path she needed from people's movements, then applying the bare minimum force to manipulate the flow and create space for her body to pass through. In the sense of using the other person's force while moving them, it was closer to aikido.

Mizasaki and I only had to follow the path Mikage took.

On the second floor of the science building, there was a neat lounge area. The walls and floor were wood-paneled, with wooden tables and chairs set out. Since the second floor of the science building also connected to the gym, it was probably a break spot for students involved in activities there.

Invited by Mikage, we ended up eating lunch there.

Even though she'd said, ``Do you have a moment?'' it didn't seem like Mikage had any particular business with us. She'd pretended she did in order to rescue us from the swim team.

I was deeply grateful---but it left the question of why she'd done it.

``Man, Mikage-san, seriously, thanks. I never thought we'd get jumped by kappas in the hallway. You saved our lives.''

Mizasaki's thanks were met only with Mikage's quiet, faint smile.

She wasn't the talkative type. Silently, she opened her school bag and took out a convenience-store plastic shopping bag. The sight nearly made me flash back to the katakuri, and I looked away.

Mikage opened a paper carton of unsweetened iced tea, stuck in a straw, and started chugging it down. It was such a bold way of drinking that it was almost refreshing to watch. Mizasaki and I laid out our own lunches.

Mine was my usual mini-tomato bento. Mizasaki had a nori bento---nine-tenths of it was white rice packed into a big plastic container. ``In the end, carbs give you the most satisfaction,'' he said. Apparently it was homemade by his mom.

What Mikage pulled from the plastic bag was a pack of temaki sushi. Tuna mayo. It might've been the first time I'd ever seen someone buy that for lunch instead of an onigiri.

``Still, those upperclassmen had insane energy.''

Mizasaki said it with his mouth still full of nori bento. Swallow before you talk.

``Energy, sure\dots\ but wasn't it closer to hunger and thirst?''

At my remark, Mikage nodded.

``I heard the activity restrictions were lifted last year, and club activities are only just getting back to full strength. Apparently every club is desperate to secure members. Increasing membership is necessary to energize a club.''

She spoke in a flat, even tone, without showing emotion.

I hadn't known this school had that kind of situation. Even Mizasaki, who was usually well-informed, nodded along in agreement.

``Ahh, makes sense. That level of desperation made me think they were kappas or carnivores, but\dots\ basically, they were trying to stock up on nutrition before breeding season, huh? If Mikage-san hadn't been there, we would've seriously gotten eaten.''

It was, as always, a hard-to-follow metaphor. Chewing her temaki sushi, Mikage responded again with only a faint smile.

While I was thinking that Mizasaki was really steering the conversation in a direction that emphasized gratitude toward Mikage, Mizasaki suddenly clapped his hands.

``Oh! Hey, Mikage-san---since you saved us and all, why don't we go around and check out the welcome events together?''

So that was it. Whether it was Iwama or Mikage, when it came to girls, Mizasaki showed a level of aggressiveness that was hard to comprehend.

I'd expected Mikage to find it bothersome, but instead she nodded, almost as if she'd been waiting for the invitation.

``Of course. I was just thinking it'd be boring alone.''

She didn't look like the type to think that, but it wasn't a bad idea. With Mikage's way of moving---after she'd cut through that hellish hallway---she seemed like she could help us avoid any unwanted ``attacks'' from upperclassmen too.

``Alright! So, Mikage-san, which clubs are you thinking of checking out?''

``The Physics Club. I'm actually interested in mathematics, but I heard the Math Club was absorbed into the Physics Club a long time ago.''

``Physics Club, huh\dots\ yeah, that works, Delta! Let's go check it out! I was just thinking we should peek at the science clubs anyway.''

Mizasaki and I had our own reasons from middle school for having a bit of an aversion to physics, but just looking in on the Physics Club wasn't a problem. I nodded.

``So you two are interested in the Chemistry Club after all, Mizasaki-kun?''

We'd told Mikage that we were formerly in the chemistry club. Mizasaki swallowed the huge amount of white rice he'd been chewing.

``Yeah, it's on the list. Can't really say anything until we see it, though.''

Mizasaki turned toward me.

``For now, let's just look around at a bunch of stuff today and think about which club suits us.''

``Yeah.''

There was one thing I was curious about.

``Mikage-san\dots\ do you happen to have a sibling at this school?''

After finishing the last bite of her tuna-mayo temaki sushi, Mikage looked at me with interest.

``Why do you think that?''

The fact that she didn't deny it meant my guess wasn't far off.

``You rescued us from the upperclassmen even though you didn't get anything out of it. I tried thinking about why.''

``Why? Because we're classmates, obviously. We've discussed enzyme reactions together.''

``Is that really all?''

Sure, we'd chatted a bit about enzyme reaction rates and the Michaelis--Menten equation. But just because two male classmates like us were about to get hauled away by the swim team---would this quiet girl really push through a crowd to rescue us? There had to be some other reason.

Once I started thinking that way, something came to mind.

``You knew a lot about this school's clubs. That the activity restrictions were lifted last year, and that the Math Club was absorbed into the Physics Club---those aren't things most new students would know. But if you had a sibling here, or an older acquaintance, that would explain it.''

Mikage nodded as if acknowledging it. I continued.

``So here's my hypothesis. Mikage-san has a connection in the Physics Club. And the Physics Club, too, desperately wants members. You were asked by that person to bring someone in. That's why you rescued us---because you knew we were science-track---and then you could steer us straight into the Physics Club's welcome event. It's a much smarter way than dragging people off by force.''

A brief silence. Then Mikage spoke without changing her expression.

``Exactly right.''

So I was right. It wasn't often that anyone showed me kindness for free.

``Just as you said, Delta-kun. My oni---''

Mikage suddenly cut herself off and gave a throat-clearing cough, as if she'd choked. Oni?

``My onigiri was something my older brother bought this morning at the convenience store, and that brother is a third-year here. He's the president of the Physics Club. He knew this year's welcome events would be a fierce competition, so he asked me to rescue you and Mizasaki-kun, then bring you along to the Physics Club afterward.''

Mizasaki's and my eyes naturally dropped to Mikage's hands. What she was about to open wasn't an onigiri---it was a second temaki sushi. This time it was negitoro.

Mikage, smiling as if nothing mattered, peeled the packaging with practiced hands, forcibly bent the cylinder-shaped rice into a rounded shape, then wrapped the lump in a separate sheet of seaweed.

\dots\ Right. It was, indeed, an onigiri. We decided we hadn't seen anything.

``Wow---so that's what it was. You got us good.''

Mizasaki said it, sounding amused.

``Fine, we'll go look at the Physics Club. But whether we join is a different story. We were in the chemistry club in middle school, and for classes we're planning to choose biology and chemistry, not physics and chemistry. Physics just doesn't really fit us.''

In high school, you had to choose science subjects---if you were science-track, you usually picked two from physics, chemistry, biology, and earth science. Physics and chemistry were the overwhelming majority at roughly three-quarters, but chemistry and biology still made up about a quarter, and that was what Mizasaki and I were considering. There were also a handful of unusual types who picked physics and biology, and a very small number who chose the unpopular earth science.

Mizasaki checked with Mikage, looking a little apologetic.

``So\dots\ even if we don't join the Physics Club, you won't hold it against us, right?''

``Of course. If you come take a look, I think my brother will be satisfied.''

``Alright! It's settled! This is starting to sound fun!''

While I was picking up mini tomatoes, the conversation kept moving forward. Mizasaki looked ecstatic that things were headed toward him doing welcome-event rounds with a beautiful woman. If he were a peacock, he'd probably be spreading his tail feathers to their fullest right about now.

After finishing her negitoro onigiri, Mikage suddenly made a suggestion, as if something had occurred to her.

``Why don't we go see the Chemistry Club first? They should be doing their welcome event in the chemistry lab just past that corner.''

And so, after we finished lunch, we headed for the chemistry lab.

``Even if my attitude changes suddenly, I'd like you not to be surprised.''

Right before opening the chemistry lab door, Mikage gave us a meaningful warning.

The Chemistry Club's welcome event was livelier than I'd expected. Unlike my middle school chemistry club, where a small handful of us---including Mizasaki and me---had quietly done experiments, here there were lots of students chatting in the chemistry lab. As expected of Tsunanagai High School.

We could also see new students getting excited as they watched a demonstration where the color of a BTB solution changed. It was an indicator that turned yellow in acidic conditions, blue in basic conditions, and green in neutral. But since its transition range is specifically suited for near-neutral pH, it's often used to show biological respiration rather than precise titration measurements. I didn't really understand why they were playing around changing colors.

``Oh! The newbies are here!''

A broad-shouldered upperclassman spotted us the moment we hesitated near the entrance. He held out a chart drawn on loose-leaf paper with ruler-straight lines.

``Write your class and name here. I'll show you around.''

After Mizasaki and I wrote our names, Mikage wrote hers last. The flowing handwriting matched her aura perfectly. Maybe it was a habit---she even added a period at the end of her name.

The broad-shouldered upperclassman confirmed the three names, then widened his eyes.

``Mikage Aya? Mikage\dots\ as in that Mikage?''

He asked with exaggerated emphasis, staring hard at Mikage.

``Presumably, I'm that Mikage.''

``Well, I'll be damned. I thought he was just a siscon, but you're actually seriously cute.''

What a crude guy.

Mizasaki was the same, but saying someone is beautiful or cute right in front of them was unacceptable. He probably thought he was complimenting her, but wasn't the act of voicing and judging beauty inherently rude?

Even after being called cute, Mikage didn't change her expression in the slightest, only giving a small bow.

``If it's Akki's younger sister, Indie's a better fit than me\dots\ hey!''

The one he called over wasn't a whip-carrying archaeologist---she was a bright blond girl.

Her makeup was flawless, and she was the kind of person the word gyaru suited perfectly. There was a little heart stitched onto the collar of her lab coat, which she wore loose and stylishly unbuttoned. Flashy, over-the-top nails---completely unlike a chemistry club---glittered at her fingertips. It must have been hard to put on gloves.

``Wahwah! Aya-chii! You actually came!''

Mikage was shockingly unreactive to the gyaru rushing over. Instead, Mizasaki reacted.

``Hongo-senpai!''

So they knew each other. I felt like I'd seen her face somewhere too, but I couldn't remember where.

``Oh! Mizacchi too! Welcome, welcome!''

When I asked with my eyes if he'd met her before, Mizasaki told me in a small voice.

``Before the entrance ceremony, remember that little guidance session? After the textbook sale. She taught me a bunch of stuff then\dots\ like this school's normie rate, and the cherry blossom rumor.''

You go and ask about the most pointless things.

During that guidance session, I'd been near Mizasaki too. I didn't think I'd spoken to Hongo-senpai directly, but maybe I'd seen her there.

Mizasaki puffed out his chest and proudly introduced me.

``Hongo-senpai, this is Delta.''

``Hm? Delta?''

``Yep yep. Some people call him Izuta, but his real name is Del---''

``I'm Izuta Sho.''

I cut Mizasaki off and introduced myself.

``I see. Then you're Delchi!''

Listen to what I'm saying.

It wasn't like you could just slap ``chi'' onto any name. If you did, you'd end up calling the Mohorovi\v{c}i\'{c} discontinuity---the Moho---the ``Mohorovi\v{c}i\'{c}-chi.'' And more importantly, there was no ``del'' sound anywhere in my name. Still, there was no point in protesting. I just bowed my head.

The gyaru in the lab coat placed a hand on her chest, looking utterly confident.

``I'm Hongo Nagisa. And yes, I'm the Chemistry Club President! Sayacchi, thanks---after this, I'll do the tour, so take care of those kids over there.''

The senpai holding the roster answered, ``Yeah!'' and went back to dealing with more first-years.

Led by Hongo-senpai, the three of us headed for one of the long lab tables. On the way, Mizasaki asked her something.

``Senpai, why do people call you Indie?''

``Only in the Chemistry Club. A senpai started calling me that as a joke.''

Just as ``Delchi'' didn't fit Izuta Sho, the name Hongo Nagisa didn't feel particularly ``Indie,'' either. While I was wondering why she had that nickname, Mizasaki pointed it out instead.

``But senpai, why Indie?''

``It used to be `indicator,' then it got shortened to Indie. It only makes sense in the chemistry club, though.''

She stopped. The black lab bench top was cluttered. Beakers and mortars, and---had someone caught a cold?---even gargle solution and vitamin C powder. And what looked like Hongo-senpai's pencil case. I could tell because it was decorated to an absurd degree, covered in countless pin badges and a mountain of straps.

As we sat down, Mizasaki asked me:

``What does `indicator' mean?''

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Notes:} In chemistry, an indicator is a substance that changes color to show a reaction's state. It's like a visual sensor.}

``Isn't it `display'? I think `indicate' comes from the same root as `index'---as in index finger---meaning to point something out. It's the part of a device that points to a scale and shows the value.''

``Got it\dots\ a display\dots''

But ``display'' didn't really make sense here. Maybe she'd gotten the nickname because her expressions changed a lot and she was easy to read.

``Now then---since you're interested in the Chemistry Club, let me show you something fun right away.''

Hongo-senpai pulled a plastic bottle of barley tea from a bag on the floor. Smiling sweetly, she set it on the table with exaggeratedly careful hands.

``There's tea here.''

``Is this a magic trick?''

``Yup, Mizacchi. If you figure out the trick, I'll give you something niiiice.''

With a wink and a sultry tone, Hongo-senpai gestured toward the barley tea.

``Alright then---let's make this tea disappear!''

I see. A battle of wits. This sounded fairly interesting.

Mizasaki and I nodded and looked at the plastic bottle. It was ordinary barley tea you'd buy at a convenience store. Senpai lifted it with the hands of a magician.

``Here we go\dots\ three, two, one---ta-da!''

She shook the bottle as if she were about to toss it, then thunk---set it down in front of us.

In an instant, the contents turned into colorless, transparent water.

``Tadaaa! The tea is gone!''

After staring at the bottle for a moment, Mizasaki clapped politely. I followed, not wanting to be rude. For some reason, Mikage was about to open a mathematics magazine. She had nerve to do that in front of senpai, but she apparently wasn't interested in the trick at all.

``So? Figure it out?''

As senpai asked, Mizasaki prompted me with his eyes. ``You explain,'' apparently.

The trick was obvious. Betting the pride of a former chemistry club member, I stated it plainly.

``What was in that bottle wasn't barley tea to begin with.''

Hongo-senpai's eyes went wide in surprise.

``Oh? Why do you think that?''

``You played fair---you left the materials on the table. What looked like barley tea was gargle solution with iodine mixed into water.''

I pointed at the gargle-solution bottle on the table. It was a dark brown liquid with iodine dissolved as povidone-iodine, but if you diluted it, it would match the color of bottled barley tea.

``When iodine (I\textsubscript{2}) is reduced by a reducing agent like ascorbic acid, it becomes colorless iodide ions (I\textsuperscript{-}). As the reducing agent, vitamin C powder was probably taped to the inside of the cap or something. By shaking the bottle hard, the vitamin C dissolved into the liquid, and the redox reaction turned the solution clear.''

The vitamin C powder was also sitting on the table. If she'd truly wanted to perform a magic trick, she would've hidden both the gargle solution and the vitamin C. She was testing our level.

\dots\ Or you could also argue Hongo-senpai was simply careless.

``Whoa---amazing. Dead on!''

Hongo-senpai beamed and gave me a big round of applause.

``How do you know that much? Did you do chemistry in middle school too?''

``Huh? Didn't we say so? Delta and I were in the chemistry club in middle school.''

``Oh, really? That explains it. No wonder you know your stuff.''

Laughing, senpai poured the mixed gargle solution and vitamin C down the sink.

``By the way, senpai---what was that `something nice' you were going to give us?''

Mizasaki aimed a sleazy look of anticipation at her. Senpai stuck out her tongue with a teehee.

``I didn't think you'd actually get it right, so I didn't decide anything!''

So she'd been underestimating us, plain and simple.

And it seemed leaving the clues on the table really had just been carelessness.

``Oh! I know---I'll give you that. Come on.''

Hongo-senpai stood and started walking toward the back of the room. We followed, but Mikage stayed glued to the magazine she'd spread out on the table, showing no sign of moving. Maybe she and senpai were close enough that she didn't have to be polite, but even so, it felt a bit too cold.

I remembered what she'd told us before we entered the chemistry lab: her attitude would change suddenly. So this was what she meant.

Just as she'd advised, I decided not to dwell on it.

Senpai opened a small refrigerator and took out something packed in a silver pouch. Inside was a small plastic spray bottle, filled with a clear liquid with a faint yellow tint.

``This is luminol reagent---the chemiluminescent stuff! It's the one that glows blue when it reacts with an oxidant, using the iron in blood as a catalyst. You know it?''

We nodded. We'd even run the experiment before.

``I mixed it up today for the welcome event, but then I realized it's hard to use unless the room's dark, and if we make it dark we can't do the welcome event, and I made way too much so there's no way we'll use it all\dots\ It should last about a week in the fridge, so here!''

I would've been happy to accept it, but I had a concern.

``We're planning to check out other clubs after this. We'll be out late, so it might go bad while we're gone.''

``Ahh, right! Totally. Sorry! It's kinda like\dots\ you find a delicious-looking snack, but it has to be refrigerated, so you give up on gifting it to your boyfriend?''

``Oh---senpai, you have a boyfriend?''

Mizasaki immediately latched onto the least relevant part.

``I do. He's super handsome, and I'm totally in love!''

``I see\dots\ that's a shame\dots''

Do not go for this gyaru. She is absolutely not for you.

``What, Mizacchi---were you aiming for me?''

``Haha---caught me?''

``Sorry, you'll have to give up. I love my boyfriend. We're totally cross-coupling like crazy---and no, I don't mean the Suzuki--Miyaura kind!''

``Y-You're bonding!?''

``We might even form a double bond.''

``Even a $\pi$ bond!?''

Hey, you two---stop happily trampling chemistry terms.

I suddenly felt something cold at my back---like killing intent---and turned around. Mikage, who should have been absorbed in her mathematics magazine, was looking our way. But her face was as expressionless as ever. When our eyes met, she gave a faint smile. The killing intent was probably just my imagination.

When we returned to the table, senpai handed us some kind of booklet. It was a simple piece---B4 paper printed, folded in half, and stapled. On the cover were titles like ``The Evolution of Polymers---Ebiwaka Resin Factory Tour'' and ``The Miracle of Calcium Carbonate---Fujido Cave Tour.'' The cover was full color. Whether they'd commissioned it or not, the design was fairly polished.

``This isn't quite the same, but here---take this! Our chemistry club goes on trips pretty often, and we write a report every time. What I gave you is a curated selection of the best ones---plus I designed the covers myself, and they're my favorites!''

As expected of Tsunanagai High School---this gyaru didn't seem like an ordinary person.

At least, in terms of design sense.

I bowed and accepted it.

``Thank you. Is it okay if I read a bit of it here?''

``Of course! Take your time!''

Mizasaki and I sat down and flipped through the booklet. The factory-tour report I opened was from last year, written by a second-year named Sarasayama. She should be a third-year now. If so, she might have been Sayacchi---the one who greeted us at the entrance.

As I kept reading, my mood sank.

The content was flat, and the Japanese was oddly off in places. The parts I cared about most---the chemical reactions---looked like they'd been copied straight from the handouts; the numbering on the reaction equations even started midway, and the content never really got to the point.

Tsunanagai High School was supposed to put particular emphasis on science education. Even if students varied widely, it was still one of the prefecture's top academic schools. And this was what the chemistry club called ``especially well done''?

Mizasaki was wearing a grim look as he read the cave tour report. Thinking I might borrow another one if there were more, I glanced at Hongo-senpai---only to see her leaning over Mikage's hands.

``Hey, hey, Aya-chii---what're you doing?''

``I'm doing partial derivatives.''

``Ehh, what's that? Like\dots\ weird derivatives? Sounds hard!''

Senpai's voice was bright, but Mikage's was cold as ice. The temperature gap between them was so extreme the air looked like it might start convecting. It really did seem like there was something between the two of them.

Next to the open mathematics magazine, Mikage had placed a sheet of copy paper divided into two columns with a vertical line, and she was writing spell-like equations on it with a mechanical pencil. I could at least tell she was solving problems.

She was completely in study mode.

She didn't seem to care much about her belongings. She used a flat zippered mesh case---like something you'd buy at a hundred-yen shop---as her pencil case. The contents also looked minimal. Compared to senpai's pencil case---swollen and cluttered like a datemaki, covered in decorations---the contrast was almost comical.

``Heeey, Delchi---did my pi catch your attention?''

The sudden voice startled me. Beside me, Mizasaki's chair scraped loudly as he jolted.

``Hey, Delta---staring at a senpai's boobs when she's got a boyfriend is not a good idea!''

Getting hit with nonsense from two directions, I had no choice but to protest.

``What do you mean, boobs? I'm not interested in anything.''

Senpai picked up her own pencil case and showed me a strap.

Among the many colorful straps, one stood out. It looked handmade---cloisonné. On a black background, ``$\pi$'' was written in light blue.

I couldn't tell whether it was meant as pi, the circle constant, or $\pi$ electrons, but apparently this was the ``pi'' Hongo-senpai meant. It was true that, since I'd been looking at her pencil case, the ``$\pi$'' had caught my eye. Still, the way she'd put it was misleading.

Losing the will to argue, I prompted her instead.

``Um\dots\ could you introduce the chemistry club members now?''

Please forgive me for the slight edge in my tone.

``Sure! Come to think of it, I haven't yet. Sorry, sorry.''

Hongo-senpai spoke breezily as she made an OK sign beside her cheek.

Somehow, I felt like this chemistry club wasn't for me.

``How was it, Delta?''

After leaving the chemistry lab, we returned to where we'd eaten lunch. Mikage seemed to be at a critical point in her problem-solving; she walked over with the mathematics magazine, copy paper, and pencil case tucked together, sat beside us, and---after saying, ``Can I have one minute?''---immediately reopened the magazine and resumed calculating.

She'd looked uninterested in the chemistry club from the start, so Mizasaki and I continued the conversation on our own.

``Honestly? It was mediocre.''

According to Hongo-senpai, the chemistry club was one of the larger groups in the school---sports clubs included. It was very lively. That wasn't inherently bad, but it wasn't for us. Ideally, we wanted to spend our time doing experiments quietly and murmuring chemistry talk to each other.

``Yeah. I mean, it's Tsunanagai High School and all, so maybe we just expected too much\dots''

Mizasaki held up the booklet he'd received from Hongo-senpai.

``This cave report is basically just a field-trip impression. It barely says anything about chemistry. Like\dots\ you can see the lack of passion for chemistry bleeding through.''

``This resin plant one was the same. You'd be better off calling it an elementary-school field trip essay.''

``For real\dots? Senpai picked the good ones and handed them to us, right?''

``Maybe she absentmindedly grabbed the wrong one?''

Mikage suddenly looked up.

``That woman isn't the type to make that kind of mistake.''

It seemed she had finished solving the problem. Apparently it had been a proof problem, and at the end she had written ``Q.E.D.''

``I think it's either the best report in the Chemistry Club, or else that woman deliberately handed you a low-quality report.''

We froze for a moment at the unexpected remark. Not just the content, but the way Mikage calmly referred to senpai as ``that woman'' had a creepy, curse-like edge to it.

At times like this, it was always Mizasaki who found the words first.

``\dots\dots\dots\dots By the way, Mikage-san, how do you know Hongo-senpai?''

``Know her? I don't. I don't know that damned she-fox.''

It absolutely sounded like she knew her, but neither Mizasaki nor I was foolish enough to dig deeper here.

Mizasaki was so taken aback that the air had frozen, so I changed the subject.

``Mikage-san, what problem were you solving just now?''

It was an obvious topic change, but Mikage returned to her usual manner and smiled faintly.

``A simple proof problem involving complex exponential functions. It wasn't that advanced, but there were a lot of calculations, so it took me a bit longer than I expected. I wanted to finish while that woman was talking, so I'm sorry.''

For some reason, she apologized to me. The content from the math magazine and the formula-filled symbols lined up on the copy paper were completely beyond me, and it didn't look simple at all.

Once again, ``that woman.'' Maybe to steer things away, Mizasaki said in an oddly cheerful voice.

``Man, Mikage-san, you're cool! You really give off that math devotee vibe. Like, literally just paper and a pen, and you can kill time forever? That type?''

Mizasaki pointed at the copy paper and mechanical pencil.

``Maybe. By the way, I use copy paper because I can keep it tucked into the magazine---normally I use a plain notebook.''

``Woooow.''

Mizasaki looked impressed. Mikage was so rational it was almost numbing. Did that have something to do with liking math?

``And also, stationery---''

For some reason, Mikage held up a mesh case she used instead of a pencil case.

``Basically, it's a mechanical pencil and an eraser, and then a two-color pen---red and black. Red is for grading, and black is for letters and paperwork. If a class requires it, I'll bring a ruler or compass too.''

Inside really were just a mechanical pencil, spare lead, an eraser, and a two-color pen.

``That's insane. I think I keep my stationery pretty minimal too, but I use highlighters to make words stand out, and also a green marker for memorization. You know, the kind that turns black under a red sheet.''

``One of the good things about mathematics is, above all, that it is simple. Mathematics is beautiful because it is fully explained while remaining at the necessary minimum. So stationery should be necessary and sufficient as well---that's what my brother and I think. Using some color like you do, Mizasaki-kun, is rational, but I would never use a bunch of unnecessary colored pens or put annoying straps on a pencil case.''

It was obvious who she was talking about, but we did our best not to touch it.

The Physics Club can be last, Mikage told us, so next we decided to go check out another science club: the Biology Club.

``Ah, Izuta-kun!''

On the stairs down to the first floor, where the biology lab was, we happened to run into Iwama. She had probably changed in the first-floor locker room---she was in a brand-new gym uniform. The skin showing from her short sleeves and shorts was dazzling, and I answered while shifting my gaze slightly to the side.

``Iwama-san, are you on your way to the basketball club's welcome event?''

``Yeah. What about you, Izuta-kun?''

``We're going around checking out science clubs. We just saw the Chemistry Club, and next is the Biology Club.''

``Oh, I see! Hey, what was it like---''

Mid-sentence, Iwama closed her mouth. It looked like the brown-haired girl with the side ponytail behind her had nudged her, and she turned around. To the girl asking, ``Who are these people?'', Iwama answered briefly, ``Um\dots\ people from my class.''

The side-ponytail girl looked like she played sports, and she sized us up. This was probably the ``friend from middle school'' who had invited Iwama to the basketball club.

The two of them were giving off an overwhelming ``sun'' aura. I felt like I was being pressured. As ``people from Iwama's class,'' I didn't think we should hold her up with a long conversation.

``Sorry, we're blocking the stairs.''

``Huh---r-right, sorry! Our bad!''

``See you tomorrow.''

No one was trying to pass, but I hurried down to the first floor anyway.

As we parted, I saw Iwama give us a small wave.

Even so, I couldn't bring myself to wave back.

Dry footsteps echoed over the linoleum floor. The first floor of the science building was quiet and empty. Aside from us, there were only a few students like Iwama, already changed into gym uniforms, heading toward the stairs.

``Delta, did something happen with Iwama-san?''

While we were heading to the Biology Club, Mizasaki whispered some needless worries to me.

``It's nothing.''

There really was nothing. After Mizasaki said, ``I see,'' he lowered his voice even more.

``But Iwama-san is pretty slim---''

Before Mizasaki could finish, I smacked him on the head.

Mikage looked at us as if puzzled by our behavior, but we ignored it and headed for the biology lab.

The biology lab was directly below the chemistry lab. It felt like there was no one around, but on the whiteboard by the entrance, a color-printed poster reading ``Welcome to the Biology Club!'' was displayed. Photos of various creatures were nicely collaged: a Japanese pond turtle sunbathing, a hamster with puffed cheeks, a light-blue budgerigar, and a sea urchin eating cabbage.

But from the slit in the biology lab door, it was clear the lights were off.

``Hey, Mizasaki. Isn't anyone here?''

``Hm? But look, it says `welcome event.'''

Mizasaki pointed at the Biology Club schedule written on the whiteboard beside the poster.

\textbf{April 18 (Mon) Welcome events begin!}

``Yeah, it does---'' I started, and then a question crossed my mind. Was today the 18th?

Mizasaki seemed to be thinking the same thing, because he checked the date on his phone.

``No, today's Monday, but it's the 15th. This isn't this year's schedule.''

``Since this year is a leap year, that date is probably from the year before last.''

Mikage told us from behind. Mizasaki's eyes went wide.

``Wait, Mikage-san, that was fast!''

``It's not a difficult calculation. The remainder when you divide 365 by 7 is 1, so the day of the week shifts forward by one each year. In a leap year, you have February 29, so it shifts by two. Since it's shifted by three, this is from the year before last.''

Now that she said it, it wasn't some huge calculation---but being able to answer instantly and confidently was impressive.

Still, leaving the schedule not updated for two years was something else. People who wanted to know the club's schedule would be inconvenienced.

``Huh, but that's weird. This poster is for this year. It's printed with 2024.''

It was odd. When I tried touching the writing on the schedule, the black text didn't smudge at all.

``I see\dots\ Someone must have written it with a permanent marker, and nobody bothered to erase it.''

``Huh? You could wipe it off with acetone in one go.''

``Maybe it was too much trouble.''

Setting aside why a schedule that was supposed to be used for club activities had been left alone for two years, Mikage ignored us as we started to turn back and slid the biology lab door open.

As expected, there was no one inside. In the dim biology lab, the futile sounds echoed: a skittering rustle like a small animal moving, and splashing from a turtle thrashing around by the window.

``Excuse me.''

What answered Mikage's voice was a high-pitched, ``Hellooo\dots'' Then, from the same direction, came the flapping sound of wings, so it was clearly some kind of parakeet or parrot.

``Looks like the bird senpai is here,'' Mizasaki said with a wry smile. There wasn't a single human in sight.

``Doesn't look like they're doing a welcome event. Let's not,'' I said.

``Yeah,'' Mizasaki agreed.

Just as Mizasaki and I were about to leave, we heard a door open.

A man with thinning white hair poked his head out from the biology prep room. He was probably a teacher. He wore a blue outdoor-brand fleece over his dress shirt. The wrinkles etched into his face made him look strict.

``What do you want?''

A low voice questioned us. Mizasaki straightened his posture as he answered.

``Ah, um, we thought the Biology Club might be holding a welcome event\dots\ There was a poster on the whiteboard outside.''

Without a word, the teacher walked over to us. We hadn't done anything wrong, but I started to feel like we were about to be scolded. While I stood there nervously wondering what he was going to do, he simply flicked the switch on the wall right next to us. The lights came on.

If he'd told us, we could have turned them on ourselves.

``Come in. I'm Tokumura, a biology teacher and the Biology Club advisor. If you're interested in the Biology Club, I'll show you around today.''

Mikage bowed and said, ``Please,'' before we could even hesitate. Since Tokumura-sensei welcomed us in without so much as a millimeter of a smile, we had no choice but to go farther inside.

``Are there\dots\ no club members here?''

When Mizasaki asked, sensei turned back toward us expressionlessly.

``It seems they've already gone home for the day. I'm sorry---you came all this way. I thought perhaps they weren't going to do welcome events this year, but\dots''

It sounded like the members had no intention of running a welcome event. And then---

``That poster outside---did you make it, sensei?''

``No. The current third-years made it.''

So, at least in form, it seemed they did intend to do welcome events. Next, Mizasaki fired off another question.

``About how many members are there right now?''

``One.''

An awkward silence. What cut through it was a high voice.

``Looonely, huhhh.''

Whether it was a parakeet or a parrot, it was impeccable timing. Since we couldn't exactly laugh in front of a strict-looking sensei, I held it back with everything I had. Mizasaki failed and let out a weird little ``Hk---!''

``When they're not used to it, plenty of students end up bursting out laughing,'' Tokumura-sensei said without so much as twitching a facial muscle.

``I'm sorry you came all this way when nothing is prepared\dots\ but I can't very well send you home empty-handed. If you like, I'll introduce the animals.''

We followed as sensei walked toward the window. This time Mikage didn't open her math magazine; she came along with us.

Tokumura-sensei stopped first in front of a large tank. The water was shallow, and part of it had been built up into a land area with blocks of stone. A single Japanese pond turtle was looking at us from on top of it.

``This is a Japanese pond turtle named Tortoise Henry Huxley\dots\ female.''

He said it with such a serious face that I couldn't tell whether it was okay to laugh. Mizasaki froze with a strange expression somewhere between serious and amused---almost like he might cry. Mikage stayed blank-faced.

``What is it? It's not as though I named her myself.''

The atmosphere was so completely frozen that I spoke up.

``A student named her, I assume. If it's a Japanese pond turtle, wouldn't it be `turtle' rather than `tortoise'?''

``Tortoise'' certainly meant ``turtle'' in a broad sense, but more specifically, it referred to land turtles without webbed feet. Since Japanese pond turtles lived by the water, strictly speaking, it wasn't a tortoise.

Only now did Tokumura-sensei's expression finally shift. He looked slightly surprised.

``This turtle has been at this school for nearly forty years, and I've spent about twenty of those years here---but as far as I know, only three students, including you, have ever pointed out that error.''

It sounded like he was praising me, so I bowed my head. He was hard to read, but it seemed he had a kind side too---praising students even over trivial things.

``Come to think of it, I never asked your names.''

At sensei's prompting, Mizasaki, Mikage, and then I introduced ourselves.

When he heard my name, sensei nodded as if something clicked.

``I see---Izuta, is it? Unless I'm mistaken, I've met your father several times at academic conferences.''

Without thinking, I let out a small breath. I hated reactions like that.

``I see.''

``Are you aiming for the same path?''

``No, that's---''

While I hesitated, Tokumura-sensei stopped me with a hand.

``My apologies. That was a foolish thing to ask. There are plenty of people who reach university without having their path decided. You can take your time deciding after you've seen various roads.''

Then, as if nothing had happened, sensei went on to briefly introduce the animals.

A budgerigar named Rotgelb (why it had that name when it was light blue was a mystery).

Hamsters named Rokujo Kawahara and Jonouchi (why they had those names was a mystery).

A spotted gar named Gaako (I thought it was too on-the-nose, but it was male).

And then the unnamed mice, medaka, and guppies, plus the eerie assortment of hermit crabs, sea urchins, and starfish being raised in a large saltwater tank.

Sensei didn't bother introducing the Arabidopsis and succulents growing thickly on the rack.

``Is there anything you can show us about your past activities?''

Mikage asked, oddly proactive. Tokumura-sensei thought for a moment, then seemed to remember something and brought over a thick ring binder from the back of the classroom. We gathered around it across a desk.

A very handmade album. It looked like research materials. Dozens of clear pockets were densely bound into the ring binder. Several photos---apparently records of the Biology Club's activities---had been taped onto A4 drawing paper in small groups with masking tape, and those sheets were inserted into the pockets back-to-back. Around the photos were comments that the students had apparently written. For example---

Jonouchi keeps running. Is he running on the playground equipment, or is the playground equipment making him run?

The meaning was unclear.

``These were left behind by last year's graduates. I think you'll be able to see what things were like back when the activities were still going.''

Mizasaki riffled through the pages quickly.

In addition to photos taken in the biology lab, there were pictures from training camps, from river surveys, for some reason a shrine at night, and even tomatoes growing in a field that had been carved out of the garden in front of the biology lab.

``Besides indoor research, they actively did fieldwork. They published a club journal twice a year, in summer and winter, and at the cultural festival they displayed the animals and presented their research results as well.''

Noticing that everything Tokumura-sensei said was in the past tense, I felt a stab of loneliness.

Over the low, continuous hum of the pump, the hollow splashing of Tortoise Henry Huxley thrashing around by the window echoed pointlessly.

As if remembering something, sensei flipped the page and showed us a group photo.

``This is Karato. She's a third-year now, and the only remaining member at present.''

The person he pointed at was a girl who somehow gave off a listless vibe. Glasses, hunched shoulders, hair grown out into a messy mop, a belt end hanging down, and something dangling from her pocket.

I thought the conversation would develop from there, but sensei offered no further explanation. Instead, he spoke to us as we continued staring at the photo, expecting him to continue.

``If you're curious, the Biology Club has a website. Photos like these should be posted there as well, so I'd like you to use it as a reference.''

``Thank you.''

As we thanked him one after another, Tokumura-sensei said one last thing.

``I'm sorry it's in this state, but if you join, I'll support you as best I can. If you're willing, please consider it. If possible, I don't want the tradition to die out.''

After we left the biology lab, we were silent for a while.

The Chemistry Club was noisy to the point of excess, while the Biology Club was on the verge of extinction. You couldn't force students into clubs, so it was only natural that popular clubs would flourish and those that weren't would die out. Even so, I couldn't help feeling a pang.

``We're almost out of time, but will you come to the Physics Club too?''

A promise was a promise. At Mikage's words, we nodded.

The physics lab where the Physics Club was holding its welcome event was apparently on the third floor. As we climbed the stairs, Mikage must have sensed our reluctance, because she gave us a reassuring little smile.

``It's fine. Unlike that woman, my brother who bought me the onigiri is a very good person.''

He was Mikage's brother---the Physics Club President who had bought onigiri for Mikage. According to Mikage, he was Student Council President, second in the grade academically, exceptionally athletic, kind, loved math, and the most handsome man in the world.

But that wasn't what we were worried about.

The moment I opened the physics lab door, the source of that worry came into view.

The source of our worry recognized me too, and smirked maliciously.

``Yo, loser.''

The one who granted me that greeting was Watari Marejou, from the same middle school as me. Tall and muscular, with sharp, well-defined features. He'd dyed his long hair a much lighter brown than Mizasaki's. He looked like a delinquent.

He called me a loser because he had beaten me by just three points on the final in-school exam in middle school. His ugliness---still nagging about results from ages ago---made me sick, but since it was true that I'd lost, I couldn't talk back.

``What's wrong, Delta? What do math-weaklings like you guys want in the physics lab?''

``Math-weakling'' meant someone weak at math. He was getting full of himself just because he was a little good at calculation.

The Physics Club was lively, if not as much as the Chemistry Club. Even so, Watari was standing right by the entrance, arms crossed, as if he meant to keep Mizasaki and me from getting past.

We got along terribly---like cats and dogs. Since it had been obvious Watari would join the Physics Club, both Mizasaki and I wanted to stay away from it if at all possible. There was no way he and I would ever be in the same club. It was more likely a cat and a dog would successfully interbreed.

``It's fine, it's fine. Watari-kun---both of you, I called them over!''

A voice from the far side of the classroom saved us.

The one who hurried over was a pleasant-looking upperclassman. A likable young man with short black hair spiked up slightly, he gave the impression of a refreshing, sporty type. He must smile a lot, because there were wrinkles at the corners of his eyes. Just like with Hongo-senpai, something about him snagged in my memory---like I'd seen him somewhere before.

When he reached us, senpai naturally patted Mikage on the head.

``Aya-chan, nice work on the plus-one duty today. All three of you, come over here.''

As Mikage was patted on the head, she wore the richest smile I'd seen from her yet---catlike.

``So it's like that, huh\dots'' Mizasaki muttered, but neither of them heard him, and we moved to seats by the window.

``Mikage Akifumi. Feel free to call me Akki.''

The Physics Club President---and the brother who had bought Mikage Aya's onigiri---introduced himself in that frank way.
