At the owner's words, Iwama and I tried a bite of the greens. The texture was soft and limp on the tongue, and from behind the dashi came a spring-like aroma typical of mountain vegetables, along with a faint sweetness.

``It's good! It doesn't have any strong taste at all.''

Iwama gave such an expressive reaction on my behalf that I only had to nod.

Still, it was impressive that she'd recognized it as katakuri even though the flowers had changed color.

``So boiling makes the petals change color. From pink to purple.''

``Yeah! Even if you dry them carefully, they end up kind of purplish. Anthocyanins, maybe?''

The plastic shopping bag fluttered through my mind again.

But from Iwama's tone, it sounded like she'd actually dried katakuri flowers before. Which meant that katakuri might be---

No, stop. What good would it do to dig into that? It was pointless to think about.

I brought the topic back to the greens.

``If you made it into something vinegared, it might turn pink again. If it becomes acidic.''

``Yeah, that's true! Let's try it sometime.''

The owner watched us having that conversation with an amused look.

``At this time of year, they're out in the wooded thickets. I think they've opened up quite a bit already, but when they're still buds is when they're best to eat. If you can, it might be good to leave as much of the roots as possible so they'll sprout again next year.''

After offering that advice, he turned his back to us and started draining freshly boiled udon.

``They store nutrients underground, and it takes eight years before they bloom. I'm curious about what the roots taste like too, but yeah---maybe we shouldn't take too many. And I don't want to mess up my stomach, either.''

It sounded like going to pick them was already decided.

Kannabi, who had finally finished her first cooled bite, turned her eyes to Iwama.

``Rio, you know a lot.''

``Del-chan taught me.''

``About katakuri?''

For a split second, I saw Iwama's expression freeze.

``Y-Yeah! We just happened to be talking about katakuriko in class, you know?''

``Ah\dots\ yeah. What was it---when we were talking about that Daira-whatever thing.''

There was nothing shady about it, but Iwama was probably being considerate so Kannabi wouldn't realize the fact that we'd gone to a famous ``love-fulfillment'' spot on the back hill together. I went along with her.

But I didn't know if my acting had been convincing enough. Kannabi's sharp gaze turned on me, and even though I hadn't done anything wrong, a chill ran down my spine.

``Hm. Interesting.''

``\dots What is?''

Kannabi's comment was impossible to read, and my tone came out guarded without meaning to.

``Looking at ohitashi and jumping to anthocyanins isn't exactly a common leap.''

Was it not?

``Ah, you're right. Sorry---I guess I said something weird while we were eating\dots''

``Why are you apologizing, Rio? That was a compliment.''

She said it with a straight face, but immediately afterward, her second bite must have been too hot---she ended up drinking water, her eyes watering.

Savoring the satisfaction of the thick noodles, we set off for Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine first.

We walked through the lively central shopping street. Along the wide sidewalk separated from the road, all kinds of stores were lined up---from an old tofu shop to a stylish café. The foreigners I spotted here and there were probably tourists. Even as someone who lived here, just walking around put me in a good mood. It was the perfect way to work off lunch.

After we went a bit farther, the shops thinned out, and a large stone torii facing south loomed with a commanding presence.

The historic Hachimangu Shrine had a vast precinct stretching from this area near the shopping street up the green mountain slope. Just that alone made it suitable as a walking course. A wide variety of trees had been planted, with cherry blossoms among them, and within the grounds---half like a park---there were multiple subsidiary shrines.

Once we passed through the torii, the approach continued between trees.

``Those trees are huge!''

Iwama looked up at a gigantic cedar and said it. It stood out as taller than the surrounding cedars, and shide paper streamers hung around its trunk.

``This shrine was founded in the Kamakura period, and it seems they've valued these trees since back then.''

I stopped and pointed at the signboard installed beside the cedar.

\begin{center}
{\bfseries
Minister of the Right Cedar

City-designated Natural Monument

Estimated age: approx.\ 800 years

Trunk circumference: 6.5 m

Height: 28 m
}
\end{center}
``What a weird name. If there's a Minister of the Right Cedar, is there a Minister of the Left Cedar too, I wonder?''

Kannabi muttered the same question I'd had when I was little.

``There is. On the other side of the approach.''

Iwama and Kannabi lifted their gazes as if searching for the big tree. That was a bit misleading.

The Minister of the Left was already gone. I walked across the approach.

``This way.''

The two of them followed with puzzled looks, and when they found the Minister of the Left, they let out an ``Ah.''

The Minister of the Left Cedar had been reduced to a miserable stump.

``Apparently it got taken out by lightning around the time we were born. They cut it down because it was dangerous, and only the stump was left here.''

``I see\dots\ Maybe because it's the Minister of the Left, it got taken out?''

Iwama said something so off-the-wall that I tilted my head.

``Like that Sugawara no Michizane vengeful-spirit kind of thing?''

``No, not that. The Minister of the Left outranks the Minister of the Right, right? So I was thinking the cedar, too---maybe the Minister of the Left was taller.''

``Oh, that's what you mean. Lightning strikes the taller one.''

So it was a scientific point.

If the Minister of the Left Cedar had been taller than the Minister of the Right Cedar, lightning would have been more likely to strike it.

When I checked the signboard, sure enough, the height read ``30 m.''

``You're right, Iwama. Looks like this one was about two meters taller.''

``It really was!''

Iwama's eyes lit up as she read the signboard.

``It says the age of the Minister of the Right Cedar was estimated by using Muromachi-period illustrations as a reference, and then analyzing the rings and doing radiocarbon dating on this Minister of the Left stump! That's so scientific!''

The part she latched onto was weirdly niche. But hey---she looked happy.

``Apparently it's hard to measure a tree's age precisely. To take growth rings or collect carbon samples, you have to damage a valuable old tree. So they probably guessed the two trees were about the same age based on Muromachi-period illustrations, then applied the age of this one---since it was already cut down and fair game for testing---to the Minister of the Right.''

``How ironic.''

Kannabi muttered that, and I turned to her, wondering what she meant by ironic.

``The successful one gets struck by lightning, and the other uses its `seal of approval.' It's like Japanese society.''

Please don't suddenly launch into a blistering social critique.

As we continued along the approach, both sides turned into rows of cherry trees. By now they were completely leafed out. During peak bloom, people had crowded around them so much, and yet now no one bothered to stop and look at the blossoms. We headed straight for the worship hall.

The three of us lined up and did two bows, two claps, and one bow. I prayed for today to end peacefully.

Beside me, Iwama seemed to be praying intently even after I finished my final bow. I found myself a little curious what she was thinking, but I gave a small shake of my head to rein myself in.

After we finished paying our respects, we were walking along wondering what to do next when we happened to come near the ema rack. I didn't miss the way Iwama looked over there and froze for just a moment.

``Let's look at that map!''

Was she trying to get away from the ema as quickly as possible? She pointed ahead at a large signboard.

Neither Kannabi nor I objected, so we followed.

It was a map of the Hachimangu Shrine grounds. With a title like ``A Grove of Giant Trees That Makes You Feel History,'' it even included the Minister of the Right Cedar and the Minister of the Left Cedar (stump) from earlier. The Minister of the Right Cedar even had its estimated age written in, with careful thoroughness.

``So besides the cedars, there's a ginkgo and a camphor tree too.''

On the map, in addition to the Minister of the Right Cedar with its estimated 800 years, two other giant trees were highlighted.

One was the ``Mimamori Ginkgo'' within the grounds.

And the other, slightly farther off, was the ``Big Camphor.'' This one was 1,200 years old.

``Hm? This\dots''

Iwama was looking at the ginkgo illustration.

``What is it?''

``Did they get the ginkgo's age wrong?''

When I looked, there was a green sticker---matching the background---placed under the ginkgo. It was a long rectangle. Since the Minister of the Right Cedar and the Big Camphor had their ages written in the corresponding spots, it made sense to assume the ginkgo's age was being covered up.

``Probably. They messed it up, so they hid it with a sticker.''

``Are you sure? If they realized it was wrong, it seems like they'd overwrite it with the correct age instead of hiding it.''

For Kannabi, it was a perfectly reasonable point.

``Yeah, you're right. Then why\dots?''

Iwama snapped her head around toward me.

``Why do you think? Can you figure it out---scientifically!''

``Scientifically?''

``Del-chan, you know a lot about plants, right?''

``I know some. But as for what the shrine staff were thinking, I can't---''

Seeing the way Iwama's eyes were shining, my words dropped straight to the ground. I hurriedly searched for something else to say.

``Well, it might be interesting to think about it a bit. The ginkgo's right nearby. Let's look at the real thing first.''

The Mimamori Ginkgo was a famous giant tree even in Tsunanagai, thanks to its imposing presence. Its light-colored bark bulged in uneven knots, and branches as thick as street trees' trunks stretched out in multiple directions, as if they were holding up the sky.

The most distinctive part was the base. There was a hollow big enough for a person to fit inside. It was fenced off so you couldn't enter now, but supposedly someone once hid in that hollow to protect themselves---which was why it was called the Mimamori Ginkgo.

In spring, the still-small ginkgo leaves were a vivid yellow-green, glittering as they caught the sunlight.

``This one's huge too! If we're talking thickness, it might even be more impressive than the Minister of the Right Cedar.''

Iwama's feet headed straight for the signboard. Then she let out an ``Ah!''

``Did you figure something out?''

``No\dots\ but this, it really is\dots''

I looked at the signboard too.

\begin{center}
{\bfseries

Mimamori Ginkgo

Nationally Designated Natural Monument

Trunk circumference: 9.8 m

Height: 23 m

}
\end{center}

``See? The age here was hidden later too.''

Here, a pale resin plate---close to the tree's own color---had been affixed with some kind of adhesive. Judging from the careful workmanship that must have cost money, it didn't look like someone's malicious prank. It seemed like something the administrators had done.

``Thorough cover-up work.''

Was it?

``Maybe it really was just wrong, and they erased it. Maybe they figured it wasn't worth the trouble to overwrite it and just decided to cover it.''

``Mm, maybe\dots\ but with something this impressive, it wouldn't be strange for it to be over a thousand years old\dots\ And if they're touting a `Grove of Giant Trees That Makes You Feel History,' they should just write the correct age.''

As Iwama said, a tree big enough for a person to fit inside its hollow certainly did feel historical.

But what she'd said was mistaken.

``It's impressive, sure, but its age actually isn't even a thousand years.''

Iwama's eyes went wide. ``Huh, really?''

I nodded.

``Have you heard that ginkgo is an introduced species? The ginkgo lineage originally flourished back in the Jurassic, when dinosaurs were thriving. Then it went through the ice ages and was pushed to the brink of extinction, and only the single ginkgo species we have today survived in the warm southwestern part of China.''

``I've heard that. They call it a living fossil, right?''

``Right. That living fossil started spreading within China around the eleventh century. It was about seven hundred years ago that seeds were imported to Japan and the tree became established here. There are plenty of ginkgo trees in Japan claimed to be a thousand years old, but none of those claims have been scientifically verified---they're estimates back-calculated from legends.''

``I see. So then the age would be, at most, seven hundred years\dots''

Iwama started counting the width of the resin plate stuck onto the signboard, matching it to the number of characters on the line beside it.

Eight characters' worth. So it would probably read something like ``Estimated age: approx.\ \rule{1cm}{0.5pt}\ years.'' There wasn't necessarily any discrepancy.

Then Iwama shifted her gaze and read the detailed explanation.

``It says it's also called the `Mimamori Ginkgo' from the legend that a child hid here and protected themselves from a tengu. Maybe if we research that legend, it could give us a clue about the age.''

Things moved along at a rapid clip, and we headed to the small museum permanently set up at Hachimangu Shrine.

Calling it a museum, though---it was really just a small, single-floor space you could enter for free. Behind large glass panels along the wall, items related to Hachimangu Shrine were displayed in neat rows. It was my first time inside too.

The first thing that caught my eye was a huge cross-section of wood displayed leaning against the wall. Maybe because it was too big, it had been cut in half after being sliced, forming a semicircular shape.

The Minister of the Left Cedar---the end state of the giant tree struck by lightning. Marks had been added to its growth rings, and next to it, a graph used for radiocarbon dating had been posted as well. There was a long explanation of how they'd measured its age. It seemed that the ages of all the giant trees at this shrine had been scientifically examined.

I skimmed through it, and when I reached the last line, I let out a sigh.

``Let's go. Looks like there'll be a drawing of the Mimamori Ginkgo over there.''

But it seemed I hadn't been able to play it off as well as Iwama had at the ema rack.

``Hey, Del-chan\dots\ this Professor Izuta---could it be\dots?''

Iwama asked me that. I had no choice but to answer.

``\dots My father.''

``I knew it!''

On the last line of the explanation, my father's name was written. Apparently, the one who'd investigated the shrine's giant trees was my father. Come to think of it, when I'd asked about the Minister of the Left Cedar ages ago, my father might have hinted at something like that.

I didn't really want to talk about it, but not explaining here would make it look like I had some kind of complex about it, which wouldn't do either. Reluctantly, I opened my mouth.

``My father is a plant ecologist. He taught me various things when I was little, so I know a bit about plants too. Of course, I'm nowhere near his level.''

I'd added an unnecessary line. But Iwama looked openly impressed.

``So that's why you knew so much about katakuri too!''

``Yeah\dots\ I guess.''

Wanting to get away from my father's name, I moved on ahead. The two of them followed.

Past displays of earthenware and porcelain said to have been excavated from the shrine grounds, there was an illustrated scroll with a tengu drawn on it.

It was apparently from the early Kamakura period. Maybe because it was that old, the actual original was kept at the prefectural museum, and what was displayed here was a replica. It wasn't very realistic, but on aged paper, thin lines and pale colors depicted a single scene.

From atop clouds drawn with simple lines, a frightening man with a beak looked down at the ground. It was apparently the shrine grounds. Near a small hokora shrine stood a single large tree with a big hollow, its leafy green canopy vaguely rendered like a cloud. Inside that hollow, a small child was clutching their head.

The title read ``Tengu Attack.'' I read the explanation.

The long-nosed tengu statues became widespread from the late Muromachi period onward. Before that, tengu were primarily depicted with beaks. This illustration depicts a child who hid in the hollow of a giant tree to escape the tengu's punishment, and shows that since the Kamakura period, this Hachimangu Shrine has been known as a grove of giant trees.

Next to it, a travelogue from the Edo period was on display. This one seemed to be the real thing, but because it was written in cursive script, it was almost impossible to decipher. I had to rely on the printed explanation.

Even in the Edo period, this Hachimangu Shrine was loved as a grove of giant trees. In the diary of a merchant who visited, the legend of the Mimamori Ginkgo---about a child hiding in the ginkgo to protect themselves from a tengu---was recorded.

Ah. I see.

Based on everything we'd learned so far, I had a rough grasp of the facts.

And if I organized what we'd seen, the reason the ginkgo's age had been hidden became obvious.

Tsunanagai Hachimangu Shrine had been keeping one secret, while taking care not to tell a lie.

``\dots Del-chan, this is weird, right?''

``Yeah. It's weird.''

``Is it\dots? What's weird about it?''

As Kannabi looked confused, Iwama explained.

``Because if the ginkgo only came into Japan around seven hundred years ago\dots\ then it's strange for there to be a giant ginkgo tree in the early Kamakura period.''

``\dots Around when was the Kamakura period again?''

``From 1185 to 1333. Early Kamakura would be about eight hundred years ago.''

Iwama's instant answer---even down to the years---was impressive.

``I see\dots\ so even if there's a lot of fuzziness in the numbers seven hundred and eight hundred, it still doesn't make sense for a ginkgo to have grown large enough for a person to hide inside it eight hundred years ago.''

``The person who wrote this explanation must have noticed that. You can tell if you read it carefully. Nowhere does it say that, in the Kamakura period, a child hid in a ginkgo's hollow.''

``Huh---no way, but---''

She reread it, and Kannabi's eyes went wide.

``You're right. The illustration's description doesn't even mention the ginkgo.''

``So now you can see why the ginkgo's age was hidden, too. They weren't hiding an incorrect age. They were hiding the correct age.''

After taking a moment to think, Iwama spoke carefully.

``If we stack up only what we can say for sure, it comes out like this, right? In the early Kamakura period, there was a giant tree with a big hollow at this Hachimangu Shrine, and based on that, `Tengu Attack' was drawn. That tree wasn't a ginkgo. But in the Edo period, there was a giant ginkgo with a hollow. People in the Edo period mistakenly told the story as if the legendary tree the child hid in was a ginkgo.''

``In other words, after the ginkgo grew large, the stage of the legend quietly got swapped over to the ginkgo without anyone noticing. It even got named the Mimamori Ginkgo, and by the Edo period, that version had already stuck.''

Once something became established as a famous spot, it was hard to bluntly deny it.

``If they wrote the age straightforwardly, people would realize, `Wasn't this ginkgo tree not here yet in the era when the tengu illustration was made?' Maybe someone actually pointed that out. So to keep things vague, they had no choice but to hide the ginkgo's real age.''

``\dots But then what was the giant tree drawn in `Tengu Attack'?''

At Iwama's question, one possibility came to mind.

``The Big Camphor. The camphor tree up in the mountains. It said that one was 1,200 years old. Even eight hundred years ago, it would've been around four hundred years old. It wouldn't be strange if it was already a giant tree with a hollow.''

Kannabi frowned.

``The Big Camphor still exists, right? Then why did it get swapped to the ginkgo?''

A perfectly fair question. Why, indeed?

Just then, footsteps approached from behind.

``I was wondering what you were discussing so earnestly. So it was about the Mimamori Ginkgo.''

It was the elderly man who'd been sitting at reception. He watched us with a genial smile.

``This is just my guess, but I think it's because the ginkgo stood out more. In autumn it turns a beautiful yellow, and it's planted in a conspicuous spot, isn't it? A camphor tree, on the other hand, is rather plain. Perhaps not many people went out of their way to climb the mountain path just to see it.''

A sigh slipped out of me. That was probably all there was to it.

If you were going to cast a tree as the legend's star, it had to be the one that looked good in bright light.

Cherry blossoms. Ginkgo. Maples too. The world adored what lived in the sun.

People didn't gather in the shade.

Plenty of people would climb a mountain path to see cherry blossoms, but hardly anyone would bother going to see a camphor tree.

I said it was fine, but Iwama insisted---with surprising enthusiasm---that she wanted to go see the Big Camphor. Kannabi agreed, so I tried threatening them with, ``It's a pretty serious climb,'' but she shot back, ``That's nothing. Who do you think I am?'' and in the end, we headed up the mountain path.

I had no idea who Kannabi was. But wasn't she supposed to be the ``ultimate indoors type''?

As we walked along the narrow dirt trail, I realized this was the second time I'd climbed a mountain path together with Iwama.

The first time was to see the famous cherry blossoms.

And this time, it was to see the camphor tree abandoned by legend.

Of all pairings, those two made it feel like a bitter sort of irony.

``Ah---this flower.''

Iwama stopped partway. On a low shrub beside the path, pale pink blossoms were blooming in a wild scatter.

``Rhododendron!''

Iwama identified it before I even had the chance to say anything.

Rhododendrons are low shrubs found mainly in mountainous areas. They keep slender, glossy leaves year-round, and---true to being in the azalea family, they bloom with beautiful flowers from spring into summer. They're also popular as ornamental cultivars, so it's a plant you can run into in all sorts of places without ever climbing a mountain. Since this area's elevation was low, it was probably something someone had planted here.

``Hey, Del-chan---what do you think about the `language of flowers'?''

The sudden question made me think. The language of flowers. I knew the concept, but I'd never looked into it.

``\dots Honestly? I'm not very interested.''

Iwama nodded.

``I thought so at first too. Like, it isn't scientific and all.''

``Th-then\dots\ do you feel differently now\dots?''

Kannabi cut into the conversation, practically on her last breath. Maybe the reason Iwama had stopped in front of the rhododendron was to spare Kannabi's stamina. Until I noticed Kannabi wheezing, that possibility hadn't even occurred to me. It was a quiet kind of kindness.

``For example---one of the rhododendron's flower meanings is `caution.' And I wondered why, since it's such a pretty flower, but apparently it's because it blooms high up in the mountains. Going to pick it can be dangerous, so it's basically saying, `Be careful.' ''

``I see\dots\ so it's a `flower on a high peak,' then\dots\dots\dots\dots''

Kannabi said it like she was looking up at Iwama. You should stop talking and rest.

If a `flower on a high peak' were blooming in a place like this, you wouldn't have to go through all this trouble.

Still, I could understand why Iwama had taken an interest in flower meanings.

``So even the language of flowers has its own interesting side that connects to scientific knowledge.''

``Yeah. So when I see a flower, I try to look up its flower meaning too. If you pick flowers and give them to someone, it'd be a problem if they had some weird meaning.''

As I thought about how Iwama might be the only person I knew who actually sourced gift flowers from somewhere other than a flower shop, I also had to admit she had a point. If you gave someone flowers because they were pretty and their meaning turned out to be ``suffering for generations,'' that would be a problem.

Once Kannabi's breathing had evened out, we resumed the hike, and in less than five minutes we reached the Big Camphor. The mountain path was shaded the whole way, and beneath the Big Camphor was no exception.

I'd been here a few times before. The memory that stood out most was the first time. It was so long ago I wasn't even sure I was fully aware yet, but my family of four had come here together. My little sister, about two years younger than me, might still have been riding on our father's back.

Even so, I clearly remembered what my father had said to me.

---Someday, you'll become this, Sho.

In the dim forest---even in daylight---the tree gave me the same impression as it had back then.

Divine.

An age of twelve hundred years felt entirely plausible. A trunk so thick it looked like it had swallowed a small hut. The way it soared upward was like a giant, and its spreading branches and leaves were like a dome roof. Near the base, a hollow gaped open---complete with an actual torii gate---like the entrance to a cave.

Wordless, the three of us stood for a while, overwhelmed by its presence.

Finally, I lowered my gaze to the signboard.

\begin{center}
{\bfseries
Hachimangu's Big Camphor

Prefectural Designated Natural Monument

Estimated age: approx.\ 1,200 years

Trunk circumference: 15.5 m

Height: 26 m
}
\end{center}

A trunk circumference of 15.5 meters meant it was big enough that ten adults could link hands and barely wrap around it. Even with its huge spread of branches, its height nearly matched the Minister of the Right Cedar, which had a tower-like form.

It was probably the largest tree anywhere around here.

``A single tree can get this big\dots''

Iwama, who'd been looking almost straight up, slowly lowered her head again. Maybe her neck had gotten tired---she rubbed her shoulder, then suddenly noticed something and pointed.

``Hey, look---this little shrine.''

The three of us walked over to the side of the camphor. There, a small stone hokora sat quietly. Moss-covered, partially crumbled, it looked quite old.

It was probably the one drawn beside the giant tree in the ``Tengu Attack'' illustration.

``Looks like the tree in that drawing really was this camphor.''

``From the ginkgo's age being hidden\dots\ all the way to reaching a truth like this\dots''

Kannabi's breathing was still a bit ragged. I told you.

But I agreed with what she was saying. From a small snag that I alone would've brushed off, we'd traced our way all the way to the truth behind a legend that had shifted over the course of eight hundred years---

For a while, we just stood there, staring blankly at the giant tree.

When the wind blew, the branches rustled far overhead, and glossy reddish-brown leaves fluttered down.

One of them smacked Kannabi square in the face.

``Ubgh.''

After that bizarre reaction, Kannabi pinched the leaf that was still sitting on her face and lifted it away.

``Leaves falling\dots\ at this time of year?''

When I looked down, the ground was littered everywhere with camphor leaves.

``Camphor trees are evergreen broadleaf trees. They keep their leaves year-round so they can photosynthesize through the winter, and when spring comes, they put out new buds while dropping the old leaves. So this is their season for shedding.''

``Huh. I didn't know that. Dropping leaves in the middle of everything blooming\dots\ are you just being contrarian?''

``Probably.''

As if answering her, the wind blew again, and dead leaves fluttered down once more. A camphor tree baring itself in loneliness deep in the forest in spring was the opposite of the cherry trees at the school's back hill, tossing up a storm of petals.

``But\dots\ that's exactly why camphor trees are strong. Throwing away old leaves in spring---when new buds are coming in---is a strategy for optimizing photosynthesis. Since they keep leaves through winter, they don't lose out in competition even in a dark forest. They grow steadily where no one notices, and sooner or later, they become a giant tree like this.''

There is a concept involving sun trees and shade trees.

Sun trees are trees that can only grow in sunlight. Many deciduous trees fall into that category---cherries and ginkgos, for example. They take in as much sun as they can and grow rapidly, a strategy of claiming the light first.

Shade trees, on the other hand, are trees that can grow even in the shade. Many evergreen trees fall into that category, like camphors. They grow slowly in a dark forest and aim for late maturation.

Of course, it isn't that one is superior. Their survival strategies are simply different.

And that was why, to the camphor tree discarded by the legend, I had to say it.

``\dots Even the ones in the shade have a survival strategy of their own.''

Had it sounded like an excuse? Had Iwama seen through why I'd said something like that? It was obviously an unnecessary line.

An ugly claim I'd dressed up in plants---something that didn't need to be put into words. But maybe defending myself this pathetically in front of those dazzling cherry blossoms was, in itself, exactly the kind of life a ``shadow-dweller'' would live.

``I envy that.''

Iwama's murmur slipped out. A voice close to a sigh, like she'd let it fall by accident.

Watching the countless leaves drifting down, Iwama slowly parted her lips.

``Working hard where nobody sees you, deep in a mountain like this, and becoming the lord of the forest---people tend to make a big deal out of famous cherry blossoms, but I like trees like this better. The kind that spread their branches out inside the forest.''

She took a deep, pleasant breath, then added:

``\dots It feels like they aren't lying to themselves.''

The sun gradually began to slant lower, and the temperature started dropping little by little. After coming down the mountain, we left Hachimangu and headed for the next ``famous spot.'' It wasn't that there was anything in particular there---just the sea, where plenty of people stop by anyway.

We bought warm drinks at a coffee shop in the Central Shopping District, then headed south at an easy pace, watching the town as we went, until we finally reached the coast.

We walked because Kannabi insisted, ``I'm fine. Who do you think I am?'' but it was a fair distance. The rest of the coffee I'd been sipping along the way had gone completely cold. There was a trash can beside a public restroom at the entrance to the beach, so I finished it there and tossed the paper cup.

Maegahama. A long, narrow stretch of sand ran along the shoreline. The white sand glittered brilliantly under the late-afternoon sunlight. In the cool spring evening, there weren't any daredevils going into the water; most people were just out walking, like us.

Without really thinking about it, we started heading west, toward where the sun was about to set. The backlight was blinding. Dry sand was hard to walk on, so we followed the wet waterline instead. When you stepped on the sand, the area around your shoes somehow swelled outward like ripples.

``Rei used to be in the astronomy club in middle school, right? She's aiming for science, and she's planning to choose biology, so she's the same as me.''

Thanks to Iwama's introduction, I finally learned one small piece of what kind of person this mysterious Kannabi Rei actually was.

``You did astronomy, but you're choosing biology?''

``Yeah. I'm bad at formulas, so I didn't calculate celestial motion or anything. I just watched the stars.''

``So you like stars.''

``Not really. I joined astronomy club because I thought I'd be able to learn astrology. But none of the upperclassmen or the advisor would teach fortune-telling, so I taught myself seriously.''

Do astronomy.

Still, if she'd made it to Tsunanagai High School, she must've studied more than just fortune-telling.

``By the way,''

Iwama looked at the two of us.

``How did you two meet? When Rei said she was going to call you, Del-chan, I was honestly pretty surprised. I didn't really have the image of you two talking much.''

It seemed Kannabi still hadn't told Iwama about yesterday's club activity. Why not? When I looked, her black eyebrows were twitching in a way that looked like a signal. She wanted me to keep quiet.

``\dots Bread.''

``Bread?''

Iwama tilted her head at Kannabi's baffling statement.

``I was running with bread in my mouth, and I crashed into Delta at a corner.''

I wanted her to come up with a slightly more plausible lie.

``Whaaat? Were you okay?''

Didn't it hurt her heart to deceive someone this pure? But Kannabi was completely unfazed.

``Yeah. The bread fell and got ruined, but that's how we met.''

``I see\dots''

That wasn't it at all, though.

Thinking it would be better to change the subject, I tried steering the conversation.

``How did Iwama and Kannabi end up getting close?''

``Because our attendance numbers are next to each other, I guess? Our seats are far apart in the classroom, but outside of that we end up together pretty often. We're paired up in P.E., too. And then when it came to choosing electives and stuff, we found we had a lot in common.''

So that was it.

Sure enough, if you divided us into groups of five by attendance number, Iwama ended up in a different group from me and in the same group as Kannabi. Even without any sense of duty as class reps, the two of them had reasons to get closer.

It made me feel stupid for having slapped labels like ``loner'' and ``class rep'' onto what was just a normal friendship.

``I have zero cooperativeness, so it really helps having Rio nearby. Even though our seats are far apart, lately we've been together through lunch, too. Thank you for always keeping me company.''

Kannabi threw out some self-deprecation that was hard to deny, and Iwama shook her head.

``Keeping you company isn't it\dots\ I'm just having fun being with Rei.''

``Is that so? Fun?''

``It is! The things Rei says are kind of weird and it's funny\dots''

She really was a little strange, that much was true. You could also say she was a bit off. Either way, someone who says things you can't predict is interesting.

Maybe because I was thinking along those lines, I almost missed what Iwama said next.

``Also, Rei treats me really normally.''

Kannabi and I both failed to react, and only the quiet sound of waves and our footsteps along the shore filled the air.

She must have realized her mouth had slipped. Iwama snapped her gaze upward, startled.

``Ah, sorry. That was a weird thing to say.''

She said it in a voice that sounded just a hair higher than usual.

Saying she needed to use the restroom, Iwama jogged back the way we'd come.

Before I could go after her, her back had already receded into the distance. There was only one public restroom on the beach, and it was pretty far away, so Kannabi and I were left standing there alone.

After we stood in silence for a while, Kannabi suddenly started walking in the opposite direction from the restroom.

``Shouldn't we wait?''

When I called out, Kannabi didn't stop walking, and began like this.

``Rio, you see\dots''

It sounded like she had something to say. I walked alongside her.

``Rio wanted to know more about you. That's why I planned today's outing.''

It was sudden, but I understood why Kannabi had started walking.

There was something she didn't want Iwama to hear. And it would take a while. So we walked.

``Iwama\dots\ wanted to know about me?''

``Yes. It seems you and Rio get along quite well, don't you? We're the same type.''

It was true that in middle school I'd been in a science club, and that I was even considering choosing biology. In that sense, ``same type'' wasn't entirely wrong.

``But why are you intervening in that, Kannabi?''

``Because you seemed like you were avoiding Rio.''

``\dots I'm not avoiding her.''

``Are you sure? When lunch break starts, you always go straight to sit next to Mizasaki-kun. Like a lover who's been longing for a rendezvous.''

That was unfair. But setting the metaphor aside, I couldn't deny I'd been doing it, so I pushed back from another angle.

``You went straight to your own seat too, didn't you?''

``Which came first, the chicken or the egg? When Delta disappears, that seat becomes isolated and lonely, so she started coming over to me---she phrased it more gently, but Rio said something like that.''

Was that true? My trust in what Kannabi said had already fallen well below standard, but even so, it didn't seem like she had a reason to tell that kind of lie here.

``I understand how you feel, Delta. If someone as wonderful and popular as that is kind to you, you can't help but reach your limit. That smile, not directed only at you, starts taking up a large place inside you. I don't think you should become the sort of person who chases after idols.''

I had no intention of doing that, warning or not. I would climb onto a giant's shoulders, but I lived by the principle of not reaching out for the moon.

But even though I wanted to deny it, the words didn't come out well. We kept moving slowly along the surf line.

``So then\dots\ what do you want, bringing Iwama and me together?''

``It turned out to be quite a fun outing, didn't it? I wanted to confirm that.''

That wasn't an answer.

``That's not all, is it. When you came to the biology room yesterday, was that for some purpose too? You didn't actually submit a club application, did you?''

``No. I did submit the club application to Tokumura-sensei on Thursday. I'm sure of it.''

``Even though you haven't even seen the Biology Club's activities?''

Without answering that question, Kannabi threw one back at me.

``Do you know why Rio didn't go to the welcome events for the science clubs?''

``She didn't go\dots? I just assumed she never planned to.''

``Even though she was in the Science Club in middle school, and it's obvious how much she loves science? Of course she really wants to check out a science club.''

``She did talk about what kinds of clubs she was considering. But then it was like, she got invited, so she'd go check out the basketball club\dots''

Thinking back, Iwama never actually said what she wanted. I feel like all she talked about was her mom, or what middle school was like---things like that.

``Right. That's the kind of person Rio is. Someone who puts what other people want from her first, and can't clearly say what she herself wants to do. Precisely because she's so capable, she can't settle on what she actually wants\dots\ and she suffers the most from that. She suppresses her real self and offers up the version of herself others want. That's what it means to keep standing in the light.''

I remembered what Iwama had said.

---I kind of admire that.

In the forest, looking at the camphor tree spreading its branches and leaves in thick, somber green, Iwama had said that.

Iwama---whose name carried ``cherry blossom.''

``Her mother and her friends recommended an athletics club, so she went to the basketball club's welcome event. And even when I told her that you two had joined the Biology Club and encouraged her to go try it out, she held back out of consideration for you two.''

``Held back\dots? Why? It's not like she had any reason to.''

``Rio said this, you know---`If I went to try it out\dots\ and then I still ended up deciding on a different club, what would Izuta-kun and the others think?'''

I couldn't keep up.

``What would we think\dots? We wouldn't think anything.''

``Really? Wouldn't you be at least a little disappointed?''

``Even if that were possible, that's not Iwama's problem---''

After I said it, I realized.

Iwama was kind. Unbelievably careful in the way she considered others, and she exercised that kindness quietly.

She'd made it sound like she was just talking about the language of flowers for rhododendrons, and used that as a pretext to make Kannabi take a break.

And the way she'd insisted so strongly that she wanted to go see the great camphor tree---maybe that, too---

``Right. Rio thinks that far ahead before she acts. She's compelled to think that way. Like she's wearing a collar she can't take off.''

``A collar?''

I couldn't picture it. What did a collar have to do with compulsively thinking about other people's impressions of you? Whether it was an apt metaphor, or one of Kannabi's signature mysterious turns of phrase, I couldn't tell.

``If you don't know, that's fine. This isn't foreshadowing or anything.''

Listening to the waves, I pulled my thoughts together.

``So\dots\ you joined the Biology Club because you were trying to create a situation where Iwama could easily drop by?''

``Yes. It's a simple story. `If you're interested, why don't you go?' won't get Rio to move. But `Could you come take a look for my sake?' will. Because then it becomes a request from someone else.''

It was as if she were thinking in terms of hacking someone's behavioral patterns. But the logic held. If you want to get a logical agent who only acts altruistically to perform a self-interested action, you just need to make them interpret that self-interested action as altruistic. Not ``You can eat,'' but ``Help me eat this.''

``Why would you go that far for Iwama?''

When I asked, Kannabi's eyes showed a sharp glint of amusement.

``Because I didn't want a friend I finally made to go far away.''

Sure enough, Kannabi didn't look like she'd be good at basketball.

``\dots So after this, I'm going to tell Rio that I joined the Biology Club. When she heard you two had joined, Rio was really interested. One more push and she should come. \dots So, if you don't mind, could you help me? You'd be happy too, wouldn't you, if someone with that much aptitude became a member?''

``Well, I can lend a hand a little, I guess\dots''

It felt like the mystery surrounding Kannabi had finally unraveled. The reason she'd suddenly shown up in the biology room yesterday, and the reason she'd tricked me into coming out today, both came from something simple: joining the Biology Club together with Iwama.

\dots Huh?

There was one thing that still didn't sit right.

``How did you even hear that we joined the Biology Club?''

We'd only told Mikage, at most. We filled out the applications on the forms we were given and submitted them directly to Tokumura-sensei. I didn't remember having any conversation in the classroom that would've made it obvious. So how did Kannabi---someone I'd only spoken to for the first time yesterday---know something even Iwama, right behind me, hadn't known?

``Good question. As expected of Delta.''

After glancing back toward the restroom, Kannabi continued.

``It seems Tsunanagai High School has a monster living in it. A terrifying venomous snake that stalks people in secret.''

The phrasing sounded nothing like a club-related topic, and I tilted my head.

``What do you mean? If you're talking about a specific person, can you tell me who it is?''

``I think it would be better if I don't say the name yet. So\dots''

With a slightly meaningful smile, Kannabi spoke as if whispering.

``Right. Let's call it being the one giving directions, for now.''

It seemed we'd walked quite a bit. Iwama came running back to us, saying, ``Sorry! I'm late!'' Her breathing was a little ragged. Kannabi glanced sideways at me as my chest tightened, and said,

``\dots\dots A seagull.''

``Hm? A seagull\dots\dots?''

``Because Delta chased after a seagull, we ended up moving a bit. Sorry.''

``Oh, really? That's totally fine!''

I'd been made into the culprit without my consent, but I had no choice but to go along with Kannabi's lie.

Without denying the seagull story, I only muttered, ``Sorry.'' There hadn't been any seagulls anywhere.

We resumed our stroll. As we kept walking along the beach further west, we reached Morigasaki, which jutted out into the sea. It was a small cape covered in pine trees, with steep cliffs all around. We were talking about whether we should climb up using the stairs built into the cliff, when Iwama suddenly noticed something.

``The sand around here is black.''

``Yeah, it is.''

Now that she mentioned it, the white beach sand had somehow turned into something darker. It wasn't that it was wet and looked deeper in color---the grains of sand themselves had become black.

``Did the composition change, I wonder.''

In front of Kannabi, who tilted her head, Iwama took out her phone. I wondered what she was doing, but she crouched down, opened the folio-style case, and began holding it over the surface of the sand as if she were scanning it.

``\dots\dots What are you doing?''

Iwama stood up right away, and instead of answering, she showed me the clasp on her phone case with a pleased look.

``I see---iron sand.''

The folio-style phone case closed with a small magnet. She'd held that magnet over the sand. If there was iron sand mixed in, it would definitely stick---but it sounded like a pain to remove.

I'd been thinking it since the cherry blossoms, but even though she looked reserved, she didn't hesitate at times like this.

``Yeah! It seems there's magnetite in the strata around Morigasaki. I'm sure somewhere there's---''

She cut herself off mid-sentence, suddenly pressing her lips together.

``Sorry, I said something weird. Don't mind me.''

It was probably close to a habit. The way she denied her own statement by calling it ``weird'' made me remember Kannabi's phrasing about ``suppressing her real self.''

From experience, I knew well what happened when you talked about scientific things in everyday life: most people treated you like some kind of weirdo. In mainstream content, the ``science-type character'' was often an odd person wearing glasses. Sadly, I happened to fit into that mold too.

If Iwama was the type to want to find scientific elements in everyday events, there had to have been countless times she'd been seen through that ``weirdo'' lens. I'd chosen to cast aside the majority's gaze, but Iwama had been the opposite.

Hiding what you like in order to play the role of a reasonable, likable person---how lonely must that be?

Imagining what Iwama had been through until now, I felt a shiver run up my spine.

``\dots\dots Close-packed structure.''

At some point, my mouth had moved on its own.

``Huh?''

Maybe because it was so sudden, Iwama stared at me with her eyes wide.

``A close-packed structure is an arrangement that packs spheres of the same size into a fixed space with the least wasted volume, maximizing density. It's used when considering the structures of metallic crystals and molecular crystals.''

The words spilled out smoothly.

``Dilatancy is a property where something flows like a liquid if you apply force slowly, but if you apply force suddenly, it stops moving like a solid. The reason the wet sand under our feet hardens every time we step on it is because of that.''

Iwama was staring blankly. I was embarrassed enough that I almost wanted to stop, but I kept going anyway.

``Neo-Darwinism is modern evolutionary theory. It adds newly gained knowledge to the concept of natural selection proposed by Darwin, trying to capture evolution more accurately.''

I pointed at Iwama's phone.

``And magnetite is triiron tetroxide. It's a type of iron oxide with strong magnetism, so it sticks to magnets. It's the main component of iron sand. This is all within the scope of high school chemistry, so it's not `weird' at all. It's something you and I and Kannabi would know.''

I didn't actually know whether Kannabi knew it, but there was the bread and seagull situation. I wanted her to forgive me.

``When it comes to the words you use, you don't need to correct yourself for us, and you don't need to apologize. There might be things that don't get across to people, and you might get seen as pedantic. But in front of us, you don't have to hold back. It comes across normally.''

The sound of waves, and the sound of wind rustling through the pine grove. The sea breeze was a little cold. Beyond Morigasaki, it seemed the sun had set before I'd noticed. The clear sky was starting to darken in tone.

Had I overdone it?

Iwama looked stunned for a while at my strange behavior. Then she lowered her gaze just a little and,

``\dots\dots Thank you,''

she said, almost like a mutter. I couldn't really see her face.

``You know, I decided I'm joining the Biology Club.''

Kannabi stepped forward from the side. Iwama lifted her face in surprise.

``I already submitted my club application. We decided we'll start real activities from Monday. So, Rio, if you'd like---''

My waist felt oddly itchy. It was like I was overhearing a love confession from the side.

``If you'd like, Rio, won't you join the Biology Club too?''

Holding the phone she'd had in one hand, Iwama hugged it to her chest with both arms. She looked like a girl who'd just been confessed to out of nowhere, and it made me feel even more uncomfortable.

``Rei\dots\dots thank you.''

Kannabi, who nodded once at Iwama's thanks, glanced toward me.

Right. Iwama had only said thank you so far. Even this still wasn't enough.

Everyone has their own survival strategy.

I'd chosen to live by respecting that, and for that reason I wasn't the type to interfere with other people's choices. Shade, in other words, was exactly that kind of place.

But this time---even knowing my words would become a single drop that sent ripples through Iwama's way of living, I was probably supposed to say it.

``Just trying it once is fine\dots\dots if you're interested, I'd be happy if you came too on Monday, Iwama.''

From behind a rock, a loud \textit{zabun} of a wave answered.

% ==============================
%        Chapter 4
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Chapter 4\\
    Lorentz's Spiriting Away
\end{center}
\label{sec:ch4}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Chapter 4}
\vspace{1cm}

The Austrian ethologist Konrad Lorenz was famous as the discoverer of ``imprinting,'' where baby waterfowl come to chase after the first thing they see. There was even a photo in biology textbooks of him with only his head above the lake surface while greylag geese on either side fussed with his hair.

True to the impression that photo gave, it seemed he was a pretty extreme person. No---calling a Nobel Prize--winning physiologist ``extreme'' would be rude. Maybe it would be better to say he lived according to his own distinctive way of being.

When it came to observing behavior, Lorenz didn't like confining animals in cages or pens. So he apparently kept birds and monkeys and the like roaming freely in and around his home. The natural result was that his own daily life suffered enormous damage. But it seemed he spoke of it cheerfully---and even proudly.

Even if it wasn't to that extent, the girl named Karato was unmistakably an extreme person.

Apparently her parents ran an animal hospital. She said their house overflowed with rescued creatures, and her uniform was covered in fur. That wasn't all. Her roughly tied, messy hair. Her black-framed glasses with parts of the frame missing here and there. It wasn't that she was dirty---more like, at a glance, you could tell, ``Ah, this person is not ordinary.''

I heard later that her hair was stiff and rough because there wasn't any human shampoo at her house. With normal shampoo, she apparently couldn't bathe together with the animals. And because her best friend (a sulphur-crested cockatoo) often perched on her shoulder and chewed on her glasses, she'd given up on replacing them.

A week had passed since that outing, and on Monday, an official activity day was finally set through Tokumura-sensei. Karato-senpai casually showed up in the biology room where Mizasaki, Kannabi, Iwama, and I were waiting.

I thought ``casually'' was an extremely accurate description. When she opened the door, her very first words were shocking.

``Huh, who\dots?''

Even as I thought she couldn't seriously say ``who'' to new members, we each introduced ourselves.

Karato-senpai listened with her eyes still wide, with heavy dark circles beneath them, nodding along, and then said,

``Ahh, so for now I should teach you how to take care of the creatures, I guess? It's hard, but do your best. I'm Karato. You don't have to feed me---though if you give me food, I'll take it. I eat pretty much anything.''

She said in a flat, emotionless tone. This time, it was our turn to widen our eyes.

We'd been prepared to some extent, given that the Biology Club had been reduced to its last remaining member. I'd talked with Mizasaki about how we'd manage somehow on our own. But we hadn't expected to receive no welcome at all, and for her to act so halfhearted---and then, right out of the gate, seem like she was about to dump the animal care on us.

Especially since I'd already gone and said ``Iwama'' out loud, I wanted to avoid stumbling here.

Without even waiting for our response, Karato-senpai briskly moved to the back of the room. From a cluttered area lined with aquariums and cages, she deftly carried the animals over, then set them on a long table, sorted by type.

``There are lots of creatures, but basically there are three things you do. Feed them, clean, and if needed, let them out. Well, only a few need to be let out, so don't worry about that for now.''

``Konnichiwa!''

``Yeah, hello, hello.''

After replying to the bird in the cage, Karato-senpai made a full circuit of checks---the sliding door at the entrance, the door leading to the prep room, the door to the yard, and the glass windows---then opened the cage. A beautiful light-blue-and-white parakeet flitted out and perched neatly on her sleeve. A budgerigar.

``This one's Rotgelb. Ro-chan for short. She's a girl, but she talks a lot, you see. When there are classes, Tokumura-sensei shuts her away in the prep room. At first we left her out even during class, but after we did the sea urchin development experiment, she started saying stuff like `sperm' and `egg cell' even during lectures. All right, say hello?''

``Egg cell\dots''

Too cruel.

``She's probably the smartest creature in this room. So play with her in moderation. But she can fly short distances, so you absolutely can't let her escape. When I'm not around, don't take her out of the cage on your own yet, okay. As for food and cleaning\dots\ it should be written on some paper somewhere, so, well, read that.''

\fullpageimage{5.jpg}


\thispagestyle{plain}

\vspace{1cm}

The care tutorial that had suddenly started. We were half dumbstruck, but watching the parakeet happily bob its head back and forth made it hard to feel bad about it.

``Um, senpai.''

It was Iwama who cut in.

``Hm? Ah, Ponytail-chan. Got a question?''

``Even though she's light blue, why is her name Rotgelb?''

I'd expected a question about the care tutorial, but it was a jab about the name. I'd thought the same thing when Tokumura-sensei introduced her. Rot is German for red, and Gelb is yellow. Nothing about it matched a light-blue budgerigar.

``Ahh, that. He's already dead, but a long time ago there was a male named Gelbgr\"{u}n---yellow-green. A yellow-and-green budgie. This one got named Rotgelb in reference to him.''

It didn't answer the question at all. Was it quoting something?

Apparently thinking she'd explained enough, senpai moved over to the next long table with the parakeet still perched on her sleeve.

``And this one is Jonouchi. Can you see him?''

She beckoned us over, and we drew closer. The cage was lined with finely shredded newspaper. Inside were a food dish, a water bottle, and a running wheel. Jonouchi himself was nowhere to be seen---maybe he was hiding under the newspaper.

``Jo-no-u-chi\dots?''

Iwama tilted her head, and Karato-senpai answered in a carefree tone.

``I think it means `inside the castle.' I don't know why it ended up with that name, though.''

I figured the wheel made it obvious, but I added it anyway.

``It's a hamster's name.''

``Ah, I see!''

``Right. A golden hamster. The greatest animal God ever created for the sake of pitiful animal lovers in the city. Cute, smart, and above all easy to keep. Oh, right---today I think I'll have you take care of this one.''

``Sounds good. I'll do it!''

Mizasaki looked completely fired up. It was the kind of moment where it'd be fine to look reluctant, but since Karato-senpai was also a girl, he was probably in peacock mode.

``Ohh, that's great. The care is simple---take Jonouchi out, clean the cage, put in shredded newspaper, and change the food. The details should be written on some paper somewhere. If you read that, you can probably handle most of the care yourselves. For the aquatic creatures, it's basically just feeding them. Of course there are filter changes and gravel replacement, but those only need to be done once in a while\dots''

Even if she explained it all out loud, I didn't feel like I'd remember it.

``And that paper\dots\ could we get a copy?''

When I said that, senpai and Rotgelb both looked at me with the exact same expression.

``\dots I thought it might go more smoothly if we saw that first.''

``Mm, you're completely right. Wait a sec---I'll just give you everything.''

As she spoke, senpai moved Rotgelb onto her shoulder and opened Jonouchi's cage. With practiced hands, she dug through the layered newspaper and plopped the hamster onto the long table.

Maybe he was still half asleep---the hamster sat back on his rear and looked around like a rice-ball fairy. Besides the brown that was close to gold, he had patches of black and white mixed in too, like a calico.

``I'm going to get the care papers, so watch this one. I guarantee you won't get bored.''

Senpai started to leave the long table just like that. Mizasaki hurriedly called out.

``Uh---sorry! Excuse me!''

``What is it, Brown Hair-kun?''

``Isn't it going to run off if you just leave it on top of the desk?''

Jounouchi seemed to have finally come to its senses and started trudging along the long table.

``No need to worry. Those little guys are smart---when they get close to the edge of a desk, they always turn back.''

Senpai went straight over to the cabinet at the back of the classroom and, with Rotgelb still perched on her shoulder, began rummaging through the drawers.

Since we'd been told to watch, we sat around the long table as if surrounding Jounouchi. The hamster scurrying around was cute. Though I figured I'd get bored after a few minutes.

``It's a handy habit, not getting off the desk. If you just do this, it won't escape, right?''

When Mizasaki said that with impressed admiration, Kannabi muttered,

``Like those pitiful high schoolers trapped inside the school grounds.''

What perspective even was that?

``Sometimes you feel like bursting out, but you absolutely can't burst out, right---school?''

I hadn't known Iwama had that kind of desire hidden inside her.

While muttering idly back and forth, we watched Jounouchi confined to the long table. It darted around, but sure enough, whenever it reached the edge, it immediately turned back. It must understand perfectly that falling would be dangerous. What a clever little guy.

``As for care, we'll split it up between us, but we also have to think about what we're doing for our main activities.''

When I started things off, Iwama was the first to nod.

``Like investigations and research---looking into what senpai did before us, and trying things out even if we start by imitating them.''

Iwama still hadn't submitted a membership form, and today was supposedly just a trial, but she was the most eager of all. The way she talked, it was like she already considered herself a member.

Mizasaki put a hand to his chin and put on an air of serious thought.

``With this many different living creatures around, we might even be able to do research using them. This is Tsunanagai High School---we're not just keeping them for no reason.''

``For hamsters, maze experiments are famous, right? Apparently their learning ability is pretty high.''

``A false labyrinth\dots''

Ignoring Kannabi's cryptic comment, I pointed out my own concern.

``If we're doing learning and cognition experiments, we might be able to use the budgerigar too. But there should only be two hamsters including Jounouchi, and there's only one budgerigar, so I feel like our n might be too small.''

``Maybe starting with a small sample as a trial is fine. Like, if all we do is measure learning speed within a single individual, repeating it a few times might still let us collect data that's meaningful to some extent.''

``Repeated causality\dots''

``Exactly!''

\dots Is it?

``Man, I'm really glad Gamma-san came today. It's reassuring.''

When Mizasaki suddenly started calling her by his own made-up nickname, Iwama reacted a beat late.

``Ah, me?''

``Yeah, you. I mean, if you do jukubako-yomi with Iwama-san, it becomes Gamma-san, right?''

``I see\dots\ so it's the same as Delta.''

``Delta isn't jukubako-yomi, though.''

Both ``deru'' and ``ta'' are kun-yomi.

``Come on, it's fine. Isn't it awesome? Our attendance numbers are adjacent, and our Greek letters are adjacent too.''

``That's true! Mizasaki-kun, you came up with that really well.''

``Heh-heh. Well, leave it to me.''

Iwama's deflection was impressive, as expected. Mizasaki clearly wanted to steer the conversation toward how fateful my meeting with Iwama was---but by praising Mizasaki's imagination here, Iwama cleverly dodged that path.

``But Gamma-san, why did you suddenly come today?''

Having been forced off the road he wanted, Mizasaki stepped right into the one place I least wanted him to touch.

It wasn't like I was trying to hide it at all costs. I just hadn't said anything yet. Still, what happened on Saturday was, for some reason, hard to bring up with Mizasaki. One reason was that he would definitely tease me about the fact that I went out with two girls, but more than anything, Saturday's outing was a tangled mess of Iwama and Kannabi's delicate relationship and my own delicate involvement in it---something that was, in any case, difficult to explain.

``\dots The liver.''

``The liver?''

Mizasaki cocked his head at what Kannabi said. I'd seen this flow somewhere before.

``Rio said she wanted to see a liver preserved in formalin, so I dragged her here.''

There was a brief silence.

``Did I say that\dots? Hm, I guess it's not like I didn't say it\dots''

She absolutely hadn't, but Mizasaki fully bought it and looked around at everyone.

``I don't see anything like that. Maybe it's in the prep room.''

With the conversation successfully diverted, I changed the subject.

``By the way, did they find the paper?''

All four of us looked toward the back of the classroom. Karato-senpai had her back to us as she stood in front of the cabinet, hands moving as she rummaged around in the drawer. Rotgelb on her shoulder looked bored, fiddling with senpai's hair.

``Should we go help look?''

When I called out, senpai jerked and turned back toward us. Rotgelb swayed cheerfully.

``Ah\dots\ no, yeah, it's fine. I found the one for the hammy.''

Senpai closed the drawer and came over to us. She set a single sheet of old-looking B5 paper on the desk.

``Sorryyy. I said I'd give you all of them, but it's kind of a mess, and I could only find this one. I'll keep looking for the rest little by little, so forgive me for today with just this, 'kay?''

I looked at the paper she'd set down. It was a handwritten memo on loose-leaf paper. Using colored pens in moderation and even mixing in illustrations, it carefully laid out the steps for taking care of a hamster. The date was April, four years ago. It was probably something senpai's senpai had put together. Maybe from being used so much, the edges were a little ragged. In the upper right, the words ``No taking this out!'' written in red felt-tip pen stood out.

``A secret recipe passed down through the ages. Really, we probably should've made it into computer data and all, but I'm suuuper bad at, like, digital? stuff\dots\ So, how about we try doing it together with this big sis today? Basically it's just like the manual says, but there're all sorts of tips and tricks.''

I felt a flicker of discomfort. It wasn't because of the way she said ``and tricks.'' I already knew senpai liked quirky ways of talking. It was more like\dots\ her whole vibe felt a little different.

Up until now, she'd seemed like an unmotivated part-timer handing off work, but now she finally felt more like a senpai\dots\ or rather, for some reason, she suddenly seemed kinder.

Senpai gently stroked Jounouchi with a fingertip, then went to fetch some newspaper.

``Okay, first we start by tearing up newspaper. Oh---Megane-kun, can you help clean too?''

And so, the five of us got to work taking care of Jounouchi.

Maybe because she'd been looking after them all this time, senpai's technique was excellent, and her instructions were spot-on.

Deciding the four of us could handle it, senpai started cleaning the cage for Rokujogawara---the other hamster---on the desk next to us partway through.

``They start bloody fights right away, so when you let them out, put Rokujogawara on a different desk from Jounouchi, okay?''

Saying that, she took Rokujogawara out of its cage too. Rokujogawara was a mouse-colored hamster, a bit smaller than Jounouchi. Was ``mouse-colored'' an inappropriate way to describe a hamster?

``A mouse-colored hamster\dots''

I heard Kannabi mutter it under her breath, and I felt embarrassed that our thought processes were the same.

Everything we needed to do was written in the care manual, and with senpai's instructions being so appropriate, we finished the necessary care without incident. While senpai fed the other creatures, we were tasked with giving snacks to Jounouchi and Rokujogawara.

``Don't give them too many seeds, 'kay? They're high in fat and they'll get chubby. They don't really eat it at all, but hamster food is best.''

It struck me as odd that hamsters wouldn't like hamster food, but senpai was right. When we handed it over, they'd hold it for the moment, but they quickly got bored and left it behind.

Karato-senpai finished up a full round of work briskly, and she put Rotgelb---who'd been riding on her shoulder like a pirate's partner the whole time---back in its cage.

``Yokatanee.''

``Yeeeah, Roo-chan, yokatanee, huh?''

Despite the meaning of its words, Rotgelb seemed dissatisfied, as if it still wanted to be outside, muttering grumpily. But senpai locked the cage door with a carabiner clip.

``Man, I'm really glad. That you all came.''

Karato-senpai looked at us with a beaming smile as she spoke.

``I don't think I have any lingering attachment to this club. Prosperity must give way to decline---there's a reason it's gotten run-down. But, you know\dots\ people can quit the Biology Club, but these kids can't quit.''

Rotgelb clung to the cage as if it wanted to be touched by Karato-senpai. Jounouchi and Rokujogawara scampered around on top of the long table. Tortoise Henry Huxley (the Japanese pond turtle) splashed its water noisily, looking like it wanted something.

``\dots Um,''

Iwama began.

``Have you ever recorded anything---observations or behavioral experiments---using these animals?''

``Hoh. Experiments?''

``If we're only taking care of living creatures, it won't function as a club activity. Besides how to keep them, I want to know what kinds of activities you were doing.''

She said it. Well done. We joined the Biology Club---we didn't sign up to be the animal-care committee.

Senpai thought for a moment, then clapped her hands.

``Ah, right---now that you mention it, I just found something like this.''

Heading back to the cabinet at the rear of the classroom, senpai brought back a wooden board about the size of a go board.

On top of the board, thin wooden pieces stood upright, forming a maze of twisting passages. A clear acrylic plate was fixed over the top. A maze. A small-animal one---a false labyrinth.

``That a hamster maze, or what?''

``It is, Brown Hair-kun. A hammy's learning ability is no joke---if it's Jounouchi, I bet it can still clear it without getting lost.''

Senpai set the maze down on the long table we'd been gathered around. Jounouchi, which had been wandering around on the desk, started showing interest in the maze.

``Oh, let's have it try.''

Senpai scooped Jounouchi up and placed it at the entrance. But maybe because it was so old, part of the maze's outer wall clumsily toppled over right then. Jounouchi toddled off in the opposite direction of the maze.

``Welp, yeah. That won't do, huh.''

Opening a shelf along the side of the classroom, senpai brought over a roll of duct tape. Mizasaki took it, frowned slightly, set it beside the maze, and peered at us through the hole in the tube.

``There's barely anything left on this.''

I see---the tape's almost gone. It's basically trash. There's obviously not enough left to repair anything.

``Sorryyy. That's probably all that's left in the biology room. We'd have to get some from Tokumura-sensei or the student council.''

Well, there was no need to force the maze today. The reward would be food, but we'd already given Jounouchi quite a few snacks, so its motivation to clear a maze was probably low too.

Just as I was thinking that, the sliding door at the biology room entrance clattered. It was locked and wouldn't open, and it was rattled again.

``Ah---please wait a second!''

Senpai hurried over to the entrance at a quick trot. The sound of unlocking. The door slid open.

``Hm, first-years? Sorryyy---earlier we had the budgerigar out, so I locked it so it wouldn't open. Go on in, go on in.''

I was an idiot for getting even a little curious, thinking it might be a new prospective member.

The ones who came in were faces I knew well. Watari---the guy who'd mocked us in the physics room during the club fair. Then two more. Oosawa, a skinny guy who'd apparently dyed his hair a bright blond since entering high school, and Fujito, still sporting the same scruffy black hair and thick glasses as ever. The three of them had always hung out together since middle school, and all three were in the Physics Club; they were on terrible terms with Mizasaki and me, even though we'd been in the Chemistry Club.

Mizasaki often calls them the ``Ototo Trio,'' taking syllables from each of their surnames. It sounds stupid, so I like it too.

I'd heard all three had already submitted their membership forms to the Physics Club. They were probably here to heckle us.

``Are you interested in the Biology Club?''

Watari shook his head curtly at Karato-senpai, who was trying to welcome them kindly without knowing anything.

``No---we're Physics Club. Since we're off today, Mikage-senpai told us to come observe other clubs. The Chemistry Club and the Biology Club.''

``Ah\dots\ I seeee.''

What a bunch of shameless jerks. They walked right past senpai and came over to where we were gathered around Jounouchi.

``Ugh, mice?''

Fujito (black hair, glasses) flinched back when he saw Rokujogawara.

``It's not a mouse. It's a hamster.''

With Iwama, Kannabi, and senpai here, I didn't want to get into it, but I blurted it out anyway.

``Same thing in the end. I'm just bad with mice and stuff.''

Then don't come into the biology room, Doraemon---was what I thought, but with the girls here, I didn't say it out loud.

Watari (brown hair, long) shot a glance at Fujito, who was keeping his distance from the long table, then walked over toward us.

``Got tired of messing with test tubes, and now you've started playing with the hammy, huh, Delta?''

``It's not playing. Today we're just being taught how to take care of them. If you're not interested, then go home and go interfere with waves or something.''

I snapped back without thinking, and the air in the biology room turned prickly.

``What's wrong with that? I came to observe, for friendship.''

Watari did not look remotely friendly. Looking bored, he casually reached out like he was going to touch Jounouchi.

``\dots It bites.''

Kannabi spoke up out of nowhere.

``That hamster---once it bites your finger, it won't let go until you die.''

Like a snapping turtle.

But it worked instantly. Watari yanked his hand back like he was scared. Then, maybe because he felt embarrassed, he swept his gaze around the biology room with the same delinquent eyes as always.

``Animals everywhere.''

``Well, yeah. It's the biology room. It'd be weird if there were tuning forks in here.''

Ignoring Mizasaki's quip, Watari glared at me. That look meant he had something he wanted to say to me---the look he gives when he thinks he's doing you a favor by opening his mouth, even though nobody asked.

``Did you know, Delta? There's a reason the Biology Club has gotten this run-down.''

I couldn't believe he'd say that with the Biology Club's senpai right there, but I was interested in this ``reason.'' I didn't interrupt---just glared back at Watari.

``Four years ago, even when activities got tightly restricted, someone still had to keep taking care of the animals. It's the short straw. New members plummeted, and even when people joined, apparently it was only animal lovers. As a result, the Biology Club got mocked as an animal-lovers club, and the crowd that used to come to the Biology Club flowed elsewhere.''

Only after rattling all that off did Watari seem to remember Karato-senpai was there.

He probably heard it from Mikage-senpai. It was a convincing story. But even so, it wasn't something to say here. Out of the corner of my eye, I saw Karato-senpai shift slightly, but I couldn't bring myself to look directly at her.

I opened my mouth to retort, but Watari stopped me with a hand.

``Join whatever club you want\dots\ but don't become a boring person.''

Maybe that was all he needed to say. Satisfied, he turned on his heel and left.

None of your business.

Fujito looked like he wanted to get away from the small animals as fast as possible, and he headed for the exit first. Oosawa (skinny blond), who'd been silent the whole time, dipped his head awkwardly and followed after him.

Then that blond head vanished from my field of view.

Almost at the same time, a loud crash rang out from around there. It sounded like a bunch of glassware shattering. We sprang up and rushed over.

Oosawa had fallen. He's a clumsy type who trips easily when he walks without watching his feet, so that much was whatever---but it looked like he'd stumbled into a bucket filled with broken glassware. A light-blue bucket labeled ``Non-burnable Trash'' had tipped over, scattering shards all over the floor.

``Owww, owowowow\dots''

``Hey, what the hell are you doing, idiot.''

Watari, looking exasperated, lent a hand and helped Oosawa to his feet. Oosawa rubbed both hands like they hurt.

``Are you okay\dots? You didn't touch the glass, did you?''

Kind-hearted Iwama was the first to rush over.

``Ah---uh, n-no\dots\ probably. S-sorry.''

With Iwama in front of him, Oosawa's ears turned bright red, and he started stammering. What an easy guy to read.

I went over to the mess of scattered glass and set the toppled bucket upright.

``\dots Make sure you clean it up.''

``Yeah, I know that.''

After replying irritably, Watari turned back toward Karato-senpai.

``Sorry. Can we borrow a broom?''

``A broom\dots? Sure. There's one in the cleaning supplies locker in the back.''

Watari and Oosawa went to get a broom from the locker senpai pointed to. While they did, Fujito, Mizasaki, and I picked up the larger shards of glass. Iwama crouched down beside me as well.

``Ah---no, no, no. Gamma-san and you guys don't have to. We'll handle it.''

``No. Let me help.''

In the end, Kannabi joined too, and then Watari and Oosawa came back with a broom and dustpan, and the cleanup was over in no time. Fujito, with duct tape wrapped around his hand, finished by patting up the tiny fragments.

Until the three from the Ototo Trio hurried out and the door clattered shut, Karato-senpai stood there as if stunned.

``I'm sorry. They were my classmates back in middle school\dots\ they're the type who say stuff like that without feeling bad about it.''

Mizasaki bowed his head to senpai. Only then did senpai seem to come back to herself, shaking her head.

``No---mm-mm, it's fine. Survival of the fittest. And it's all true\dots\ sorryyy.''

``Don't apologize.''

``I'm kind of tired. Let's call it a day and go home.''

Thanks to the Ototo Trio, the mood was awful. Nobody objected.

Well, maybe this was just how the first day went.

We started to put things away---

and then we realized Jounouchi was gone.

``I'm sure one of those three took him!''

Karato-senpai didn't shout, but her tone had sharpened.

He wasn't in the maze. He hadn't gone back into his cage.

In the moment we'd looked away, Jounouchi had neatly evaporated from the top of the long table.

``Couldn't he have fallen?''

I had no choice but to raise the objection.

``They're awful people, but they're not the type to do something that thoughtless.''

``Jounouchi doesn't fall off the edge of a desk. Not ever. I'd know best. And besides---I don't know you well enough to swallow your evaluation of those three without question.''

Fair point. As I hesitated over my words, Iwama spoke up.

``Um\dots\ is it possible he got startled by the noise? The bucket with the glass fell over, and it made a loud sound. If he reacted to that sound and started running, maybe he fell off the desk\dots''

``No. That's not it. When a hamster senses an unseen threat, it freezes first and looks around. If it runs off at full speed, it'll get spotted by a predator right away and eaten, won't it.''
