She said it with a mischievous grin and took out a key. After showing it to me, she unlocked the door.

``You keep it locked even though it's your own house?''

``Yeah. It's my hobby room---only I can go in. Even my parents can't.''

Since she was an only child, they probably had a spare room, but letting their daughter have a private space like that---her parents really were something. Meanwhile, all I had was my own room, where I never knew when my little sister might sneak in.

As Iwama moved to open the door, I confirmed it with her.

``Are you sure it's okay for me to see a place like that?''

``I called you because I want you to see it today.''

When I saw beyond the door she pushed open, I was left speechless.

All the ``lived-in'' feeling that seemed to have been removed from her bedroom had been compressed and stuffed into this small room. No---at this point, it probably couldn't even be called ``lived-in.''

If anything, it was a workshop.

Along the wall stood a large metal rack, lined with baskets neatly packed with all sorts of items: tools, materials like wooden boards and acrylic sheets, electronics supplies like enamel wire and a soldering iron, bottles of chemicals of questionable legality, a collapsible telescope, and more. Sheets like polarizing film and Fresnel lenses were stacked together off to one side, and anything too large to fit on the rack was propped in the corner of the room. On the floor was a plastic storage case crammed full of files of pressed-leaf specimens. A simple desk and chair sat at the very back, but judging by how an in-progress project was spread out on the floor, the desk seemed to be mainly for using the PC placed on it.

If the police saw this room, they'd probably say they wanted to take a look around.

Hearing ``a girl's room even her parents don't know about'' and letting my imagination run wild for even a second was my mistake. There was no way she'd have flowers and dolls on display. If this room reflected Iwama's inner world, then of course it would turn out exactly like this.

``\dots That's incredible.''

Taking my one stunned remark as a straightforward compliment, she---

``Right?!''

---beamed and beckoned me inside.

To make maximum use of the space she'd been given, the walls were covered with posters---world maps, the periodic table, phylogenetic trees---packed in edge to edge. As we talked while I picked up and examined all sorts of surprising items, I noticed a large empty space on the wall beside the rack.

There was only one framed piece displayed there. If I'd assumed there was no way this room had anything like flowers, I had to revise that.

A plant mounted on white Kent paper---a pressed-leaf specimen.

Most of them were kept in the storage case, but this one alone was displayed like a work of art.

``This is the one you gave me, Del-chan. Do you remember?''

Noticing where my eyes had gone, Iwama said it, looking a little embarrassed.

The collection date read April 9, 2024. No matter how you looked at it, it was the one I'd handed Iwama on that spring day right after we'd started school---when I'd been carried away.

``So you treated it this seriously.''

``It's a flower with memories\dots\ and if I do this, I can see it every day.''

There was no mistaking it. It was katakuri.

It had been about half a year since---near the end of my third-year summer---I'd received the pressed katakuri from Iwama. I couldn't say I'd never thought about what it meant. Back then, Iwama had said it was a return gift. There was a pretty big time lag between before club activities started and after they ended, but well\dots\ it was a return gift, all the same.

``Enduring loneliness.''

I tried murmuring it under my breath. The katakuri's flower language---what Iwama had told me.

``\dots You remembered.''

``Of course.''

That was what it meant, right? I asked the framed pressed specimen on the wall.

``A katakuri that stores up nourishment for eight years before it blooms was, for you, Iwama, a symbol of finally being able to bring out your own strength. And you gave it to me after club ended because\dots\ well\dots''

I couldn't find the right words. But Iwama looked at me and smiled.

``I can take it as a message that you were glad you joined the Biology Club, right?''

After a moment of silence---as if she were thinking---Iwama nodded.

``Yeah\dots\ something like that.''

Iwama's fingers fussed with her hair. It looked like she was searching for words too.

``After joining the Biology Club, I\dots\ I was happy.''

``I see\dots\ I'm glad.''

``Happy'' was a pretty grand word.

I wanted to believe it had nothing to do with what I'd once blurted out to that so-called ringleader back then.

``It all went by so fast. I didn't think it'd turn into such a fulfilling club experience.''

``Yeah. If I could go back, I'd go back again. It was short, but I'm really glad I got to do club activities with everyone.''

The katakuri from three years ago bloomed on the wall, still wrapped in the memories of that day. Iwama said she'd been looking at it almost every day. I, too, often found myself staring at the pressed flower I'd received from her in the breaks between exam prep.

The youth that began on that spring day now came quietly to an end, like this.

Every time I looked at the katakuri, I found myself thinking: was spring really this short?

``\dots So you gave me the pressed flower on purpose, just to tell me that.''

``Sorry. It was hard to understand.''

``That wasn't a very scientific way to convey it.''

Iwama laughed at what I said.

``Even if there's a scientific component\dots\ flower language still isn't really suited to sending messages to people, is it? It can get taken the wrong way too.''

``Yeah. And flower language isn't exactly something that's been compiled into a paper, either. What would you even cite as the primary source---what kind of literature would count as the original text?''

``Oh, well, that's\dots!''

Iwama looked like she was about to start hunting down references in earnest, so I gently stopped her.

I swear there was nothing going on, but since it would be awkward if Iwama's parents came home, I left early. The days were getting longer, but it was already after sunset.

Under the white streetlights, I walked through the residential area along the Ebiwakagawa. Before long, I slipped into the shopping arcade.

I thought the reason I stopped in front of a florist---rare for me---was because I'd just chatted a bit with Iwama about flower language. There were blue lupines that looked like wisteria blossoms turned upside down.
{\bfseries

Lupines just came in!

The flower language is said to include ``always happy,'' ``you are my peace,'' ``imagination,'' ``greed,'' and more♪

}

A handwritten pop card in felt-tip pen had been stuck into the display.

I'd heard that lupines were highly capable of pulling nutrients up from the soil. If that was true, then ``greed'' made sense---but that still left me wondering what ``always happy'' was even supposed to mean. Did flower language really come with this many entries?

I thought about it for a while.

Was the flower language Iwama wanted to convey really supposed to be ``enduring loneliness''?

To be honest, I'd felt all along that it didn't quite sit right.

Ideally, I should have checked a written source closer to a primary reference---if possible, looked it up in an actual book---but right now, my urge to know won out. I pulled out my phone and quickly typed into the search bar.

Katakuri flower language

The results appeared almost immediately. I scrolled and checked multiple entries.

``\dots''

I hurriedly put my phone away and quickly turned back.

% ==============================
%        Afterword
% ==============================
\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Afterword
\end{center}
\label{sec:afterword}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Afterword}
\vspace{1cm}

Back when I was an elementary schooler who had just started writing stories, becoming a novelist was a dream within a dream. I loved both reading and writing, but for some reason, I never imagined I could turn it into my job. Maybe it belonged in the same category as becoming an astronaut or a pro baseball player (or these days, a YouTuber).

Even so, in a vague sort of way, I thought I wanted to become a researcher.

I loved playing in nature. I loved reading field guides and going to museums. The idea that the trees, insects, and pebbles you find anywhere all have their own types and underlying logic---and that smart adults investigate those things, compile their findings, and if they're lucky, even make them useful---must have felt fascinating to me as a child.

Most of my specific memories from that time have faded, but for some reason I still remember visiting the science room during lunch breaks in elementary school. I can vaguely recall his face, but I can no longer remember his name: an elderly teacher with glasses who taught science. During lunch, he'd have a group of volunteer students take turns reading the textbook aloud, and once they finished, he'd hand out small rewards---litmus paper, medaka fish eggs, and other little things. I don't think I cared about the reading; I suspect I went purely because I wanted those rewards. With the litmus paper I received, I tested the pH of every liquid around me, and I hatched the medaka eggs and raised them for several generations. Looking back, it might be a bit questionable from a compliance standpoint, but if that teacher hadn't existed, I might not be who I am today.

In middle school, I joined the Science Club. It was called the Science Club, but in practice, it was the Biology Club. Since my school was an integrated middle-and-high program, we did activities together with the high school Biology Club. I was also in the judo club, so I couldn't participate very often, but on days off we'd investigate aquatic insects in the Tama River, or observe flying squirrels at night on Mount Takao. That's where teachers and upperclassmen taught me how fun fieldwork could be.

Following that path almost inevitably, I chose biology in high school and entered the Faculty of Science as a biology major in university. I wasn't interested in whatever biotech was trendy at the time---I simply wanted to learn about living things---so I chose a plant ecology lab where I could pursue that. There were times I took a cannon-like lens out to Ishigaki Island to photograph wild birds, and times I stayed at a botanical garden for nearly two months to survey Japanese deer. I loved biology, I loved science, and I was completely absorbed in those years.

Science is mystery-solving on the stage of the real world. It is the work of explaining and conveying truths you have uncovered yourself in a form that anyone can be convinced by. It's hard to find anything as enjoyable as that.

Now, after going on at such length about my past, I apologize for what comes next---but in truth, I did not choose the path of becoming a researcher. Academia is a thorny road, and I felt it would be difficult to make a living with my abilities. I still loved writing stories, and I also thought that after graduating, I might be better suited to work that communicates the significance and fascination of science to people. So I joined a mass media company where I could stay involved with nature and science---and then, unbelievably, I received the Gold Prize in the Dengeki Novel Award for a novel I had submitted while I was in university, became a novelist, and ended up where I am now.

What I wanted to convey by spending these pages is simply this (sorry for the long-windedness): this book is the thing I most want to put out into the world right now.

Even in someone like me, there is a small flame I received from a great giant.

The elementary school teacher who ``diverted'' supplies to me, my middle-and-high mentor who---for some reason---served as advisor for both the Science Club and the Judo Club and supported me constantly, my university professors who showed me the view at the cutting edge of biology, and the researchers in Aomori who gladly cooperated with me after I began working---my flame was nurtured by many teachers. It still burns within me, and I don't want to let it go out.

I still haven't repaid them.

For a long time, I kept thinking about whether there was some way---my own way---to convey the appeal of science, biology, and ecology. Now that I'm able to make a living as a novelist, I feel that I've finally been able to attempt pouring that passion into a book.

Of course, this novel isn't anything stiff or formal (if anything, it's the opposite), so there's no need to prepare candles. I'll be happy if you can simply enjoy it as a story. Still, my hope is that the fascination of science, as I've depicted it in my own way, might help nurture someone else's flame.

Finally, please allow me to express my gratitude once again.

First and foremost, to everyone who picked up this book. Some of you may have been reading since my previous series, Pig's Liver Must Be Heated. (It's been a while!) The reason I'm able to keep writing books like this is thanks to all of you. Thank you, truly.

To my editors, Anan-san and Motoyama-san, who have continued working with me since Pig's Liver Must Be Heated as well: when I'm writing what I love, my pen tends to run out of control, so your many pieces of advice were a huge help.

To Tosaka Asagi-sensei, who is also continuing as the illustrator from Pig's Liver Must Be Heated: even with a stage that's about 180 degrees different from a world of swords and magic, you still gave me wonderful illustrations as always, and it makes me very happy.

To Aozaki Yugo-sensei, Kono Yutaka-sensei, and Konno Tenryu-sensei for your recommendation comments: although this work is a light novel, receiving such warm words from authors active in mystery and literary fiction was incredibly encouraging.

And to the many people involved in publishing, distribution, and sales of this book: my deepest gratitude.

Now, since I've labeled the title with ``1,'' I intend for this story to continue as a series. If ``The Science Notes $\delta$ and $\gamma$'' is too long, please feel free to call it something like ``the Faculty of Science series.'' I'll do my best to search for mentions, so if you'd like, I'd be delighted if you posted your thoughts somewhere. Of course, I'll also be grateful to read letters!

Volume 2 is planned to be mainly about Golden Week for the Biology Club members. Their first investigation as a club will be an overnight trip. Together, they'll close in on the mystery of a tengu legend passed down at a shrine. (Scientifically!)

Then, I'll see you again in Volume 2!
\begin{flushright}
  October 2024 --- Sakai Takuma  
\end{flushright}

\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Bonus Chapter 1\\
        Sakura Hue Beneath the Water
\end{center}
\label{sec:bonus}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Bonus Chapter 1}
\vspace{1cm}

Mizasaki and Kannabi seemed like polar opposites in personality, but they actually shared one very obvious trait---both had a bad habit of making sure Iwama and I ended up alone together.

Today, after club activities, I once again fell victim to their scheming and found myself walking home with Iwama.

Kindhearted as she was, Iwama seemed to genuinely believe their obvious lies: ``I've got an online meeting,'' or ``I need to go buy something secret.'' But I wasn't so easily fooled. While the secret purchase might have been plausible, there was no way Mizasaki would ever have an online meeting to attend.

In any case, on this road home with just the two of us, Iwama suddenly spoke up.

``I heard a really curious story from a friend.''

I murmured in acknowledgment as I gazed at the school route dyed in the colors of the setting sun. Iwama had to head to the station, while my house was in the city center, so normally we would part ways at the shopping street up ahead. But if what she had to say today turned out to be a long story, I might have no choice but to walk her all the way to the station.

``At a construction site, they said, there was this little bottle with a yellow light glowing at the bottom.''

``You mean there was something like a liquid inside that was glowing?''

``Yeah, yeah. They said the transparent liquid was giving off a yellow light, but only in the lower half.''

``Only the lower half...?''

At first, I thought it might have been coated with luminous paint or something, but that didn't seem to be the case. It wasn't going to be that easy to explain. I gave up on the idea of turning off at the shopping street.

``We need more details first. Was there a label on the little bottle?''

``No. Nothing was written on it, apparently.''

``Since it was at a construction site, it must have been outdoors, right? And if the glow was visible, that means it was nighttime?''

Iwama nodded.

``It was a pitch-dark, unmanned construction site, apparently.''

A pitch-dark, unmanned construction site, and yet a bottle was glowing. And on top of that, only the lower half of the liquid was emitting light. It was a bit hard to picture.

``...Why was your friend near a construction site at night? Was the bottle glowing brightly enough to be seen from a distance?''

``Well, you see---ah, Del-chan, you might know about this---he said there was this light installed at the site that made a really loud crackling sound, and he went closer to see what was going on, thinking something might have happened.''

Iwama looked at me expectantly, as if waiting for my answer. It seemed she wanted to test my knowledge.

``That light---did it have a bluish tint to it?''

``Yes, it did!''

``That's an insect killer lamp. It's a device that attracts insects with light and then electrocutes them. Every time a bug flies into it, it makes that loud crackling sound.''

``Yes, yes! And that glowing bottle was placed on a table right under the insect killer lamp.''

``In that case... we can narrow things down.''

We kept talking, and before I knew it, we were almost at the station. The clues were gradually coming together, but I still hadn't arrived at an answer. It would be too frustrating if the mystery remained unsolved right when we were on the verge of cracking it.

``I'm getting a bit hungry.''

I used that line as an attempt to buy a little more time.

``Then let's check out that sweet shop! I've been curious about it for a while!''

Iwama pointed to an old-fashioned confectionery tucked away in a corner of the shopping street. It looked a bit upscale, and I'd never been inside before, but they seemed to sell items individually, so it probably wouldn't be too expensive. Using Iwama as my excuse, I stepped into the shop---slightly too refined for a high school student---and browsed the sweets in the glass case.

What caught my eye most was the shop's recommended item: sakura-mochi. They had two styles: the domyoji type, which kept the grains of glutinous rice intact in a round shape, and the Kanto-style type, with a thin wheat-flour crepe wrapped around the filling.

Though we hadn't planned it, Iwama bought the domyoji style, and I bought the Kanto style.

With our sweets wrapped simply in paper, we headed to a small park just outside the shopping street and sat down on a bench. The terrain of this city sloped downward toward the sea, and from the park, we could faintly see the ocean illuminated by the evening sun.

I immediately took a bite of my sakura-mochi. The moderate sweetness and the chewy texture were delightful. But the most enchanting part was the fragrance of the salt-pickled cherry blossom leaf. Although the actual cherry blossoms themselves have almost no scent, when it comes to ``sakura flavor,'' nearly every Japanese person thinks of this sweet aroma.

It's delicious, so good---we exchanged our impressions. I idly thought to myself that, since I wasn't here with Mizasaki, there was no way we'd trade bites like that... but seeing that Iwama seemed curious about the Kanto-style flavor too, I suggested, ``Next time, let's try the one we haven't eaten yet.'' Then we finally returned to the main topic.

``First, regarding the glowing liquid---I think it must be some kind of fluorescent substance. There are liquids that glow on their own, but the chemical energy required for that kind of luminescence is limited, and if left alone, it will eventually fade. The fact that it was still glowing at that unmanned construction site means its energy source came from outside.''

Fluorescence is a phenomenon in which a substance absorbs a high-energy photon, becomes excited, and then emits a photon of lower energy. For instance, if you shine ultraviolet light on a fluorescent marker or on laundry detergent that contains fluorescent whitening agents, they glow---that's a classic example.

``So then, this external energy source must be...''

``The insect killer lamp!''

``That's what I was thinking too. Insects that fly toward the sky during the day exhibit phototaxis and are particularly drawn to ultraviolet light. A lot of insect killer lamps take advantage of that ultraviolet light feature.''

---Some substance in that bottle was fluorescing under the UV light of the insect killer lamp.

``Next, the real question is what exactly was inside that little bottle... I can't say for sure yet. But we do have clues. The fact that only the bottom of the liquid was glowing. That means either a precipitate was glowing, or the liquid had separated into layers---like water and oil---one on top of the other.''

If it was a precipitate, then some kind of glowing substance had settled at the bottom. Or, if it was a case of liquids of different densities separating into layers, like water and oil, then only the layer with the greater specific gravity was glowing.

``Hmm... I remember my friend describing it as the liquid itself glowing, or at least that's the impression he gave.''

``Then it's probably liquid layering... By the way, did he take any photos?''

``He said he didn't.''

If it had been Iwama, she would definitely have taken a picture. But in reality, most people wouldn't go out of their way to photograph something just because it seemed a little strange.

``Then we'll have to rely on deduction. A fluorescent liquid that glows yellow, and one that might be found at a construction site...''

Suddenly, a tool popped into my mind---a spirit level, the kind that contains fluorescent liquid and uses an air bubble to check whether something is horizontal. But taking that liquid out and putting it into a small bottle would be far too unnatural.

As I slowly nibbled my sakura-mochi and pondered, my gaze unconsciously fell upon the sweet in my hand.

``Ah, I think I might know the answer.''

``Really?''

``The cause of the glow might be coumarin.''

``Wait, coumarin? You mean... the one in cherry blossom leaves?''

As expected, Iwama caught on fast. I nodded.

``Coumarin is the main aromatic component of pickled cherry blossom leaves, but it actually has other uses too.''

``For plants, it's used to defend against fungi, insects, and other plants.''

``That's right. But this time it's not about plants---it's about a human use. As a fluorescent substance.''

``Coumarin fluoresces too?''

Iwama looked at the domyoji sakura-mochi in her hand with an expression of surprise, as if she'd just discovered a hidden talent of the sweet.

To supplement my vague memory, I looked up a relevant paper on my phone. I quickly found what I was looking for, and it was clearly written.

``To be precise, it's a derivative of coumarin---a substance formed when it undergoes hydrolysis in an alkaline environment. By taking advantage of its fluorescent properties, coumarin is actually used quite often in our daily lives. For example, in kerosene.''

``There's coumarin in kerosene too? ...But why? Not for fragrance, right?''

If oil heaters gave off the scent of cherry blossoms, that would be rather romantic, but that wasn't the point.

``Have you ever heard of illegal diesel? Diesel and kerosene have similar properties, but because of tax differences, kerosene is cheaper. So some people mix kerosene into diesel to cut costs. That's illegal fuel. Coumarin is added to kerosene precisely to distinguish it from diesel.''

``So that way, in situations where diesel must be used, they can check whether it's been mixed with kerosene by detecting the fluorescence---''

``Yes. There's a detection method where you add alkali and alcohol to diesel, shake it, and mix it. If kerosene has been mixed in, the coumarin inside undergoes hydrolysis and dissolves into the water layer. Then, with the oil layer on top and the water layer on the bottom, you'll see a yellow fluorescence glowing at the bottom.''

``That's incredible... so scientific!''

Right at that moment, we also happened to finish our sakura-mochi.

With that sense of satisfaction that comes from solving a mystery, we left the park together. Before I realized it, I had already walked Iwama all the way to the station. In truth, the conversation had already ended; there had been no need to go that far out of my way.

``Well then, see you tomorrow!''

``See you tomorrow.''

I parted ways with Iwama at the ticket gate in the twilight and quickened my pace homeward.

A few days later, Iwama went out of her way to bring me a newspaper clipping. On it was a report that a construction company responsible for a site in Ebiwakagawa City had been caught using illegal diesel.

\clearpage
\thispagestyle{plain}
\phantomsection
\begin{center}
    \large\bfseries
    Bonus Chapter 2\\
        Izuta and Iwama's Science Trivia Corne
\end{center}
\label{sec:b}
\addcontentsline{toc}{chapter}{Bonus Chapter 2}
\vspace{1cm}

``Hello, dear readers! Today, I'd like to take this opportunity to explain some of the trivia that appeared in Volume 1. Scientifically, of course!''

``Hold on, Iwama. Who exactly are these `readers'? Why have you suddenly started narrating into thin air?''

``I'll leave the assistant and straight-man roles to Del-chan. Since the content contains spoilers, I think it's best if you come back and listen to this commentary after you've finished reading Volume 1!''

\textit{(What exactly \textit{is} Volume 1, anyway\dots?)}

``Let's start with Chapter 2. I'll explain the magic trick that Hongo-senpai showed to Del-chan and Mizasaki-kun.''

``You mean the one where she shook a plastic bottle of `barley tea' and it instantly turned transparent. But how do you even know about that, Iwama, when you weren't there?''

``This is a special universe.''

``So we're going with a multiverse premise now\dots''

``Well then, the plastic barley tea bottle that Hongo-senpai used at the time still had its label on. But the liquid inside was actually an iodine-based mouthwash---the kind you can buy at any pharmacy---dissolved in water.''

``Keeping the label on was a clever touch. Anyone would subconsciously assume a brown liquid labeled `barley tea' was the real thing.''

``Exactly! If you actually try this yourself, though, please be \textit{very} careful that no one unknowingly drinks it by mistake.''

``It'd be a disaster if someone gulped down mouthwash thinking it was barley tea.''

``Japanese people eat so much seaweed that they rarely need extra iodine supplementation anyway!''

``Is \textit{that} really the issue here\dots?''

``The trick itself is simple. Vitamin C powder was applied to the inside of the plastic bottle cap beforehand. You can buy that at a pharmacy, too. If you're just doing this experiment, you can use the powder from inside a vitamin C capsule, or crush a vitamin C tablet and use that.''

``You know an awful lot of detail. Have you actually tried this yourself?''

``Of course I have! First, dissolve the mouthwash in the bottle until the water turns a barley-tea-like color. Then lightly dampen the inside of the cap and smear on a tiny amount of vitamin C powder. Finally, gently screw the vitamin-C-coated cap back on, and the preparation is complete. This step requires careful handling!''

``If any vibration makes the vitamin C fall off beforehand, the color will disappear before the performance even begins.''

``Right, right! After that, all you have to do is call over whoever you want to see the trick, and shake the bottle right in front of them. Once the vitamin C dissolves, the brown-tinted water from the iodine mouthwash turns transparent.''

``That's a redox reaction. The vitamin C acts as a reducing agent, donating electrons to the colored elemental iodine and turning it into colorless iodide ions.''

``C\textsubscript{6}H\textsubscript{8}O\textsubscript{6} + I\textsubscript{2} $\rightarrow$ C\textsubscript{6}H\textsubscript{6}O\textsubscript{6} + 2I\textsuperscript{-} + 2H\textsuperscript{+}! The vitamin C loses two hydrogen atoms.''

``How on earth did you just pronounce that reaction equation out loud?''

``Next, I'll explain the shear-thickening experiment using potato starch! This one also appeared in Chapter 2 of Volume 1---Mikage-senpai demonstrated it for Del-chan and Mizasaki-kun.''

``You mean the one with the electric massager.''

``Wait, is that what it was\dots?''

``So you know the demonstration but not the tool. It was my first time seeing one too, but apparently an electric massager is a medical device that applies fine vibrations to the human body.''

``Oh, I see. So that's what it was. Still, that topic is a bit \textit{that}, so let's not go any deeper this time.''

``A bit \textit{what}, exactly?''

``All you need is potato starch and water. The ratio doesn't have to be precise---just try it with the starch volume a little greater than the water volume.''

\textit{(I'm being strangely ignored\dots)}

``Simply put, a shear-thickening fluid has the property of being soft when you apply force slowly, but hardening when force is applied suddenly.''

``It really is fascinating how counterintuitive the reaction is when you actually touch it.''

``It's addictive! In a resting state, the starch particles in the water are densely packed, and the gaps between particles are minimized. But when force is applied suddenly, the particles try to deform and move, and the gaps try to expand as well. Only, those gaps are already completely filled with water---''

``I see. Water's volume doesn't change easily, which means the gaps can't easily expand---''

``And if the gaps can't expand, that means deformation is difficult. And if deformation is difficult, that means it hardens. That's the principle behind why it stiffens.''

``When you apply force slowly, the particles can deform and move while maintaining those tiny gaps, so it stays soft.''

``Unable to cope with sudden change, they freeze up---kind of like people, isn't it?''

``When you put it that way, even a starch-water solution starts to seem kind of endearing.''

``By the way, you can do other experiments with the materials I just introduced, too.''

``Iodine and starch\dots\ the only thing that comes to mind is the iodine-starch reaction.''

``Correct! When iodine slips inside starch molecules, it turns a deep blue-purple. You can verify this using the mouthwash and starch, too. But if either the iodine or the starch undergoes a change, the blue-purple won't appear. For instance, if you add vitamin C, the blue-purple vanishes in an instant and becomes transparent.''

``Conversely, if you add even a tiny amount of the amylase enzyme---found in saliva, natto, or stomach medicine---it breaks down the starch, and the blue-purple gradually fades away.''

``Exactly! With foods that use starch, like curry rice or mapo tofu, the reason they thin out as you eat them is that the amylase in your saliva keeps breaking down the starch, even in trace amounts.''

``The power of enzymes is terrifying.''

``Also, if you use hydrogen peroxide---available at pharmacies---you can restore the blue-purple color that was reduced and decolorized by vitamin C. The iodide ions get oxidized back into elemental iodine, and the iodine-starch reaction comes back to life.''

``If you control the amounts properly, you should be able to pull off the iodine clock reaction, too.''

\begin{quote}
\textit{Mikage had been right beside me the whole time, silently listening to everything. As though it were only natural for her to be present at those confidential meetings.}

\footnote{\textbf{Translator's Note:} The iodine clock reaction is a chemical reaction in which the solution shows almost no color change at first, but after a certain amount of time, a dramatic change occurs suddenly.}
\end{quote}

``You mean the reaction where the blue-purple takes a distinct time delay to return after adding hydrogen peroxide, right! The solution suddenly changes from transparent to blue-purple---you can make that look like a magic trick, too!''

``It's amazing how many fascinating experiments you can do with materials you can just pick up anywhere.''

``Everyone, please give them a try! When you experiment, be absolutely careful not to make a mess of your room or clothes, and never let the chemicals get into your eyes or mouth!''

\textit{(So, in the end, who exactly are we talking to\dots?)}


\end{document}
